Hadd for Slandering One Who Had Temporarily Prohibited Intercourse
Ruling that slander of one who had intercourse that is haram for another reason incurs hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intercourse that breaks ihsan and intercourse that does not, and state the Hanafi ruling on slandering a person who engaged in temporarily prohibited intercourse.

Core Idea
Intercourse that is haram due to an external cause does not break ihsan; slandering such a person still incurs the hadd penalty.

In Hanafi law, the hadd for slander (qadhf) only applies if the slandered person is muhsan — chaste. But what if the person had intercourse that was prohibited for a reason other than zina, like with a menstruating wife or a temporarily prohibited slave? This passage clarifies that such intercourse does not remove ihsan, and therefore the slanderer is still liable to hadd. The text also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the mukatiba (female slave under kitaba).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is slandering a person who had intercourse that is haram for another reason (not zina). The ruling is that the slanderer incurs the hadd punishment.
2
Understand the basis of the ruling
Such intercourse does not break ihsan because the prohibition is due to an external cause (e.g., menstruation, temporary ownership), not because the act is zina. Ihsan requires being free from zina, not from all prohibited intercourse.
3
Examine the scope of exceptions
The passage lists examples: intercourse with a menstruating wife, a Zoroastrian slave, a mukatiba, a married slave, and a slave purchased via a corrupt sale. In all these, ownership of the person remains, so the intercourse does not remove ihsan.
Corrupt marriage (nikah fasid) does not establish ownership, so such intercourse does break ihsan.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that intercourse with a mukatiba removes ihsan, arguing that the right to intercourse is suspended. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) disagree, stating that ownership of the person remains, so ihsan is intact.
The dispute hinges on whether the prohibition is inherent or external.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (as per al-Hidaya) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: slander of such a person incurs hadd. In practice, the slanderer receives 80 lashes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحد بقذف مَن وَطِئ حراماً لغيره كوطء عرسه حائضاً، أو وطء مملوكة حَرُمَت مؤقتةً : كأمة مجوسية ، أو مكاتبة؛ فإن حرمة الأُولى مؤقتة إلى زمان الإسلام، والثانية إلى زمان العجز. كذا في «البحر». قوله : حراماً لغيره؛ أي وطئ وطءاً هو حرام لغيره، كالوطء حالة الحيض والنفاس، فإنّه محرم لعلة الأذى، لا لفوات ملك أو نقصان فيه، ومثله وطء الزوجة التي ظاهر منها قبل أداء كفّارة الظهار، والصائمة صوم فرض. قوله كأمة مجوسيّة؛ ومثلها الأمة المزوجة والمشتراة شراء فاسداً؛ لأنّ الشراء الفاسد يوجب الملك، بخلافِ النكاح الفاسد؛ فإنّ الملك لا يثبت فيه، فلذا لا يسقط إحصانه بالوطء فيه، فلا يحدّ قاذفه، كذا في «الفتح». وعند أبي يوسف له وطء المكاتبة يسقط الإحصان. ونحن نقول: ملك الذاتِ باق، والحرمة لغيره، إذ هي مؤقتة إلى فسخ الكتابة، كذا في «الهداية».
And there is hadd for slandering one who committed intercourse that is forbidden due to something else, like intercourse with his wife while she is menstruating, or intercourse with a female slave who is temporarily prohibited: like a Zoroastrian female slave, or a mukatiba (female slave under contract); for the prohibition of the first is temporary until the time of Islam (i.e., conversion), and the second until the time of inability (to pay). Likewise in 'al-Bahr'. His statement: 'forbidden due to something else': meaning he committed intercourse that is forbidden due to something else, like intercourse during menstruation and postpartum bleeding, for it is prohibited due to the cause of harm, not due to loss of ownership or defect. Similarly, intercourse with a wife from whom he has pronounced zihar (injurious assimilation) before paying the expiation, and with a woman fasting an obligatory fast. His statement: 'like a Zoroastrian female slave'; and similar is the married female slave and the one purchased with a corrupt purchase; because a corrupt purchase establishes ownership, unlike a corrupt marriage; for ownership is not established in it, therefore his ihsan (chastity) is not removed by such intercourse, so his slanderer is not punished with hadd, as stated in 'al-Fath'. And according to Abu Yusuf, intercourse with a mukatiba does remove ihsan. But we say: ownership of the person remains, and the prohibition is due to something else, since it is temporary until the cancellation of the kitaba, as in 'al-Hidaya'.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and its rationale for slandering a person who engaged in intercourse that is temporarily prohibited due to an external cause (not zina itself). The matn states that such slander does incur hadd. The commentary explains that these acts (like intercourse with a menstruating wife, a Zoroastrian slave, or a mukatiba) are haram for reasons other than the act being zina—for example, due to harm or temporary prohibition. Because the intercourse is not zina, the person remains muhsan (chaste), and slandering a muhsan person carries the hadd punishment. The commentary then clarifies exceptions: for a Zoroastrian slave girl, ownership is established; for a married slave or one purchased via a corrupt sale, ownership also exists, so such intercourse does not break ihsan. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding a mukatiba: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that intercourse with a mukatiba does remove ihsan (making the slanderer not liable to hadd), while the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that ownership remains intact, so ihsan is not broken. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as indicated by 'we say' (wa nahnu naqulu).

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): The status of being chaste (free, adult, Muslim, and having never committed zina), which is a condition for the hadd of slander to apply.
مملوكة حرمت مؤقتة (mamlūka hurrimat mu'aqqatah): A female slave who is temporarily prohibited for intercourse due to a specific reason (e.g., being Zoroastrian, or being a mukatiba until she pays).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, does intercourse with a mukatiba (female slave under kitaba) remove the condition of ihsan for the purpose of hadd al-qadhf?
A) Yes, it removes ihsan
B) No, it does not remove ihsan
C) Only if the mukatiba has paid half the kitaba
D) It depends on whether the intercourse was consensual

Key Takeaway
Slander of a person who engaged in intercourse that is haram due to an external cause (not zina) incurs hadd, because such intercourse does not break ihsan.

Hadd for Slandering a Convert Zoroastrian and a Musta'min
Ruling that slander of a Zoroastrian who married his mother and converted, and of a musta'min who slanders a Muslim, incurs hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on slandering a Zoroastrian convert who married a mahram before conversion, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and identify the ruling on slandering a musta'min.

Core Idea
Slandering a Zoroastrian convert who married his mother incurs hadd per Abu Hanifa because the marriage was valid based on prior belief.

This passage addresses two distinct scenarios where slander (qadhf) incurs the hadd penalty, highlighting key Hanafi principles about the validity of pre-conversion acts and the scope of huquq al-ibad. It also shows an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the first case, and a clear distinction between musta'min and dhimmi for other hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases presented
The passage first discusses slandering a Zoroastrian convert who married his mother (or other mahram) before Islam, and then slandering a musta'min (non-Muslim with safe conduct). Both incur hadd under certain conditions.
The first case involves a dispute within the Hanafi school.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa's position on the Zoroastrian convert
Abu Hanifa holds that such Zoroastrian marriages are valid based on their belief (i'tiqad), so the convert becomes muhsan. Slandering him incurs hadd.
This relies on the principle that disbelievers' acts are judged by their own laws for the purpose of ihsan.
3
State the Sahibayn's opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the marriage invalid (la sihha lahu), so the convert is not muhsan, and slander does not incur hadd.
They view the marriage as inherently invalid regardless of belief.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slanderer is punished; under the Sahibayn, he is not. The matn presents the ruling as hadd being applied, indicating the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
This dispute is detailed in the Nikah section.
5
Explain the musta'min case and its reasoning
A musta'min who slanders a Muslim incurs hadd because he undertook to uphold the rights of individuals (huquq al-ibad). Hadd al-qadhf falls under individual rights, unlike zina or theft which are predominantly divine rights (huquq Allah).
Dhimmi are subject to all hudud except for wine, while musta'min are only subject to those involving individual rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كمجوسي نكح أُمه ، فأسلم، ومستأمن قذف مسلماً هنا. قوله : كمجوسي ... الخ؛ يعني قذف رجل كان مجوسياً، فنكح في حال كفرِهِ بأمه أو أخته أو غيرهما من المحارم بناءً على أن المجوس يجوّزون النكاح بالمحارم ثمّ أسلم، فيحد القاذف عنده؛ لأنّ نكاح المحارم في حالِ الكفر صحيح عنده، بناء على اعتقادهم، وعندهما : لا صحة له، فلا حد بقذفه، وقد مر تفصيل هذا الخلاف في «باب النكاح الرقيق والكافر» من «كتاب النكاح». قوله : ومستأمن ؛ أي كافر حربي دخل دارنا بأمان، وإنما يُحدّ حد القذف؛ لأنه التزم إيفاء حقوق العباد، وحدّ القذف فيه حق العبد، بخلاف حد الزنا والسرقة، فإن حق الله فيه غالب، فلا يحدّ بهما المستأمن، وأما الذمي فيحد في الكلّ إلا في الخمر. كذا في «غاية البيان». وفي «السراجية»: إذا اعتقدوا حرمة الخمر كانوا كالمسلمين، وفيها أيضاً : لو سرق الذمي أو زنى فأسلم إن ثبت بإقراره أو بشهادة المسلمين حد وإن بشهادة أهل الذمة لا. انتهى. قوله : أي حد؛ أشار به إلى أن في قوله : كمجوسي تشبيه لمن وطئ حرام لغيره به في أن قذفهما يوجب الحد.
Like a Zoroastrian who married his mother, then converted to Islam, and a musta'min (protected enemy) who slanders a Muslim here. His statement: 'like a Zoroastrian...etc.' meaning: slander of a man who was a Zoroastrian, and he married in the state of his disbelief his mother, sister, or other close relatives, based on the fact that Zoroastrians permit marriage with close relatives, then he converted to Islam, then the slanderer is punished with hadd according to him (Abu Hanifa); because marriage with close relatives in the state of disbelief is valid according to him, based on their belief; and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), there is no validity, so there is no hadd for slandering him. The detail of this dispute has already passed in 'Chapter on the marriage of slaves and disbelievers' from 'Kitab al-Nikah'. His statement: 'and a musta'min' i.e., a disbelieving combatant who entered our land under safe conduct (aman); he is punished with the hadd of slander because he undertook to fulfill the rights of men, and the hadd of slander contains the right of the subject (slave?), unlike the hadd of zina and theft, for the right of God is predominant in them, so the musta'min is not punished with them. As for the dhimmi, he is punished in all (crimes) except wine. Likewise in 'Ghayat al-Bayan'. In 'al-Sirajiyya': if they believe wine is forbidden, they are like Muslims; also in it: if a dhimmi steals or commits zina then converts to Islam, if proven by his confession or by the testimony of Muslims, he is punished; but by the testimony of the people of the dhimma, no. End. His statement: 'i.e., hadd' indicates that in his statement 'like a Zoroastrian' is a comparison of one who committed intercourse forbidden due to something else to him, in that slandering both necessitates the hadd.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage addresses two separate cases where slander incurs hadd. The first case: a Zoroastrian who, while a disbeliever, married his mother (or other mahram) in accordance with Zoroastrian beliefs, and later converted to Islam. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa considers such marriages valid based on the couple's belief, so the person becomes muhsan, and slander of him incurs hadd. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the marriage invalid, so the person is not muhsan, and slander does not incur hadd. The relied-upon position appears to be Abu Hanifa's, as the ruling is presented as a straightforward hadd case. The second case: a musta'min (enemy combatant granted safe passage into Muslim territory) who slanders a Muslim incurs hadd because he has undertaken to respect the rights of individuals (huquq al-ibad), and hadd al-qadhf falls under rights of men. This differs from hadd for zina and theft, which are predominantly rights of God and not applied to musta'min. The commentary also notes that a dhimmi is subject to all hadd punishments except for wine. An additional note from al-Sirajiyya discusses testimony and conversion.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim combatant (harbi) who enters the Muslim territory (Dar al-Islam) with a pledge of safety (aman).
مجوسي (majusi): A Zoroastrian (fire worshipper). In this context, one who practices marriage with close relatives (like mother or sister) which is permitted in Zoroastrianism but forbidden in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (Abu Hanifa), why does slander of a former Zoroastrian who married his mother before converting to Islam incur hadd?
A) Because the marriage was valid based on Zoroastrian belief, so he is muhsan
B) Because all converts are considered muhsan automatically
C) Because the slander occurred after conversion
D) Because Zoroastrians are not subject to Islamic law

Key Takeaway
Slandering a Zoroastrian convert who validly married a mahram incurs hadd per Abu Hanifa, and slandering a musta'min also incurs hadd because it violates individual rights.

intermediate
8 min
Qadhf of a Former Magian or by a Musta'min
Intra-Hanafi dispute with talil on qadhf liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on qadhf liability when the accused had a previously valid marriage under his former religion, and distinguish the application of hadd al-qadhf to a musta'min from other hudud.

Core Idea
A Magian's marriage to his mother is valid among them; after conversion, the accused is muhsan and the accuser incurs hadd.

This passage from the Book of Hudud addresses two scenarios: first, a former Magian who married his mother and then converted to Islam, and second, a musta'min (protected foreigner) who accuses a Muslim of adultery. The Hanafi school has an internal difference on whether the Magian's marriage renders the accused a muhsan for purposes of qadhf. Additionally, the passage clarifies that a musta'min is subject to hadd for qadhf because it involves a personal right, unlike hudud that are purely rights of Allah. Let's work through the reasoning behind these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios. First, a Magian who married his mother (a mahram) during his disbelief then later embraced Islam. Second, a musta'min (a harbi given safe conduct) who accuses a Muslim of adultery. Both, according to the author, incur the hadd of qadhf.
Note that the first case is introduced with 'ka-' (like), comparing it to a previous discussion on ihsan.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on the Magian case
The ruling that the accuser is liable for hadd is attributed to Abu Hanifa. He holds that the Magian's marriage to his mother, though invalid in Islam, was valid according to Magian belief. Therefore, after conversion, the accused is considered a muhsan (chaste and eligible for hadd). The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree: they consider such a marriage inherently invalid (batil) and thus the accused is not muhsan, so no hadd.
The key difference: Abu Hanifa gives legal effect to the Magian's own law; the Sahibayn do not.
3
Examine the reasoning for the musta'min case
The passage states that a musta'min is subject to hadd for qadhf because he has undertaken (by accepting aman) to fulfill the rights of people. Hadd al-qadhf involves a personal right (haqq al-'abd), unlike zina or theft where the right of Allah (haqq Allah) predominates. Thus, musta'min are not liable for those hudud.
This distinction is crucial: it's not about status but about the nature of the right violated.
4
Connect to broader fiqh principles
This case illustrates two principles: (1) Acts performed during disbelief may be given legal effect based on the individual's own religion when determining later Islamic rulings. (2) Hudud that involve personal rights are imposed on non-Muslims who have agreed to respect Muslim laws by entering aman.
Compare this with the dhimmi who is liable for all hudud except wine (if not believed prohibited).

كتاب الحدود باب حد القذف

كمجوسي نكح أُمه، فأسلم، ومستأمن قذف مسلماً هنا
وعند أبي يوسف له وطء المكاتبة يسقط الإحصان.

(كمجوسي نكح أُمه، فأسلم، ومستأمن قذف مسلماً هنا (١)): أي حد بقذف مجوسي كذا، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما، فإنَّ عنده لنكاح المحارم حكم الصحة فيما بينهم خلافاً لهما.

وقوله: ومستأمن؛ بالرفع عطفٌ على الضمير المستتر في: حد.

[١]قوله: يسقط الإحصان؛ وهو قول زفر؛ لأنّ الملك زائل في حق الوطء، ولهذا يلزمه العقرُ بالوطء، ونحن نقول: ملك الذاتِ باق، والحرمة لغيره، إذ هي مؤقتة إلى فسخ الكتابة، كذا في «الهداية». [٢]قوله: كمجوسي ...الخ؛ يعني قذف رجل كان مجوسياً، فنكح في حال كفرِهِ بأمه أو أخته أو غيرهما من المحارم بناءً على أن المجوس يجوّزون النكاح بالمحارم ثمّ أسلم، فيحد القاذف عنده؛ لأنّ نكاح المحارم في حالِ الكفر صحيح عنده، بناء على اعتقادهم، وعندهما: لا صحة له، فلا حد بقذفه، وقد مر تفصيل هذا الخلاف في «باب النكاح الرقيق والكافر» من «كتاب النكاح». [٣]قوله: ومستأمن؛ أي كافر حربي دخل دارنا بأمان، وإنما يُحدّ حد القذف؛ لأنه التزم إيفاء حقوق العباد، وحدّ القذف فيه حق العبد، بخلاف حد الزنا والسرقة، فإن حق الله فيه غالب، فلا يحدّ بهما المستأمن، وأما الذمي فيحد في الكلّ إلا في الخمر. كذا في «غاية البيان». وفي «السراجية»: إذا اعتقدوا حرمة الخمر كانوا كالمسلمين، وفيها أيضاً: لو سرق الذمي أو زنى فأسلم إن ثبت بإقراره أو بشهادة المسلمين حد وإن بشهادة أهل الذمة لا. انتهى. [٤]قوله: أي حد؛ أشار به إلى أنّ في قوله: كمجوسي تشبيه لمن وطئ حرام لغيره به في أنّ قذفهما يوجب الحد. (1) أي في دار الإسلام؛ لأن فيه حق العبد، وقد التزم إيفاء حقوق العباد. ينظر: «درر الحكام». (2) (الهداية» (5: 337).
Book of Hudud, Chapter of Hadd al-Qadhf.

Like a Magian who married his mother, then converted to Islam, and a musta'min who accused a Muslim of adultery, here.
And according to Abu Yusuf: his (the master's) intercourse with a mukatabah cancels ihsan.

(Like a Magian who married his mother, then converted to Islam, and a musta'min who accused a Muslim of adultery, here (1)): i.e. the hadd by the accusation of a Magian like this, and this is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). For according to him, marriage with mahram has the ruling of validity among them, contrary to them.

And his statement: 'and a musta'min' — in the nominative, coordinated with the implicit pronoun in 'hadd'.

[1] His statement: 'cancels ihsan' — and this is the saying of Zufar; because ownership is removed in respect of intercourse, and therefore he is obliged to pay the dower for intercourse, but we say: ownership of the person remains, and the prohibition is due to something else, for it is temporary until the cancellation of the kitabah, as in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'like a Magian...etc.' — meaning: a man who was a Magian, so he married in his state of disbelief his mother or sister or others from among the mahram, based on the fact that the Magians permit marriage with mahram, then he converted to Islam, so the accuser is subject to hadd according to him; because marriage with mahram in a state of disbelief is valid according to him, based on their belief. But according to them (the two companions): it is not valid, so there is no hadd for accusing him. The detail of this difference has passed in 'Chapter of Marriage of Slaves and Disbelievers' from 'Book of Nikah'. [3] His statement: 'and a musta'min' — i.e. a harbi disbeliever who entered our abode with aman. He is only punished with the hadd of qadhf because he undertook to fulfill the rights of people, and the hadd of qadhf contains the right of the slave, unlike the hadd of zina and theft, for the right of Allah predominates in them, so the musta'min is not punished with them. As for the dhimmi, he is punished in all except wine. As in 'Ghayat al-Bayan'. In 'al-Sirajiyyah': if they believe in the prohibition of wine, they are like Muslims. Also in it: if a dhimmi stole or committed zina then converted to Islam, if proven by his confession or by testimony of Muslims, he is punished; if by testimony of dhimmis, not. End. [4] His statement: 'i.e. hadd' — he indicated by it that in his statement 'like a Magian' there is a comparison of one who violated something forbidden due to something else with him, in that accusing them necessitates the hadd. (1) i.e. in Dar al-Islam; because it contains the right of the slave, and he has undertaken to fulfill the rights of people. Look at: 'Durar al-Hukkam'. (2) (al-Hidayah 5:337).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Hudud, Chapter of Hadd al-Qadhf, presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of hadd for accusing a person who had previously been a Magian and married his mother (a mahram) before converting to Islam. The author opens by stating the ruling: a Magian who married his mother then embraced Islam, and a musta'min (a non-Muslim from enemy territory granted safe passage) who accuses a Muslim of adultery—both cases incur the hadd of qadhf. The commentary explains that this ruling is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani). Abu Hanifa considers the marriage to mahram as valid among the Magians based on their belief, so after conversion, the marriage is considered valid and thus the accused is a muhsan (eligible for hadd). The two companions hold that such a marriage has no validity, so the accused is not a muhsan and the accuser is not liable for hadd. The passage also clarifies that a musta'min is subject to hadd for qadhf because it involves the right of a person (the accused), which the musta'min has agreed to respect, unlike zina and theft which are rights of Allah and thus not applied to musta'min. The commentary references other works like al-Hidayah and al-Sirajiyyah for further details.

Key Terms
مجوسي (majūsī): A follower of the Magian religion (Zoroastrianism), who in this context permits marriage with mahram (close relatives).
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi (enemy) non-Muslim who enters Islamic territory under a grant of safe conduct (aman).
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of adultery or fornication against a chaste person (muhsan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is a musta'min liable for hadd al-qadhf?
A) Because hadd al-qadhf is a right of Allah, which he must uphold.
B) Because he has undertaken to respect the rights of individuals by accepting aman.
C) Because all hudud apply to musta'min equally.
D) Because he is considered a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
The accuser's liability for qadhf hinges on whether the accused is muhsan, which in the case of a former Magian depends on whether the pre-conversion marriage is considered valid; musta'min are liable for qadhf but not for hudud that are purely rights of Allah.

Multiple Hadd Offenses: When One Had Suffices
Ruling on merging hadd punishments for same-type offenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on tadakhul (merging) to determine when multiple hadd offenses of the same type require only one hadd versus separate hadds.

Core Idea
Same-type hadd offenses merge; different-type hadds remain separate.

Imagine a person repeatedly accuses another of zina before being punished. Does each accusation warrant a separate hadd? The Hanafi school answers based on the type of offense. This lesson unpacks the ruling that offenses of the same kind (like repeated qadhf or multiple zina) are covered by one hadd due to overlap (tadakhul), while different types (zina, theft, drinking) each require their own hadd. We will explore the rationale of differing purposes (maqsud) and the specific exception for qadhf when a new act of zina is alleged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage classifies multiple hadd offenses as either homogeneous (same type) or heterogeneous (different types). Recognize that the ruling depends on this classification first.
Heterogeneous examples: zina + theft + qadhf. Homogeneous: repeated qadhf or multiple zina.
2
Apply the tadakhul rule
For homogeneous offenses, the Hanafi position is that one hadd suffices due to tadakhul (overlap). The rationale is that the purpose (maqsud) of the hadd for that type is achieved by a single punishment, so repeating adds no new deterrent.
The purpose of qadhf hadd is to prove the accuser's lie and remove disgrace from the accused. Once done, repetition does not require a new hadd unless a new distinct act of zina is alleged.
3
Distinguish the Shafi'i view
Contrast the Hanafi rule with Imam al-Shafi'i's view: he looks at whether the victim or the alleged act differs, not just the type. This yields different results in cases like repeated accusations of the same zina versus different zina.
The Hanafi focus on the type of offense is based on the nature of the hadd itself, not on external variables.
4
Handle the qadhf exception
Analyze the special case of qadhf: if a person accuses the same person of the same zina again after being punished, no new hadd. But if a new distinct zina is alleged, another hadd applies. This nuance arises from the purpose of the hadd being already fulfilled.
Also: if the accused is different, or if the accusation is for a different zina, the purpose is not achieved, so a new hadd is needed.
5
Apply to partial hadd scenarios
Consider cases where part of the hadd was executed then the person fled. For zina/drinking, a subsequent same offense requires a new hadd. For qadhf, if both old and new accusations are present, only the first hadd is completed; if only the new accusation is present, a new hadd is imposed and the first is nullified.
This demonstrates the practical implications of tadakhul and purpose in different hadd types.

وكفى حد الجنايات إن اتَّحد جنسها، فإن اختلف لا (وكفى حد الجنايات إن اتحد جنسها، فإن اختلف لا) : هذا عندنا. وعند الشَّافِعِي له إن اختلف المقذوف ، أو المقذوف به ، وهو الزنا [١] قوله : وكفى حد ؛ التنوين فيه للوحدة ؛ أي حد واحد؛ والحاصل : أنه إذا ارتكب رجل جنايات متعدّدة موجبة للحد، فلا يخلو: إما أن تكون مختلفة الجنس كما إذا زنى وسرق وشرب الخمر وقذف محصناً. وإما أن تكون متحدة الجنس، كما إذا قذف مراراً أو زنى. فعلى الأوّل يقام عليه حدّ كلّ من الجنايات على حدة، ولا يكتفي بحد واحد ؛ لأن المقصود من كلّ واحدٍ غير المقصود من الآخر، والزجر الحاصل بحد لا يحصل بحد آخر، فلا يمكن التداخل. وعلى الثاني يقام عليه حد واحد، كما إذا قذف مراراً، سواء اتحد المقذوف أو تعدد، وسواء كان تعدّد القذف بكلمة أم بكلمات، في يوم أم أيام. وفي «المحيط» و«التبيين»: لو ضرب للزنى أو للشرب بعض الحد فهرب ثم زنى أو شرب ثانياً حد حداً مستأنفاً، ولو كان ذلك في القذف، فإن حضر الأول والثاني جميعاً أو الأوّل كمل الأول، ولا شيء للثاني للتداخل، وإن حضر الثاني وحده يحدّ حداً مستأنفاً للثاني، ويبطل الأول؛ لعدم دعواه. انتهى. وفي «التبيين» أيضاً: «قذفه فحدّ ثم قذفه لم يحد ثانياً؛ لأن المقصود وهو إظهار كذبه ورفع العار بالأوّل حصل». انتهى. وفي «الفتح»: «لو قذف شخصا فحدّ به، ثم قذفه بعين ذلك الزني، بأن قال: أنا باق على نسبتي إليه الزنى الذي نسبته إليه، لا يُحَدُّ ثانياً، أما لو قذفه بزنى آخر حدّ به». انتهى
And one hadd suffices for offenses if their type is the same; if they differ, not. (And one hadd suffices for offenses if their type is the same; if they differ, not.) This is according to us (Hanafis). And according to al-Shafi'i, it (multiple hadds) applies if the accused differs, or the object of accusation, which is zina. [1] His saying: 'And one hadd suffices' — the tanween in it indicates oneness; i.e. one hadd. The summary: if a man commits multiple offenses that necessitate hadd, it is either that they are of different types, such as when he commits zina, theft, drinks wine, and accuses a muhsan; or they are of the same type, such as when he accuses repeatedly or commits zina multiple times. In the first case, each offense is punished with its own hadd, and one hadd is not sufficient; because the purpose of each is different from the purpose of the other, and the deterrence obtained by one hadd cannot be obtained by another hadd, so overlap (tadakhul) is not possible. In the second case, one hadd is applied, such as when he accuses repeatedly, whether the accused is the same or multiple, and whether the repetition of accusation is with one word or multiple words, in one day or multiple days. In al-Muhit and al-Tabyin: if he was flogged for zina or drinking part of the hadd then fled, then he commits zina or drinks again, he is punished with a new hadd. If that was in qadhf, then if both the first and second (accusations) are present (for punishment) or the first (only), the first is completed and nothing for the second due to overlap (tadakhul). If only the second is present, he is punished with a new hadd for the second, and the first is nullified due to lack of claim. End. In al-Tabyin also: 'If he accused someone and was punished with hadd, then accused him again, he is not punished a second time; because the purpose, which is to show his lie and remove the disgrace, was achieved by the first.' End. In al-Fath: 'If he accused a person and was punished with hadd, then accused him of that same zina, by saying: I am still attributing to him the zina that I attributed before, he is not punished a second time. But if he accused him of another zina, he is punished.' End.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the ruling 'And one hadd suffices for offenses if their type is the same; if they differ, not' explains the Hanafi position on multiple hadd offenses. The author clarifies that the ruling applies when a person commits several offenses that incur hadd. If the offenses are of different types (e.g., zina, theft, drinking wine, qadhf), each must be punished separately because the purpose (maqsud) of each hadd differs—deterrence for one act does not cover another. If the offenses are of the same type (e.g., repeated qadhf or repeated zina), one hadd suffices due to overlap (tadakhul), regardless of whether the victim is the same or different, or whether the repetition was with one word or many, in one day or over days. The passage then cites al-Muhit and al-Tabyin for a scenario: if a person has received part of the hadd for zina or drinking and then flees, then commits the same offense again, a new hadd is imposed. But for qadhf, if both the first and second accusations are present for punishment, only the first hadd is completed and the second is not imposed; if only the second is present, a new hadd is imposed for the second. The rationale is that the purpose of the hadd for qadhf is to prove the accuser's lie and remove disgrace—once achieved, repetition does not require another hadd unless a new distinct act of zina is alleged. This is contrasted with al-Shafi'i's view, who distinguishes based on whether the accused or the act of zina is different. The Hanafi view focuses on the type of offense and the purpose of the hadd.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadākhul): Overlap or merging of hadd punishments; when multiple offenses of the same type are subsumed under one hadd.
جنايات (jināyāt): Crimes or offenses, specifically those that necessitate a hadd punishment (e.g., zina, theft, qadhf, drinking wine).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person commits zina with two different partners in two separate incidents before being caught, how many hadd punishments apply?
A) Two hadds, one for each act of zina.
B) One hadd only, because both are zina (same type).
C) Three hadds, because there are two partners and one act?
D) No hadd because the partner changed.

Key Takeaway
Same-type hadd offenses merge into one hadd due to the unity of purpose; heterogeneous offenses each require their own hadd.

Tadakhul of Hadd Penalties for Multiple Crimes
Explaining Hanafi and Shafi'i views on merger of hadd penalties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on tadakhul (merger) of hadd penalties and distinguish it from the Shafi'i rule for qadhf, citing the underlying basis in the nature of the right (haqq Allah vs haqq al-'abd).

Core Idea
Same-type hadd crimes merge into one penalty; different-type crimes never merge.

When a person commits multiple crimes that each carry a hadd penalty, do all penalties apply or do they merge? The answer depends on the type of crime and the madhhab. In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi position that same-type crimes (e.g., multiple slanders or multiple acts of zina) are punished with a single hadd, while different-type crimes (e.g., zina and theft) each receive their own penalty. We contrast this with the Shafi'i view that slander (qadhf) does not merge because it is considered a personal right.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core Hanafi rule: for crimes of the same type, one hadd suffices; for different types, each receives its own hadd.
The matn reads: 'And one hadd suffices for crimes if their type is same, but if different, not.'
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Determine which school disagrees and on what specific crime. Here, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that for slander (qadhf), penalties do not merge because it is a right of the individual.
Shafi'i view: each slander against a different person or with a different zina requires a separate hadd.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i rationale: slander primarily affects the victim's honor, making it a right of the slave (haqq al-'abd). Hence, each instance violates a distinct right, so separate penalties are due.
Shafi'is also hold that if the slander is repeated with the same accusation, it is a mere repetition and does not trigger a new hadd; only ta'zir is given.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi counter-argument: slander is predominantly a right of Allah (haqq Allah), so the goal of deterrence (zajr) is achieved by a single penalty. The purpose of showing the slanderer's lie and removing disgrace is accomplished by one hadd.
The text cites from al-Tabyin: 'If he slandered him and was flogged, then slandered him again, he is not flogged again because the purpose was achieved by the first.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Hanafis impose one hadd for multiple slanders (whether same or different victims, same or different zina), while Shafi'is impose multiple hadds (except for mere repetition of the same accusation).
Other same-type crimes like zina also merge according to Hanafis, though there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on partial flogging before escape.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكفى حد الجنايات إن اتَّحد جنسها، فإن اختلف لا : هذا عندنا. وعند الشَّافِعِي له إن اختلف المقذوف ، أو المقذوف به ، وهو الزنا. قوله : وكفى حد ؛ التنوين فيه للوحدة؛ أي حد واحد، والحاصل : أنه إذا ارتكب رجل جنايات متعدّدة موجبة للحد، فلا يخلو: إما أن تكون مختلفة الجنس كما إذا زنى وسرق وشرب الخمر وقذف محصناً. وإما أن تكون متحدة الجنس، كما إذا قذف مراراً أو زنى. فعلى الأوّل يقام عليه حدّ كلّ من الجنايات على حدة، ولا يكتفي بحد واحد ؛ لأن المقصود من كلّ واحدٍ غير المقصود من الآخر، والزجر الحاصل بحد لا يحصل بحد آخر، فلا يمكن التداخل. وعلى الثاني يقام عليه حد واحد ، كما إذا قذف مراراً، سواء اتحد المقذوف أو تعدد، وسواء كان تعدّد القذف بكلمة أم بكلمات، في يوم أم أيام. وفي «المحيط» و«التبيين»: لو ضرب للزنى أو للشرب بعض الحد فهرب ثم زنى أو شرب ثانياً حد حداً مستأنفاً، ولو كان ذلك في القذف، فإن حضر الأول والثاني جميعاً أو الأوّل كمل الأول، ولا شيء للثاني للتداخل، وإن حضر الثاني وحده يحدّ حداً مستأنفاً للثاني، ويبطل الأول؛ لعدم دعواه. انتهى. وفي «التبيين» أيضاً: «قذفه فحدّ ثم قذفه لم يحد ثانياً؛ لأن المقصود وهو إظهار كذبه ورفع العار بالأوّل حصل». انتهى وفي «الفتح»: «لو قذف شخصا فحدّ به، ثم قذفه بعين ذلك الزني، بأن قال: أنا باق على نسبتي إليه الزنى الذي نسبته إليه، لا يُحَدُّ ثانياً، أما لو قذفه بزنى آخر حدّ به». انتهى. (في كتب الشافعية: لا يتكرر الحد بتكرر القذف ولو صرح فيه بزنا آخر، أو قصد به الاستئناف، أو غاير بين الألفاظ لاتحاد المقذوف، وإن قذفه فحد ثم قذفه ثانيا بذلك الزنا عزر.) كما إذا قذف زيداً وعمراً، أو قذف زيداً بزنا ثم بزنا آخر لا يتداخل، أما إذا قذف زيداً بزنا واحد، وكرّر هذا القذفَ يتداخل، وهذا بناء على أنَّ حق العبدِ فيه غالب عنده. وأما عندنا لما كان حق الله تعالى غالباً يتداخل إذ المقصود الانزجار، أما إذا اختلفت الجنايات، فالمقصود من كلِّ واحد غير مقصود من الآخر، فلا يتداخل. قوله : لا يتداخل؛ وعندنا: يتداخل في الصورتين، كما يقتضيه إطلاق المتن، وقد مَرَّ الخلاف بين مشايخنا أيضاً في الصورة الثانية. قوله : وهذا بناء... الخ؛ يعني الخلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي مبني على أنّ حق العبدِ غالب في حد القذف عنده، وعندنا حق الله غالب وقد مر تفصيله. قوله : غير المقصود من الآخر؛ قال في «الكفاية»: فحد الزنا لصيانة الأنساب، وحد السرقة لصيانة الأموال، وحد الشرب لصيانة العقول، وحد القذف لصيانة الأعراض فلا تتداخل.
And one hadd suffices for crimes if their type is same, but if different, not: this is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i, he has [a hadd] if the person slandered or the object of slander (i.e., zina) differs. His statement: 'And one hadd suffices' — the tanwin indicates singularity, i.e., one single hadd. The summary: if a man commits multiple crimes warranting hadd, it is either: they are of different types, as if he committed zina, theft, drank wine, and slandered a muhsan; or they are of the same type, as if he slandered repeatedly or committed zina. In the first case, each crime's hadd is carried out separately, and one hadd is not sufficient, because the purpose of each is different from the purpose of the other, and the deterrence achieved by one hadd is not achieved by another, so merging is impossible. In the second case, one hadd is carried out against him, as if he slandered repeatedly, whether the slandered person is the same or different, and whether the repetition was with one word or many words, in one day or several days. In al-Muhit and al-Tabyin: 'If he was flogged for zina or for drinking some of the hadd then fled, then committed zina or drank again, he is flogged a fresh hadd. And if that was in slander, then if both the first and second [victims] are present or only the first, the first is completed, and nothing is due for the second due to merging; if only the second is present, he is flogged a fresh hadd for the second, and the first is nullified due to lack of claim.' End quote. In al-Tabyin also: 'If he slandered him and was flogged, then slandered him again, he is not flogged again because the purpose – which is showing his lie and removing the disgrace – was achieved by the first.' End quote. In al-Fath: 'If he slandered a person and was flogged for it, then slandered him with that same zina – i.e., he said: I still attribute to him the zina which I attributed to him – he is not flogged again. But if he slandered him with another zina, he is flogged for it.' End quote. (In Shafi'i books: The hadd is not repeated by repetition of slander even if he explicitly mentions another zina or intends renewal or changes the wording because the slandered person is one; if he slandered him and was flogged then slandered him again with that zina, he is given a ta'zir.) As if he slandered Zayd and 'Amr, or slandered Zayd with zina then with another zina – they do not merge. But if he slandered Zayd with one zina and repeated this slander, they merge. This is based on the fact that the right of the slave is predominant in it according to him. And according to us, since the right of Allah is predominant, they merge because the purpose is deterrence. But if the crimes differ, the purpose of each is different from the purpose of the other, so they do not merge. His statement: 'they do not merge' – and according to us they merge in both cases, as the absolute wording of the matn implies, and the dispute among our scholars also occurred in the second case. His statement: 'This is based on...' – meaning the difference between us and al-Shafi'i is based on that the right of the slave is predominant in the hadd of slander according to him, and according to us the right of Allah is predominant, and its detail has passed. His statement: 'different from the purpose of the other' – al-Kifaya said: The hadd of zina is for protecting lineages, the hadd of theft for protecting wealth, the hadd of drinking for protecting intellects, and the hadd of slander for protecting honors, so they do not merge.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute on whether multiple hadd penalties can merge (tadakhul) when a person commits multiple crimes. The passage is a sharh (expansion) of the base matn: 'And one hadd suffices for crimes of the same type, but if different, not.' The commentator explains that this is the Hanafi position. For crimes of different types (e.g., zina, theft, drinking, slander), each hadd is separate because the purposes differ. For same-type crimes (e.g., multiple slanders, multiple zina), only one hadd is imposed because the goal is deterrence (zajr), and deterrence is achieved once. The passage then contrasts with the Shafi'i view: al-Shafi'i does not allow merging for slander (qadhf) because he considers it a right of the individual (haqq al-'abd), thus repeating the slander against the same person requires a new hadd, unless it is a mere repetition of the same accusation. The Hanafi view, however, treats slander as predominantly a right of Allah (haqq Allah), so merging applies. The commentator also cites sub-rulings: if someone receives partial flogging for zina/drinking then commits the crime again, a fresh hadd is imposed; for slander, if the first victim is present, the first hadd is completed and the second lapses; if only the second victim is present, a new hadd is given. The passage clarifies that the basis of the dispute is the nature of the right – whether it is predominantly Allah's right or the individual's right.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Merger of penalties: when multiple crimes of the same type are punished by a single hadd penalty.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed penalty (80 lashes) for falsely accusing a chaste person of zina without four witnesses.
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): A right belonging to an individual; in the context of hadd, it means the punishment can be waived or affected by the victim's action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person slanders the same person multiple times with the same accusation of zina, how many hadd penalties are imposed?
A) One hadd for each slander.
B) One hadd only, because the penalties merge.
C) No hadd; only ta'zir.
D) Two hadds: one for the first and one for the last.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, multiple same-type hadd crimes result in one penalty; different-type crimes each have their own penalty.

Tadahul in Hadd al-Qadhf: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Expands on tadahul rule with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on tadahul in hadd al-qadhf and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument based on the dominance of Allah's right versus the servant's right.

Core Idea
Hadd punishments overlap when the right of Allah (deterrence) dominates, but not when the right of the servant prevails.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah clarifies a key disagreement between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether multiple hadd punishments for qadhf (false accusation of zina) can overlap (tadahul). The ruling depends on which legal right is considered dominant: the right of Allah (public deterrence) or the right of the servant (vindication of the individual). Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the correct punishment in cases of repeated or multiple false accusations.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafi school, if a person accuses the same individual of zina repeatedly, the multiple hadd punishments overlap into a single hadd. Similarly, in some Hanafi views, if a person accuses two different individuals or accuses one person of two different zina acts, the punishments may overlap depending on the circumstances.
The passage notes an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether accusation of one person with two different zina acts leads to tadahul.
2
Identify the opposing school and its position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the right of the servant (the accused) is dominant in hadd al-qadhf, because the punishment vindicates the honor of the individual. Therefore, each separate accusation requires its own hadd punishment; there is no tadahul.
For Shafi'is, the servant's right is not satisfied by a single punishment when multiple accusations are made.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi proof is based on the purpose (maqsad) of the hadd: deterrence (zajr). Since the goal is to deter people from making false accusations, a single punishment suffices to achieve that purpose when the same victim is repeatedly accused of the same act. The right of Allah (public interest) is dominant, and thus punishments overlap.
The commentary also cites al-Kifaya to explain that different types of hadd (zina, theft, drinking, qadhf) serve different purposes and therefore do not overlap with each other.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The Hanafi response is not explicitly detailed in this passage, but it follows from the principle that when the right of Allah is deemed dominant, the legislator prioritizes general deterrence over individual vindication. The disagreement thus reduces to a fundamental usul al-fiqh issue: categorization of the hadd as predominantly a right of Allah or a right of the servant.
The matn's absolute wording (إطلاق المتن) is also cited as evidence for tadahul in both scenarios according to some Hanafis.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference is concrete: In a Hanafi court, if a person accuses Zayd of zina multiple times, or accuses both Zayd and Amr of zina, the offender receives only one hadd punishment (80 lashes). In a Shafi'i court, each separate accusation would trigger a separate hadd punishment, potentially resulting in multiple sentences of 80 lashes each.
This distinction has significant implications for judicial sentencing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود باب حد القذف
كما إذا قذف زيداً وعمراً، أو قذف زيداً بزنا ثم بزنا آخر لا يتداخل، أما إذا قذف زيداً بزنا واحد، وكرّر هذا القذفَ يتداخل، وهذا بناء على أنَّ حق العبد فيه غالب عنده. وأما عندنا لما كان حق الله تعالى غالباً يتداخل إذ المقصود الانزجار، أما إذا اختلفت الجنايات، فالمقصود من كلِّ واحد غير مقصود من الآخر، فلا يتداخل [١]قوله : لا يتداخل؛ وعندنا : يتداخل في الصورتين، كما يقتضيه إطلاق المتن، وقد مَرَّ الخلاف بين مشايخنا أيضاً في الصورة الثانية. [٢] قوله : وهذا بناء... الخ ؛ يعني الخلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي مبني على أنّ حق العبدِ غالب في حد القذف عنده، وعندنا حق الله غالب وقد مر تفصيله. [٣] قوله : غير المقصود من الآخر؛ قال في «الكفاية»: فحد الزنا لصيانة الأنساب، وحد السرقة لصيانة الأموال، وحد الشرب لصيانة العقول، وحد القذف لصيانة الأعراض فلا تتداخل.
Book of Hadd, Chapter of Hadd al-Qadhf: If he accuses Zayd and Amr of zina, or accuses Zayd of zina and then another zina, they do not overlap. But if he accuses Zayd of one zina and repeats this accusation, they overlap. And this is based on the fact that the right of the servant is dominant according to him (al-Shafi'i). According to us (Hanafis), since the right of Allah is dominant, they overlap because the purpose is deterrence. But if the offenses differ, the purpose of each is different from the other, so they do not overlap. [1] He said: 'they do not overlap'; and according to us, they overlap in both situations, as indicated by the absolute wording of the matn, and the disagreement among our mashayikh has already passed in the second situation. [2] He said: 'And this is based... etc.' means the disagreement between us and al-Shafi'i is based on that the right of the servant is dominant in hadd al-qadhf according to him, while according to us the right of Allah is dominant, and its detail has already passed. [3] He said: 'different from the purpose of the other'; he said in al-Kifaya: 'the hadd of zina is for preserving lineages, the hadd of theft is for preserving wealth, the hadd of drinking is for preserving intellects, and the hadd of qadhf is for preserving honors, so they do not overlap.'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the tadahul (overlap) of multiple hadd punishments for qadhf (false accusation of zina). The author presents the ruling as stated in the matn: if a person accuses two different individuals (Zayd and Amr) of zina, or accuses one person of two separate acts of zina, the punishments do not overlap. However, if he accuses the same person of the same zina repeatedly, the punishments do overlap. The reasoning, as clarified in the commentary, is based on whether the right of Allah or the right of the servant is dominant. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, the right of the servant (the accused's right to vindication) is dominant, so each separate accusation requires a separate hadd, thus no tadahul. According to the Hanafi school, the right of Allah (deterrence) is dominant, so punishments overlap when the purpose is the same. The commentary also notes an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the second scenario (accusing one person of two different zina acts). The text then quotes al-Kifaya, stating that different hadd punishments (zina, theft, drinking, qadhf) serve different purposes (preserving lineages, wealth, intellects, honors), hence they do not overlap.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina (adultery/fornication) against a chaste person.
تداخل (tadahul): The overlapping or merging of multiple punishments into one when the same or similar offenses are committed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental basis for the disagreement between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the overlapping of hadd punishments for qadhf?
A) The definition of zina
B) Whether the right of Allah or the right of the servant is dominant
C) The number of witnesses required
D) Whether the accused is Muslim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hadd al-qadhf punishments overlap (tadahul) when the purpose is deterrence (right of Allah), but not when offenses differ in purpose or victim.

Definition and Distinctions of Ta'zir Punishment
Defines ta'zir and lists ten differences from hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'zir linguistically and legally, and state the ten distinctions between ta'zir and hadd.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment obligatory for major sins without fixed hadd, distinct from hadd in ten key ways.

This passage introduces ta'zir as a type of punishment that is not fixed by Shari'ah but left to the judge's discretion. After defining ta'zir, the author lists ten important differences between ta'zir and hadd punishments. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying ta'zir in cases where hadd does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the linguistic and legal definitions of ta'zir. Note that linguistically it means general discipline, and legally it is a punishment for a prohibited act.
The legal definition specifies it is a 'disciplinary punishment for an act prohibited by Shari'ah'.
2
Understand the ten differences
Go through each of the ten distinctions between ta'zir and hadd. Understand the key contrast in each: fixedness, aversion by doubt, applicability to minors, to dhimmis, who executes, effect of retraction, detention, intercession, dropping by time, and waiver by Imam.
These differences are compiled from 'Nisab al-Ihtisab' and 'al-Tatarkhaniyya'.
3
Note the consensus on obligation
From al-Bahr: there is ijma' that ta'zir is wajib for major sins that do not have a fixed hadd and are proven before the judge.
The phrase 'أجمعت الأمة' indicates consensus.
4
Learn the examples
Review the examples from al-Sirajiyya (intercourse with shubha incurs ta'zir) and al-Dhakhira (an act that would require hadd but is prevented by a barrier leads to ta'zir).
These show the scope of ta'zir beyond the core definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التعزير
[١] قوله: فصل التعزير؛ لما ذكر العقوبات المقدّرة شرع في غير المقدّرة. والتعزير لغة: التأديب مطلقاً بضرب وغيره دون الحد أو أكثر منه. وشرعاً: هو تأديب جزاء الفعل المحظور شرعاً.
والفرق بين التعزير والحد على ما في «نصاب الاحتساب» و«التاتارخانية» وغيرهما بوجوه:
أحدها: أن الحد مقدر شرعاً، والتعزير مفوض إلى رأي الإمام.
وثانيها: أن الحد يندرأ بالشبهة، والتعزير يجب معها.
وثالثها: أن الحد لا يجب على الصبي، والتعزير شرع عليه.
والرابع: أن الحد يوجد في الذمي، ويطلق الحد عليه، وتأديبه لا يسمى تعزيراً بل...
والخامس: أن الحد مختص بالإمام والتعزير يقيمه الزوج والمولى وكل من رأى أحداً يباشر المعصية.
والسادس: أن الرجوع يعمل في الحد لا في التعزير.
والسابع: أنه يحبس المشهود عليه حتى يسأل عن الشهود في الحد لا في التعزير.
والثامن: أن الحد لا تجوز الشفاعة فيه بخلاف التعزير.
والتاسع: أن الحد يسقط بالتقادم دون التعزير.
والعاشر: أن الحد لا يجوز للإمام تركه بخلاف التعزير.
وفي «البحر»: «أجمعت الأمة على وجوب التعزير في كبيرة لا توجب الحد، وكل من ارتكب معصية ليس فيها حد مقدر، وثبت عليه عند الحاكم، فإنه يجب فيه التعزير». انتهى.
وفي «السراجية»: «من وطئ بشبهة عزر». انتهى.
وفي «الذخيرة»: «إن كان الفعل من جنس يجب به الحد ولم يجب لمانع...»
(۱) من «البحر الرائق (٥: ٤٧).
Chapter on Ta'zir
[1] He said: 'Chapter on Ta'zir': When he mentioned the fixed punishments, he proceeded to the non-fixed ones. Ta'zir linguistically: absolute discipline with beating and other means, less than hadd or more. Legally: it is a disciplinary punishment for an act prohibited by Shari'ah.
The difference between ta'zir and hadd according to what is in 'Nisab al-Ihtisab', 'al-Tatarkhaniyya', and others is in several ways:
First: Hadd is fixed by Shari'ah, while ta'zir is delegated to the opinion of the Imam.
Second: Hadd is averted by doubt (shubha), while ta'zir is obligatory even with doubt.
Third: Hadd is not obligatory on a minor, while ta'zir is prescribed for him.
Fourth: Hadd applies to a dhimmī, and the term 'hadd' is used for him, but his discipline is not called ta'zir; rather...
Fifth: Hadd is specific to the Imam, while ta'zir may be carried out by the husband, the master, and anyone who sees someone committing a sin.
Sixth: Retraction (rujūʿ) operates in hadd but not in ta'zir.
Seventh: The accused is imprisoned until inquiry about witnesses in hadd, but not in ta'zir.
Eighth: Intercession is not allowed in hadd, unlike ta'zir.
Ninth: Hadd is dropped by passage of time (taqādam), unlike ta'zir.
Tenth: The Imam is not permitted to abandon hadd, unlike ta'zir.
And in 'al-Bahr': 'The ummah has unanimously agreed on the obligation of ta'zir for a major sin that does not impose a hadd, and whoever commits a sin for which there is no fixed hadd and it is established before the judge, then ta'zir is obligatory for him.' End.
And in 'al-Sirajiyya': 'Whoever has intercourse based on a shubha (doubtful marriage) is to be ta'zir-ed.' End.
And in 'al-Dhakhira': 'If the act is of a type which would require hadd but it is not required due to a barrier...'
(1) From 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (5:47).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is an introductory ruling on ta'zir (discretionary punishment). It begins by defining ta'zir linguistically and legally, then enumerates ten important differences between ta'zir and hadd (fixed punishments) as outlined in authoritative Hanafi works like 'Nisab al-Ihtisab' and 'al-Tatarkhaniyya'. The ten distinctions cover: (1) fixed vs. discretionary nature, (2) aversion by doubt, (3) applicability to minors, (4) application to dhimmis, (5) who executes the punishment, (6) effect of retraction, (7) detention of the accused, (8) permissibility of intercession, (9) dropping by time, and (10) the Imam's authority to waive. Following this, the text quotes three important rulings: from 'al-Bahr' stating that there is consensus (ijma') on the obligation of ta'zir for major sins without fixed hadd, from 'al-Sirajiyya' that intercourse with shubha incurs ta'zir, and from 'al-Dhakhira' that an act which would normally require hadd but is prevented by a barrier leads to ta'zir. These quotations reinforce the scope of ta'zir as a necessary punishment for offenses not covered by the fixed hadd penalties.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Linguistically: general discipline including beating and other means, less than hadd or more. Legally: a disciplinary punishment for an act prohibited by Shari'ah.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for specific crimes, such as zina, qadhf, theft, or drinking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a difference between hadd and ta'zir according to the passage?
A) Hadd is fixed by Shari'ah; ta'zir is delegated to the Imam's opinion.
B) Hadd is averted by doubt; ta'zir is obligatory even with doubt.
C) Hadd applies to minors; ta'zir does not apply to minors.
D) Hadd is exclusive to the Imam; ta'zir may be carried out by the husband, master, or anyone.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is an obligatory discretionary punishment for major sins without fixed hadd, with ten distinct rules that separate it from hadd.

Definition of Ta'zir and Its Ten Differences from Hadd
Defines ta'zir and lists ten differences from hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'zir linguistically and legally, and list the ten key differences between ta'zir and hadd according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment, distinct from fixed hadd, applicable even when doubts exist, and enforceable by multiple authorities.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya, the author defines ta'zir—a discretionary punishment for prohibited acts without fixed penalties—and contrasts it with hadd, the fixed divine punishment. The ten differences outlined are essential for understanding the Hanafi classification of penal law. This lesson will walk you through the definition and each difference, clarifying the scope of judicial discretion versus fixed rules.

Guided Path
1
Define ta'zir linguistically and legally
Identify the two definitions: linguistically, ta'zir means absolute discipline (by beating or otherwise) that can be less or more than hadd; legally, it is a disciplinary punishment for a legally prohibited act.
The linguistic definition allows ta'zir to exceed hadd in severity, contrary to some other schools.
2
Note the first difference: fixed vs discretionary
Hadd is fixed by law (shar'an) and cannot be altered; ta'zir is left to the judge's discretion (ra'y al-imam).
This is the foundational distinction upon which many others build.
3
Examine the effect of doubts and age
Hadd is warded off by doubts (yudra'u bil-shubha) and is not obligatory on a minor; ta'zir is obligatory despite doubts and is prescribed for minors.
This reflects the principle that hadd is stricter in application due to its fixed nature.
4
Identify who can enforce each punishment
Hadd is exclusive to the ruler (imam); ta'zir may be carried out by the husband, master, or any person who sees someone committing a sin.
This decentralization of ta'zir allows for immediate discipline in domestic or supervisory contexts.
5
Review rules of retraction, intercession, and lapse of time
Retraction (ruju') works in hadd (e.g., a thief can retract confession) but not in ta'zir; intercession (shafa'a) is not allowed in hadd but is in ta'zir; lapse of time (taqadum) drops hadd but not ta'zir.
These procedural differences emphasize the flexibility of ta'zir compared to the rigidity of hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التعزير
قوله ; فصل التعزير ; لَمّا ذكر العقوبات المقدّرة شرع في غير المقدّرة، والتعزير لغة : التأديب مطلقاً بضرب وغيره دون الحد أو أكثر منه، وشرعاً : هو تأديب جزاء الفعل هو : محظور شرعاً.
والفرق بين التعزير والحدّ على ما في «نصاب الاحتساب» و«التاتارخانية» وغيرهما بوجوه :
أحدها : إنّ الحد مقدّر شرعاً ، والتعزير مفوّض إلى رأي الإمام. عقوبة.
وثانيها : إنّ الحدّ يندرأ بالشبهة، والتعزير يجب معها.
وثالثها: إنّ الحدّ لا يجب على الصبي، والتعزير شرع عليه.
والرابع : إن الحد يوجد في الذمي، ويطلق الحدّ عليه، وتأديبه لا يسمى تعزيراً بل
والخامس : إنّ الحدّ مختص بالإمام والتعزير يقيمه الزوج والمولى وكلّ مَن رأى أحداً يباشر المعصية.
والسادس : إن الرجوع يعمل في الحد لا في التعزير.
والسابع : إنّه يحبس المشهود عليه حتى يسأل عن الشهود في الحد لا في التعزير.
والثامن : إنّ الحدّ لا تجوز الشفاعة فيه بخلاف التعزير.
والتاسع : إن الحد يسقط بالتقادم دون التعزير.
والعاشر: إنّ الحدّ لا يجوز للإمام تركه بخلاف التعزير.
Chapter on Ta'zir. He said: 'Chapter on Ta'zir.' Since he mentioned the fixed punishments, he then began [discussing] the non-fixed ones. Ta'zir linguistically: discipline absolutely, by beating or otherwise, less than hadd or more than it. Legally: it is disciplinary punishment for an action that is prohibited by law. The difference between ta'zir and hadd according to what is in 'Nisab al-Ihtisab' and 'al-Tatarkhaniyya' and others is in several ways: First: Hadd is fixed by law, ta'zir is left to the judge's discretion. Second: Hadd is warded off by doubts, ta'zir is obligatory despite them. Third: Hadd is not obligatory on a minor, ta'zir is prescribed for him. Fourth: Hadd applies to the dhimmi, and the term hadd is used for him, but his discipline is not called ta'zir but... Fifth: Hadd is exclusive to the ruler, ta'zir may be carried out by the husband, master, and anyone who sees someone committing a sin. Sixth: Retraction is effective in hadd, not in ta'zir. Seventh: The accused is imprisoned until inquiry about witnesses in hadd, not in ta'zir. Eighth: Intercession is not allowed in hadd, unlike ta'zir. Ninth: Hadd is dropped by lapse of time, ta'zir is not. Tenth: The ruler may not leave hadd, unlike ta'zir.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction section from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. The author first defines ta'zir linguistically as absolute discipline, which may be less or more than hadd (fixed punishment). Legally, it is a punishment for a forbidden act that does not have a fixed hadd. He then lists ten differences between ta'zir and hadd, drawing from Hanafi sources like Nisab al-Ihtisab and al-Tatarkhaniyya. These differences highlight that hadd is fixed and inflexible, while ta'zir is discretionary and can be administered by various authorities. Note that the fourth difference is cut off in the text, but it likely states that the dhimmi's punishment is called hadd, not ta'zir. The tenth difference confirms that the ruler cannot forgo hadd, unlike ta'zir. This list is foundational for understanding the Hanafi classification of punishments.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for prohibited acts without fixed hadd; linguistically absolute discipline.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed, divinely prescribed punishment for specific crimes (e.g., theft, zina).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a difference between hadd and ta'zir?
A) Hadd is discretionary, ta'zir is fixed
B) Ta'zir is warded off by doubts, hadd is not
C) Hadd is exclusive to the ruler, ta'zir may be carried out by others
D) Ta'zir is dropped by lapse of time, hadd is not

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is a flexible, discretionary punishment that differs from hadd in ten key ways, including its applicability despite doubts, to minors, and by non-ruler authorities.

The Obligation of Ta'zir: Consensus and Types
Quotes rulings on ta'zir obligation and lists types of punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'zir, identify its wajib status based on ijma, and list its various forms including those that exceed hadd.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is wajib for every prohibited act without a fixed hadd, and can be severe—even execution.

This passage establishes the obligatory nature of ta'zir through consensus (ijma) and illustrates its wide scope. You will see ta'zir applied to various offenses, from shubha intercourse to repeat theft, and learn that its severity can surpass ordinary hadd punishments when maslaha demands.

Guided Path
1
Locate the consensus proof
Find the quote from 'al-Bahr' that says 'ajma'at al-ummah 'ala wujub al-ta'zir'—this is the ijma. Understand that in Hanafi usul, ijma is a binding source.
2
Identify the ruling and its condition
The ruling is that ta'zir is wajib. The condition is that the act is a major sin (kabira) or any prohibited act without a fixed hadd, and it is proven before the judge.
3
Examine the types of ta'zir listed
The passage lists many forms: slapping, frowning, imprisonment, exile, beating, execution, property confiscation, even burning houses. Note the severity of some.
4
Distinguish ta'zir from ta'dib
From 'Nisab al-Ihtisab': ta'zir is wajib like hadd, while ta'dib is merely permissible (mubah). This underscores the obligatory nature of ta'zir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي «البحر»: «أجمعت الأمة على وجوب التعزير في كبيرة لا توجب الحد، وكلّ من ارتكب معصية ليس فيها حدّ مقدر ، وثبت عليه عند الحاكم، فإنه يجب فيه التعزير». انتهى.
وفي «السراجية»: «مَن وطئ بشبهة عُزّر». انتهى.
وفي «الذخيرة»: «إن كان الفعل من جنس ما يجب به الحد ولم يجب لمانع وهو تأديب دون الحدّ ، وأصله من العزر، بمعنى الرَّد والردع وعارض يبلغ التعزير أقصى غاياته، وإن كان من جنس ما لا يجب فيه الحد لا يبلغ أقصى غاياته، ولكنّه مفوّض إلى رأي الإمام». انتهى.
وفي «نصاب الاحتساب»: «التعزير واجب كالحد ؛ لأنه جزاء فعل هو محظور، فيكون واجباً بخلاف التأديب ؛ لأنه غير واجب بل مباح». انتهى.
قوله : تأديب دون الحد؛ اعلم أن التعزير على أنواع على ما فصلتها في رسالتي «القول الجازم في سقوط الحد بنكاح المحارم»، فقد يكون بالصفع على العنق ، وبفرك الأذن، وبنظر القاضي له بوجه عبوس، وبشتم غير القذف، وبالحبس، وبالنفي، وبالقتل، وبالضرب وبغير ذلك، فإن اقتضى رأي القاضي الضرب في واقعة ، فحينئذ ينبغي له أن ينقصه من مقدار أدنى الحدود، وهو أربعون سوطاً لضارب الخمر، كذا حققه في «فتح القدير».
وذكر جمع من المشايخ التعزير بالقتل في مواضع حيث قالوا : إنَّ للإمام قتل من سرق مراراً سياسة ، ومن تكرّر منه الخنق مراراً ، والساحر إذا تكرر منه السحر، والزنديق ، ومعتاد اللواطة ، ومن سب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأكثر منه وهو ذمي، وصرحوا أيضاً بأنَّ مَن وجد مع زوجته رجلاً يزني ، له قتلهما، وأمثال هذه كثيرة. وصرحوا في «الخلاصة» و«الظهيرية» بجواز التعزير بأخذ المال وبإحراق البيت ونحو ذلك.
إذا عرفت هذا فاعرف أن قول الشارح له : تأديب دون الحد، وهكذا قاله غيره لا يستقيم أن يكون تعريفاً للتعزير لغة، لما عرفت أنه لغة عبارة عن مطلق التأديب، ولا يصح كونه تعريفاً شرعياً وإن اختاره صاحب (الدر المختار)، ولا كونه حكماً للتعزير لما عرفت آنفاً أنَّ التعزير شرعاً قد يكون أشدّ من الحـد أيضاً إذا رأى الإمام المصلحة أيضاً، فلا بد أن يجعل حكماً لنوع خاص من التعزير، وهو التعزير بالضرب. وبالجملة : فالكلام لا يخلو عن مسامحة، فاحفظ هذا فإنّه من السوانح التي قلّ من يطلع عليها.
And in 'al-Bahr': 'The ummah is unanimous on the obligation of ta'zir for a major sin that does not incur hadd, and whoever commits an offense without a fixed hadd, and it is proven before the judge, then ta'zir is obligatory upon him.' End. And in 'al-Sirajiyya': 'Whoever has intercourse under a misapprehension [of marriage] is to be disciplined by ta'zir.' End. And in 'al-Dhakhira': 'If the act is of the type that requires hadd but it was not required due to a legal impediment, then ta'zir is discipline less than hadd, and its root is al-'azr meaning prevention and deterrence. In this case ta'zir reaches its utmost limit. If the act is of the type that does not require hadd, then it does not reach its utmost limit, but it is left to the judge's discretion.' End. And in 'Nisab al-Ihtisab': 'Ta'zir is obligatory like hadd, because it is a punishment for a prohibited act, so it is obligatory, unlike discipline (ta'dib), which is not obligatory but permissible.' End. He said: 'Discipline less than hadd'; know that ta'zir is of various types as I detailed in my treatise 'al-Qawl al-Jazim fi Suqut al-Hadd bi Nikah al-Maharam'. It may be by slapping the neck, rubbing the ear, the judge frowning at him, verbal abuse other than slander (qadhf), imprisonment, exile, execution, beating, and other things. If the judge deems beating appropriate in a case, then he should reduce it from the minimum hadd, which is forty lashes for the wine drinker, as verified in 'Fath al-Qadir'. And a group of scholars mentioned ta'zir by execution in certain cases, saying that the ruler may execute a person who steals repeatedly as a policy, one who repeatedly strangles, a sorcerer who repeats sorcery, a heretic (zindiq), a habitual sodomite, and a dhimmi who insults the Prophet repeatedly. They also explicitly stated that whoever finds a man committing adultery with his wife may kill both. And many similar cases. And they explicitly stated in 'al-Khulasa' and 'al-Zahiriyya' the permissibility of ta'zir by taking property and burning houses and the like. When you know this, then know that the statement of the commentator 'discipline less than hadd', and others like him, is not valid as a linguistic definition of ta'zir, because you know that linguistically it denotes absolute discipline. Nor is it correct as a legal definition, even though the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar chose it, nor is it a legal ruling for ta'zir, because you have just learned that legally ta'zir may sometimes be more severe than hadd if the judge sees maslaha. So it must be taken as a ruling for a specific type of ta'zir, namely ta'zir by beating. In summary, the statement is not without imprecision. So remember this, for it is one of those subtleties few perceive.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on ta'zir by presenting multiple rulings from Hanafi sources. First, Al-Bahr quotes consensus (ijma) that ta'zir is obligatory for major sins without hadd, establishing its wajib status. Al-Sirajiyya provides an example: one who has intercourse under a shubha (misapprehension) is given ta'zir. Al-Dhakhira distinguishes between acts that usually require hadd (but are prevented by a legal impediment) and those that do not; in the former, ta'zir can reach its maximum, while in the latter it is limited. Nisab al-Ihtisab stresses that ta'zir is obligatory like hadd, unlike general discipline (ta'dib) which is merely permissible. The author then comments on the common definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' (discipline less than hadd). He notes that ta'zir can take many forms, even execution or property confiscation, citing scholars who permit the death penalty for repeat offenders like thieves, witches, and heretics, as well as those who find their wives committing adultery. He argues that defining ta'zir as always less than hadd is inaccurate because legally it can be more severe if the judge sees maslaha. Therefore, the definition should be restricted to ta'zir by beating specifically. This critique reflects a nuanced understanding of ta'zir's scope in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
التأديب (al-ta'dib): General discipline, not obligatory; distinct from ta'zir which is wajib.
السياسة (al-siyasa): Ruler's discretionary policy to execute repeat offenders as ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is a correct statement about ta'zir?
A) Ta'zir is always less severe than hadd
B) Ta'zir is obligatory only for major sins
C) Ta'zir can include execution for repeat offenders according to some Hanafi scholars
D) Ta'zir is not obligatory, unlike hadd

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is wajib for any prohibited act without a fixed hadd and its severity can exceed hadd when the ruler deems it necessary for maslaha.

Refining the Definition of Ta'zir: Beyond 'Discipline Less than Hadd'
Critiques the definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' and argues it only applies to beating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' and explain why ta'zir can be more severe than hadd based on Hanafi legal sources.

Core Idea
Defining ta'zir as merely 'discipline less than hadd' fails because ta'zir can be more severe.

This passage critiques a common definition of ta'zir and clarifies its true scope. The author argues that 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' is inadequate both linguistically and legally, as ta'zir includes severe punishments like execution. You will learn to refine your understanding and apply it to legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
The definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' (discipline less than hadd) is quoted from a commentator. Find it in the text.
2
Understand the linguistic objection
Linguistically, ta'zir means 'absolute discipline', not limited. The definition restricts it, which is incorrect.
3
Understand the legal objection
Legally, ta'zir can be more severe than hadd, as shown by the examples of execution. The definition contradicts this reality.
4
Apply the refinement
The definition is only valid for a specific type: ta'zir by beating. For other types, the definition does not hold.

Source Text

Al-Sirajiyya provides an example: one who has intercourse under a shubha (misapprehension) is given ta'zir. Al-Dhakhira distinguishes between acts that usually require hadd (but are prevented by a legal impediment) and those that do not; in the former, ta'zir can reach its maximum, while in the latter it is limited. Nisab al-Ihtisab stresses that ta'zir is obligatory like hadd, unlike general discipline (ta'dib) which is merely permissible. The author then comments on the common definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' (discipline less than hadd). He notes that ta'zir can take many forms, even execution or property confiscation, citing scholars who permit the death penalty for repeat offenders like thieves, witches, and heretics, as well as those who find their wives committing adultery. He argues that defining ta'zir as always less than hadd is inaccurate because legally it can be more severe if the judge sees maslaha. Therefore, the definition should be restricted to ta'zir by beating specifically. This critique reflects a nuanced understanding of ta'zir's scope in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
التأديب (al-ta'dib): General discipline, not obligatory; distinct from ta'zir which is wajib.
السياسة (al-siyasa): Ruler's discretionary policy to execute repeat offenders as ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the critique in the passage, the definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' is best understood as:
A) a precise linguistic definition
B) a valid legal definition for all ta'zir
C) applicable only to ta'zir by beating
D) completely rejected by all Hanafis

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir cannot be universally defined as 'discipline less than hadd'; this definition only fits ta'zir by beating.

Definition and Nature of Ta'zir
Definition and types of ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'zir linguistically and legally, list its various forms, and distinguish it from hadd and ta'dib.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is discretionary punishment less in number than the hadd but can be severe in quality.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of ta'zir, exploring its linguistic roots, legal classification as wajib, and the diverse forms it can take—from a stern look to imprisonment, flogging, or even death in extreme cases. The commentator also critiques the standard definition, offering a refined understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of ta'zir
Look at the root 'azr, meaning prevention and deterrence. Understand that ta'zir linguistically is any disciplinary measure.
The text says: 'wa asluhu min al-'azr, bi-ma'na al-radd wa al-rad''.
2
Understand the legal definition as 'discipline less than the hadd'
Recognize that the sharh defines ta'zir as 'ta'dib duna al-hadd'. The commentator shows this is not a perfect legal definition but applies to the specific type of ta'zir by beating.
The definition is disputed: ta'zir can be more severe in quality than hadd.
3
List the diverse forms of ta'zir
Enumerate the types mentioned: slapping, twisting ear, stern look, verbal reprimand, imprisonment, exile, death, confiscation of property, etc. This shows ta'zir is not limited to beating.
The text mentions al-saf' 'ala al-'unuq, fark al-udhun, nazar al-qadi bi-wajh 'abus, etc.
4
Distinguish ta'zir from ta'dib
Understand that ta'dib is permissible (mubah) and not obligatory, while ta'zir is wajib according to Nisab al-Ihtisab because it is a penalty for a prohibited act.
Ta'dib is for education, ta'zir for deterrence of crimes.
5
Note the commentator's critique of the standard definition
The commentator argues that the definition 'ta'dib duna al-hadd' is neither a complete linguistic nor legal definition, as ta'zir can exceed the hadd in severity. It should be restricted to the beating type.
This is a nuanced point: the definition holds for ta'zir bi-l-darb, not for all forms.
