كتاب الحدود / فصل في التعزير
وهو تأديب دون الحدّ ، وأصله من العزر، بمعنى الرَّد والردع وعارض يبلغ التعزير أقصى غاياته، وإن كان من جنس ما لا يجب فيه الحد لا يبلغ أقصى غاياته، ولكنّه مفوّض إلى رأي الإمام». انتهى.
وفي «نصاب الاحتساب»: «التعزير واجب كالحد ؛ لأنه جزاء فعل هو محظور، فيكون واجباً بخلاف التأديب ؛ لأنه غير واجب بل مباح». انتهى. [١] قوله : تأديب دون الحد؛ اعلم أن التعزير على أنواع على ما فصلتها في رسالتي «القول الجازم في سقوط الحد بنكاح المحارم»، فقد يكون بالصفع على العنق ، وبفرك الأذن، وبنظر القاضي له بوجه عبوس، وبشتم غير القذف، وبالحبس، وبالنفي، وبالقتل، وبالضرب وبغير ذلك، فإن اقتضى رأي القاضي الضرب في واقعة ، فحينئذ ينبغي له أن ينقصه من مقدار أدنى الحدود، وهو أربعون سوطاً لضارب الخمر، كذا حققه في «فتح القدير».
وذكر جمع من المشايخ التعزير بالقتل في مواضع حيث قالوا : إنَّ للإمام قتل من سرق مراراً سياسة ، ومن تكرّر منه الخنق مراراً ، والساحر إذا تكرر منه السحر، والزنديق ، ومعتاد اللواطة ، ومن سب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأكثر منه وهو ذمي، وصرحوا أيضاً بأنَّ مَن وجد مع زوجته رجلاً يزني ، له قتلهما، وأمثال هذه كثيرة. وصرحوا في «الخلاصة» و«الظهيرية» بجواز التعزير بأخذ المال وبإحراق البيت
ونحو ذلك.
إذا عرفت هذا فاعرف أن قول الشارح له : تأديب دون الحد، وهكذا قاله غيره لا يستقيم أن يكون تعريفاً للتعزير لغة، لما عرفت أنه لغة عبارة عن مطلق التأديب، ولا يصح كونه تعريفاً شرعياً وإن اختاره صاحب (الدر المختار)، ولا كونه حكماً للتعزير لما عرفت آنفاً أنَّ التعزير شرعاً قد يكون أشدّ من الحـد أيضاً إذا رأى الإمام المصلحة أيضاً، فلا بد أن يجعل حكماً لنوع خاص من التعزير، وهو التعزير بالضرب. وبالجملة : فالكلام لا يخلو عن مسامحة، فاحفظ هذا فإنّه من السوانح التي قلّ من يطلع عليها.
أكثره تسعة وثلاثون سوطاً، وأقله ثلاثة ، وصح حبسه مع ضربه ، وضربه أشد، ثم للزنا ، ثُمَّ للشرب، ثُمَّ للقذف
(أكثره تسعة وثلاثون سوطاً، وأقله ثلاثة ) : لأنَّ التَّعزير ينبغي أن لا يبلغ الحد"، وأقل الحد أربعون" ، وهي حد العبدِ في القذف والشرب، وأبو يوسف له اعتبر حد الأحرار، وهو ثمانون، ونقص عنها سوطاً في رواية، وخمسة في رواية.

.

[0]قوله : أشد ؛ أي وصفاً من جميع الضربات في الحدود، وبعده ضرب جلد الزنا، وبعده ضرب الشارب، وبعده ضرب القاذف. [٦]قوله : ليحصل الانزجار ؛ فإنّ التعزير نقص من الحد عدداً، فلو خفف وصفاً أيضاً لما حصل انزجار العاصي فيفوت المقصود من إقامة التعزير.
Book of Hudud / Section on Ta'zir. It is discipline less than the hadd, and its origin is from 'azr, meaning prevention and deterrence. ... Its maximum is thirty-nine lashes, and its minimum is three. And it is valid to imprison him with his beating, and his beating is more severe, then for zina, then for drinking, then for qadhf. (Its maximum is thirty-nine lashes, and its minimum is three) because ta'zir should not reach the hadd, and the minimum hadd is forty, which is the hadd of a slave in qadhf and drinking. Abu Yusuf considered the hadd of free men, which is eighty, and decreased it by one lash in one narration, and five in another. And it is valid to imprison him with his beating, and his beating is more severe, then for zina, then for drinking, then for qadhf. They said: so that deterrence is achieved by ta'zir. [1] His statement: its minimum is three; because what is less than it does not achieve deterrence. It is said: the minimum is not specified, rather it is according to what the Imam deems deterrent, and it varies with individuals. [2] His statement: should not reach the hadd; due to the hadith: 'Whoever imposes a hadd in a non-hadd matter is among the transgressors.' Reported by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in Kitab al-Athar and al-Bayhaqi. [3] His statement: forty; said in al-'Inaya: 'This is the truth; because whoever considers the hadd of free men has reached a hadd, and it is the hadd of a slave, and the indefinite article in the hadith contradicts it.' [4] His statement: it is valid to imprison him; meaning if the Imam sees fit to add imprisonment to beating in ta'zir, he may do so, because imprisonment alone is valid as ta'zir, and there is no harm in combining the two types of ta'zir, and upon this is carried what is reported about the married adulterer that he is flogged and then stoned as mentioned. [0] His statement: more severe; meaning in description than all the beatings in hudud, after it is the beating of zina, then the beating of the drinker, then the beating of the qadhif. [6] His statement: so that deterrence is achieved; because ta'zir is less than the hadd in number, so if it is also lightened in description, deterrence of the sinner would not occur and the purpose of establishing ta'zir would be lost.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the definition and details of ta'zir punishment. It begins by stating that ta'zir is 'discipline less than the hadd' and explores its linguistic and legal meanings. The commentator notes that ta'zir can take many forms: a slap, a twist of the ear, a stern look, verbal reprimand, imprisonment, exile, even death or property confiscation in extreme cases. The maximum number of lashes in ta'zir is 39, one less than the minimum hadd of 40 (which is the hadd of a slave for qadhf and drinking). The minimum is 3 lashes. The commentator cites the hadith: 'Whoever imposes a hadd in a non-hadd matter is among the transgressors' to justify that ta'zir must not reach the hadd. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds the maximum to be 79 (one less than the free man's hadd of 80) in one narration, or 75 in another. The passage also states that beating in ta'zir is more severe in quality than any hadd beating, because it is less in quantity. The severity order is: ta'zir beating, then zina, then drinking, then qadhf. Imprisonment may be combined with beating.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that are not subject to fixed hadd penalties, intended to discipline and deter.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for specific crimes (zina, qadhf, drinking, theft, hirabah).
تأديب (ta'dib): Discipline that is not obligatory but permissible; it is less specific than ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a form of ta'zir mentioned?
A) Slapping on the neck
B) Frowning at the offender
C) Amputation of hand
D) Imprisonment

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is a wajib discretionary punishment that may take many forms, but when it involves beating, the number of lashes must be less than the minimum hadd (40).

Limits of Ta'zir Lashes and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Maximum and minimum lashes of ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maximum and minimum of ta'zir lashes, explain the proof, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The maximum ta'zir lashes is 39, one less than the slave's hadd of 40, to avoid reaching the hadd.

This lesson focuses on the specific limits of ta'zir when it involves flogging: the maximum is 39 lashes, the minimum is 3. We examine the hadith proof, the reasoning behind the numbers, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement where Abu Yusuf holds a higher maximum based on the free man's hadd. The passage also clarifies the relative severity of ta'zir beatings compared to hadd punishments.

Guided Path
1
State the maximum and minimum of ta'zir lashes
The text says: 'Aksarihi tis'atun wa thalathuna sawtan, wa aqalluhu thalathah'. Memorize these numbers.
Maximum 39, minimum 3.
2
Understand the proof: the hadith prohibiting reaching the hadd
The hadith: 'Whoever imposes a hadd in a non-hadd matter is among the transgressors.' This shows that ta'zir (which is not a fixed hadd) must not reach the number of any hadd.
The indefinite 'had' implies any hadd, not just the free man's.
3
Identify the baseline: the minimum hadd is 40 (slave's hadd)
The minimum hadd applicable is the slave's hadd for qadhf and drinking, which is 40. Therefore ta'zir must be less than 40, i.e., 39.
Abu Hanifa uses this logic.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf considers the free man's hadd (80) as the baseline, so maximum ta'zir would be 79 or 75. The Hanafi response: the indefinite hadith includes the slave's hadd, so it must be avoided.
The practical difference: 39 vs 79 max.
5
Note the severity order of beatings
The text states that ta'zir beating is more severe in description than any hadd beating, then zina, then drinking, then qadhf. This is to compensate for the lower number.
Purpose: to ensure deterrence despite fewer lashes.

The maximum number of lashes in ta'zir is 39, one less than the minimum hadd of 40 (which is the hadd of a slave for qadhf and drinking). The minimum is 3 lashes. The commentator cites the hadith: 'Whoever imposes a hadd in a non-hadd matter is among the transgressors' to justify that ta'zir must not reach the hadd. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds the maximum to be 79 (one less than the free man's hadd of 80) in one narration, or 75 in another. The passage also states that beating in ta'zir is more severe in quality than any hadd beating, because it is less in quantity. The severity order is: ta'zir beating, then zina, then drinking, then qadhf. Imprisonment may be combined with beating.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that are not subject to fixed hadd penalties, intended to discipline and deter.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for specific crimes (zina, qadhf, drinking, theft, hirabah).
تأديب (ta'dib): Discipline that is not obligatory but permissible; it is less specific than ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school set the maximum ta'zir lashes at 39 instead of 79?
A) Because 79 would exceed the free man's hadd of 80
B) Because the hadd of a slave is 40, and ta'zir must be less than any hadd
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) specifically limited ta'zir to 39
D) Because the minimum hadd is 40 for free men

Key Takeaway
The maximum ta'zir lashes is 39 (mufta bihi), based on the hadith and the slave's hadd of 40; Abu Yusuf's view of 79 is a valid but not operative position.

Maximum Ta'zir Punishment: 39 Lashes or Less?
Ruling on ta'zir maximum with proof and intra-school dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum ta'zir lashes (39), cite the hadith proof, and distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from Abu Yusuf's intra-Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Ta'zir must be less than the least hadd – maximum 39 lashes.

This passage establishes the boundaries of ta'zir punishment. While judges have discretion, the Hanafi school imposes a clear ceiling: ta'zir lashes cannot equal or exceed a hadd punishment. The least hadd in the school is 40 lashes (the slave's hadd for qadhf and shurb), so ta'zir is capped at 39. We will examine the prophetic proof and the internal debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Ta'zir Maximum
State the Hanafi position: ta'zir maximum is 39 lashes. Note that the minimum is 3 lashes (though not the main focus).
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
Find the hadith cited: 'Whoever reaches a hadd in other than a hadd is among the transgressors.' Understand its implication: ta'zir must stay below the lowest hadd (40).
The hadith is narrated by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in al-Athar and by al-Bayhaqi.
3
Analyze Wajh al-Dalalah
The prohibition explicitly targets 'reaching a hadd' – i.e., matching the quantum of a hadd punishment. Since the least hadd is 40, ta'zir cannot be 40 or more; thus 39 is the ceiling.
This is a clear textual indication (dalalah qati'ah) in the Hanafi view.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf argued the benchmark should be the free person's hadd (80) and reduced by 1 or 5. Understand why Abu Hanifa's view is mufta bihi: the hadith refers to any hadd, including the slave's hadd.
The tarjih authority is the commentator (Al-Hidayah) who prefers Abu Hanifa.

Its maximum is thirty-nine lashes, and its minimum is three: because ta'zir should not reach the hadd, and the least hadd is forty, which is the hadd of a slave in qadhf and shurb. Abu Yusuf considered the hadd of free persons, which is eighty, and he reduced it by one lash in one narration, and by five in another. Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and the order of severity among hadd (fixed) punishments. The matn states that the maximum ta'zir is 39 lashes and the minimum is 3, because ta'zir must not reach the level of hadd. The least hadd is 40 lashes (the hadd for a slave in qadhf and shurb). Abu Yusuf considered the hadd of free persons (80) and reduced it slightly, but Abu Hanifa's view (39 max) is mufta bihi. The commentary explains that the severity of the beating in ta'zir is greater than any hadd flogging to compensate for the lesser number. The order of severity among hadd beatings is: zina (most severe), then shurb (drinking alcohol), then qadhf (false accusation of zina). This order is based on the strength of the evidence for each hadd: zina is established by the Quran, shurb by consensus of the Companions (not explicit Quranic text), and qadhf is established by the Quran but its cause is probable (the accuser may be truthful). The commentator then raises an objection: qadhf is also established by Quranic text, and shurb is analogized to it, so why is the beating for shurb more severe? The objection notes that shurb lacks a direct text. The rebuttal cites the hadith about flogging for drinking, showing shurb does have textual basis. The discussion illustrates Hanafi legal reasoning (talil) about the relative weights of proofs.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for crimes not covered by fixed hadd penalties, administered at the judge's discretion.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed, prescribed punishment for specific crimes (zina, qadhf, shurb, theft, etc.), determined by Quran or Sunnah.
القذف (al-qadhf): False accusation of zina (unlawful sexual intercourse) against a married person, punishable by 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 39
B) 40
C) 79
D) 80

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir lashes must be less than the least hadd punishment; the Hanafi school caps it at 39.

Severity Order of Hadd Beatings: Zina, Shurb, Qadhf
Ruling on the severity order of hadd beatings with talil (causal reasoning) and a critical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the order of severity of hadd beatings (zina > shurb > qadhf) according to the Hanafi school, and evaluate the talil (causal reasoning) behind the order, including the objection and rebuttal in the commentary.

Core Idea
The severity of hadd beatings reflects the strength of the evidence: Quranic text > consensus > possible truthfulness.

The passage also ranks the severity of flogging for different hadd crimes. According to the matn, the beating for zina is most severe, then shurb (drinking alcohol), then qadhf (false accusation). The reasoning, as explained by the commentators, is based on the type and certainty of the evidence for each crime. Later, the commentator raises and rebuts an objection that challenges the ranking, illustrating a classic Hanafi talil (causal reasoning) discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Order of Severity
State the ranking: beating for zina (most severe) > shurb > qadhf (least severe). Note that the passage is about the severity of the beating, not the number of lashes.
All three hadd punishments involve flogging: zina = 100 lashes (unmarried) or stoning (married); shurb = 80 lashes; qadhf = 80 lashes.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
The commentators give a rationale: zina's hadd is established by the Quran (nass), shurb's by consensus (ijma) of the Companions, and qadhf's by the Quran but its cause (the accusation) is probable (muh'tamal) because the accuser might be truthful. The beatings are ordered accordingly: nass > ijma > possible truthfulness.
The 'cause' here refers to the underlying reason for the hadd – for qadhf, the possibility of truthfulness reduces the severity.
3
Analyze the Objection (I'tirad)
The commentator objects: qadhf is also established by the Quran (nass), and shurb is analogized (qiyas) to qadhf. So why is shurb's beating more severe? The objection points out that nass should be stronger than qiyas.
This is a methodological objection: the original reasoning seems inconsistent.
4
Examine the Rebuttal (Jawab)
The rebuttal (not fully given in the excerpt) would argue that shurb too has a textual basis (hadith: 'Whoever drinks wine, flog him'), so it is not merely qiyas. The severity order might also consider other factors like societal harm.
The passage is cut off, but the discussion illustrates Hanafi critical thinking (naqd).

Severity of Hadd Punishments: Drinking vs. Qadhf
Debate on why hadd for drinking is more severe than for qadhf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the relative severity of hadd punishments for drinking and qadhf, and explain the three preponderances that support the drinking hadd’s greater severity.

Core Idea
The hadd for drinking is more severe than for qadhf because the drinker often commits both and qadhf carries an extra penalty of rejected testimony.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator defends the established Hanafi view that the hadd punishment for drinking (shurb) is inflicted more severely than the hadd for false accusation of zina (qadhf). He addresses an objection that qadhf should be stronger because it is based on a clear Quranic text, while drinking is based on consensus and analogy. By examining the certainty of the crime, the frequency of combined offenses, and the additional penalty of rejecting the qadhif's testimony, the commentator shows why the drinking hadd is actually more severe in its execution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and the objection
First, note that the original argument claims the hadd for drinking is more severe because its cause is certain, while qadhf's cause is probable. Then recognize the objection: qadhf is directly from the Quran (24:4), whereas drinking is only by qiyas on qadhf.
The objection is raised by the commentator himself ('I say') to test the reasoning.
2
Analyze the first response: drinking is also from text and ijma
The commentator counters that the hadd for drinking is also established by textual evidence (hadith) and, more importantly, by the consensus of the Companions, which is a stronger source than individual qiyas.
Even if the Quranic text is considered superior, ijma is a decisive proof.
3
Examine the three preponderances for drinking hadd
The commentator lists three preponderances: (1) certainty of the cause (drinking is undeniably proven), (2) the drinker often commits qadhf as well, resulting in two offenses, and (3) qadhf carries an extra punishment—rejection of testimony—so its beating is made lighter to avoid excessive harshness.
These preponderances override the apparent textual superiority of qadhf.
4
Understand the role of the additional penalty in qadhf
The Quran (24:4) not only prescribes flogging but also states that the qadhif's testimony is never accepted. This additional penalty justifies making the flogging lighter, whereas drinking has no such extra punishment.
Thus, the severity of the beating is inversely related to the presence of other penalties.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the beating for the hadd of drinking is more severe than for qadhf, due to the preponderances mentioned.
This ruling is from the later Hanafi authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

الأوّل : إنّ حدّ الشرب أيضاً ثبت بالنص، وهو حديث : «من شرب الخمر فاجلدوه» إلى غير ذلك من الأحاديث على ما مرّ ذكر بعضها، وجوابه من وجهين :
(۱) من سورة النور، الآية (٤).
(۲) النور : من الآية ٢.
أحدهما : إنّ المراد بالنص نص الكتاب، فلا ينافيه ثبوت حدّ الشرب بالحديث وفيه : ضعف ظاهر ؛ فإن قوله : «قيس» صريح في أنه ثابت بالقياس لا نص فيه من الكتاب والسنّة ؛ إذ لا حاجة إلى القياس بعد ورودِ الكتاب والسنة.
وثانيهما : إن المراد بحدّ الشرب هاهنا هو المقدار المعين، فإن ثبوت حد الشرب
بأصله، وإن كان بالنسبة المرفوعة لكن تقديره ثبت بالقياس.
والثاني : إنّ حدّ الشرب لم يثبت بالقياس بل بإجماع الصحابة له ، نعم سند الإجماع هو القياس، وقد ثبت في موضعه إنّ الحكم يستند إلى الإجماع لا إلى سنده. والثالث : إنَّ بعد تسليم ثبوته بالقياس لا يخفى أن لحد الشرب مرجحات على
حد القذف : ١. منها ما مرّ إنّ حدّ الشرب متيقن، وسبب حد القذف محتمل. ٢. ومنها : إنّ شارب المسكر قلما يخلو من القذف، فيصير كل شارب جامعاً بين
الشرب والقذف غالباً، فتتحقق من الشارب جنايتان ، ومن القاذف جناية واحدة. . ومنها : إنّ حدّ الشرب ثابت بالإجماع وبالقياس علـى حـد القذف، وحـدّ الشرب مخالف للقياس. وبالجملة : فبالنظر إلى هذه المرجّحات حكموا بجعل ضرب حدّ الشرب أشدّ من ضرب حد القذف، على أنّ القذف سوى الضرب عقوبة أخرى أيضاً، وهي عدم قبول شهادة القاذف أبداً، كما دلّ عليه النص، فناسب أن يجعل ضربه أخف ؛ لئلا يلزم التغليظ الشديد بخلاف حدّ الشرب، فليس للشرب جزاء سوى الجلد، فناسب
تشديده.
Book of Hudud, Section on Ta'zir. They said: So that deterrence is achieved by ta'zir. The hadd of zina is established by the text, and the hadd of drinking is established by the consensus of the Companions, and its cause is certain, and the cause of the hadd of qadhf is probable, due to the possibility of truthfulness. I say: The hadd of qadhf is established by the text, which is His saying the Most High: 'So flog them with eighty lashes' (Surah An-Nur, 4). And the hadd of drinking is measured by analogy upon the hadd of qadhf. [Footnote 1]: His saying: 'and the hadd of zina...' etc. The summary is that the hadd of zina is established by the Book, which is the verse 'The adulteress and the adulterer' (Surah An-Nur, 2) etc., so its beating is more severe than the beating of the hadd of drinking, which is established by the consensus of the Companions, not by text. Since its cause is certain and the cause of the hadd of qadhf is probable, they made its beating more severe than the beating of the hadd of qadhf. [Footnote 2]: His saying: 'due to the possibility of truthfulness'; meaning that it is possible that the accuser is truthful in his accusation. If you say: If he were truthful, he would have brought evidence for his claim. I say: This does not indicate the certainty of his falsehood, because it is possible that his witnesses have gone away and refused to testify. [Footnote 3]: His saying: 'I say...' etc.; an objection to what they used to justify the ordering in two ways: First, that the hadd of qadhf is established by the text of the Book, unlike the hadd of drinking, which is not indicated by a text. Second, that the hadd of drinking is measured by analogy upon the hadd of qadhf as mentioned. And it is known that what is established by the text, and is the object of analogy, is more preponderant and stronger relative to what is not indicated by a text but is established by analogy. Then it is objected to it from several aspects, most of which came to my mind at this time: First: That the hadd of drinking is also established by a text, which is the hadith: 'Whoever drinks wine, flog him' and other narratives as some were mentioned. And its answer is from two aspects: (1) from Surah An-Nur, verse 4. (2) An-Nur: from verse 2. One is that by 'text' is meant the text of the Book, so the establishment of the hadd of drinking by hadith does not contradict it. But it has an apparent weakness because his saying 'measured by analogy' is explicit that it is established by qiyas, and not by text from the Book and Sunnah, since there would be no need for qiyas after the Book and Sunnah. Second: That by 'hadd of drinking' here is meant the specified quantity, for the establishment of the hadd of drinking in its origin, although it is by the raised (reported) text, but its determination is established by qiyas. Second objection: That the hadd of drinking is not established by qiyas but by the consensus of the Companions. Yes, the basis of consensus is qiyas, and it has been established in its place that the ruling is attributed to consensus, not to its basis. Third objection: That after conceding its establishment by qiyas, it is not hidden that the hadd of drinking has preponderances over the hadd of qadhf: 1. Among them is what was mentioned: that the hadd of drinking is certain, and the cause of the hadd of qadhf is probable. 2. Among them: that the drinker of intoxicants rarely lacks qadhf, so every drinker becomes one who combines drinking and qadhf mostly, so two offenses are realized from the drinker, and from the qadhif only one offense. 3. Among them: that the hadd of drinking is established by consensus and by qiyas on the hadd of qadhf, and the hadd of drinking is contrary to qiyas. In summary, considering these preponderances, they ruled to make the beating of the hadd of drinking more severe than the beating of the hadd of qadhf. Moreover, qadhf has another punishment besides beating, which is the permanent rejection of the qadhif's testimony, as indicated by the text, so it was appropriate to make his beating lighter to avoid extreme harshness. In contrast, the hadd of drinking has no recompense except flogging, so it was appropriate to make it severe.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi discussion on the relative severity of hadd punishments, specifically comparing the hadd for drinking alcohol (shurb) and the hadd for false accusation of zina (qadhf). The original question (raised by 'they') is why the hadd for zina is more severe than the hadd for drinking, which is more severe than the hadd for qadhf. The commentator ('I') objects to the reasoning and provides a detailed rebuttal. The passage first presents the original argument: the hadd for zina is based on a clear textual proof (Quran), the hadd for drinking is based on ijma of the Companions (with certainty of the crime), while the hadd for qadhf is based on text (Quran 24:4) but with a possible excuse (the accuser might be truthful). Then the commentator objects that the hadd for qadhf is also from text, and the hadd for drinking is qiyas on qadhf, which would make qadhf stronger. However, he then refutes his own objection by bringing three counter-arguments: (1) the hadd for drinking is also from text (hadith), but the text here refers to the Book, so it's still weaker; (2) the hadd for drinking is actually established by ijma, not qiyas, so it's stronger; (3) even if it were qiyas, it has additional preponderances: the certainty of the crime, the frequency of combined offenses, and the fact that qadhf carries an extra punishment (rejection of testimony). Thus, the final ruling is that the beating for the hadd of drinking is more severe than that for qadhf. The passage follows the order of presenting an opinion, an objection, and a detailed defense, typical of scholarly debate in the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah, intended to deter.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by the Quran or Sunnah for specific offenses (e.g., zina, qadhf, drinking).
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina against a chaste person, subject to the hadd of 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadd for drinking is directly from the Quran.
B) Because the drinker often also commits qadhf, and drinking has no additional penalty like rejection of testimony.
C) Because the hadd for qadhf is only based on ijma.
D) Because the hadd for drinking has a higher number of lashes.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the hadd for drinking is inflicted more severely than for qadhf due to the certainty of the crime and the absence of an additional penalty beyond flogging.

Ta'zir for Accusing a Slave or Non-Muslim of Zina
Ruling on discretionary punishment for qadhf against a slave or non-Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qadhf against a slave or non-Muslim and explain why it differs from hadd.

Core Idea
False accusation of zina against a slave or non-Muslim incurs ta'zir, not hadd.

This passage clarifies a key boundary of the hadd penalty for qadhf (false accusation of zina). While hadd is reserved for accusing a free, chaste Muslim, accusations against slaves or non-Muslims fall under ta'zir — a discretionary punishment. The commentator also suggests a linguistic refinement to encompass broader insults that warrant ta'zir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qadhf (false accusation of zina) against a slave (mamluk) or a non-Muslim (kafir). The ruling is ta'zir, a discretionary punishment.
2
Understand the hukm level
The commentary states that 'establishing ta'zir is obligatory' (wajib) upon the judge. This means the judge must apply a punishment, but its severity is at his discretion.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the ruling
Hadd of qadhf (80 lashes) applies only when the accused is a free Muslim and muhsin (chaste). For slaves or non-Muslims, the conditions for hadd are not met, so the case shifts to ta'zir.
4
Reflect on the commentator's suggestion
The commentator suggests that the author could have used the word 'shatm' (insult) instead of 'qadhf' to include other insults that also warrant ta'zir, such as calling a Muslim 'ya fasiq'.

وعزر بقذف مملوك ، أو كافر بزنا
(۱) قوله : وعزر؛ مجهول من التعزير والضمير إلى القاذف، وفيه إشارة إلى أنّ إقامة التعزير واجبة.
[٢] قوله : بقذف ؛ الأولى أن يقول : بشتم ليعم ما سيأتي من قوله لمسلم : يا فاسق، وغير ذلك، فإنّ القذف يطلقُ شرعاً على الرمي بالزني لا على مطلق الشتم.
And he is punished with ta'zir for accusing a slave or a non-Muslim of zina. (1) His saying: 'and he is punished with ta'zir' is passive from ta'zir, and the pronoun refers to the accuser, and it indicates that establishing ta'zir is obligatory. [2] His saying: 'for accusing' – it would be better to say 'for insulting' to include what will come later of his saying to a Muslim: 'O sinner' and others, because 'qadhf' is legally used for accusing of zina, not for absolute insult.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for a person who falsely accuses a slave (mamluk) or a non-Muslim (kafir) of zina. The author first states the ruling: such a person is punished with ta'zir, not hadd, because the hadd of qadhf (80 lashes) is only for accusing a free Muslim of zina. The commentary then explains that the verb 'uzzira' (passive) indicates that the implementation of ta'zir is obligatory (wajib) upon the judge. The commentator also suggests that the author could have used a more general word like 'insult' (shatm) to include not only qadhf but also other insults like calling a Muslim 'O sinner' (ya fasiq), which also warrant ta'zir. This clarifies that in Hanafi fiqh, qadhf specifically means accusing someone of zina, while other slanderous statements fall under general ta'zir.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd.
قذف (qadhf): Linguistically 'to throw', but legally: false accusation of zina against a person.

Severity of Hadd for Drinking vs. Slander
Comparative argument for severity of hadd punishments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the three preponderating factors that make hadd al-shurb more severe than hadd al-qadf according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Hadd for drinking is more severe because it is certain, involves two crimes, and has no extra penalty beyond flogging.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah delves into a nuanced Hanafi argument: even though the hadd for drinking (shurb) is based on analogy and hadith while the hadd for slander (qadhf) is from the Quran, the actual punishment for drinking is more severe. The commentator addresses objections and provides three compelling preponderating factors that justify this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Recognize that the debate is about which hadd (drinking or slander) carries a more severe beating. The Hanafi position is that drinking is more severe.
2
Locate the Hanafi position and its proofs
The Hanafi position is supported by three preponderating factors (murajjihāt): (1) certainty of the offense, (2) multiplicity of crimes, (3) absence of an additional penalty beyond flogging.
The passage also responds to objections about the basis of the hadd (qiyas vs. ijma).
3
Analyze the first preponderating factor
Hadd for drinking is certain (muta yaqqin) because it applies to any adult Muslim who drinks, while the cause for hadd qadhf depends on the victim's chastity, which is probable.
Probable cause makes the punishment potentially lighter.
4
Analyze the second preponderating factor
A drinker almost always commits slander as well, thus combining two crimes (shurb and qadhf), whereas a slanderer commits only one. This aggravates the drinker's guilt.
5
Analyze the third preponderating factor
Slander has an additional punishment of permanent rejection of testimony, so its flogging is lightened to avoid excessive severity. Drinking has no such extra penalty, so its flogging is intensified.
This balances the overall punishment package.

(۱) قوله : وعزر؛ مجهول من التعزير والضمير إلى القاذف، وفيه إشارة إلى أنّ إقامة التعزير واجبة.

He gives ta'zir for slandering a slave or a non-Muslim with zina. He gives ta'zir for slandering a slave or a non-Muslim with zina. One of them: That what is meant by 'text' is the text of the Book, so the establishment of the hadd for drinking by hadith does not contradict it. In it is apparent weakness; for his statement 'qīsa' is explicit that it is established by analogy, no text from the Book and Sunnah; for there is no need for analogy after the revelation of the Book and Sunnah. And the second of them: That what is meant by hadd for drinking here is the specific amount, for the establishment of the hadd for drinking in its origin, even if it is [based] on a raised [narration], but its estimation is established by analogy. Yes, the basis of the consensus is analogy, and it is established in its place that the ruling is attributed to consensus, not to its basis. [2] His saying: 'for slandering': it would be better to say 'for insulting' to include what will come of his saying to a Muslim 'O fasiq' and others, for qadhf is used in Shari'ah for accusing of zina, not for absolute insult.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding ta'zir for slander and an extended argument comparing the severity of the hadd punishments for drinking (shurb) and slander (qadhf). The commentator begins with a footnote clarifying that 'ta'zir' is a discretionary punishment and its execution is obligatory in this context. He then notes that 'qadhf' strictly means accusing of zina, not general insult. The main discussion addresses an objection: since the hadd for drinking is established by a hadith (not Quran), how can it be considered a hadd? The commentator presents three arguments for the Hanafi position that the hadd for drinking is actually more severe than for slander: (1) The hadd for drinking is certain (it always applies), while the cause for slander is probable (the victim may not be chaste). (2) A drinker almost always commits slander, so he commits two crimes, whereas a slanderer commits only one. (3) The hadd for drinking is established by consensus and analogy, while slander has an additional punishment (rejection of testimony), so its beating is lighter to avoid excessive severity. Thus, the beating for drinking is more severe. The commentator follows the order of the matn and explains each point clearly.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses not subject to hadd or qisas, determined by the judge.
قذف (qadhf): Specifically accusing someone of zina, which entails hadd punishment. Not general insult.
حد الشرب (hadd al-shurb): The prescribed punishment for drinking alcohol, which is flogging (80 lashes in Hanafi).

B) Because the drinker almost always also commits slander, combining two crimes.
C) Because slander has no additional punishment beyond lashes.
D) Because the hadd for drinking is established by analogy only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the flogging for drinking is more severe than for slander due to three factors: certainty, multiplicity of crimes, and lack of extra penalty.

Rulings on Ta'zir for Insulting Terms
Commentary on ta'zir for insulting words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'zir for specific insulting terms and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the term 'Luti'.

Core Idea
Insulting a Muslim with specific terms triggers ta'zir, not hadd—except for 'Luti' where the Sahibayn argue for hadd.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for insulting a Muslim with terms like 'fasiq', 'kafir', 'khabith', etc. The commentary clarifies conditions, such as the target not being known for that trait, and highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the term 'Luti'. By the end, you will be able to apply these rules and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the insulting terms covered
List the specific terms mentioned in the text: 'یا فاسق', 'یا کافر', 'یا خبیث', 'یا سارق', 'یا فاجر', 'یا مخنث', 'یا خائن', 'یا لوطي', 'یا زندیق'. Note that these are examples, and similar terms also warrant ta'zir.
The list is not exhaustive; the ruling applies to any term that is clearly insulting and implies a fault.
2
Understand the conditions for ta'zir
Ta'zir is obligatory only if the target is not known for the trait being attributed (e.g., calling someone 'fasiq' when they are not publicly known as a sinner). Additionally, the insult must be directed at a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi).
The text specifies this condition for 'fasiq' and similar terms.
3
Analyze the term 'Luti' and the intra-Hanafi dispute
For the term 'یا لوطي' (O sodomite), Abu Hanifa rules ta'zir (unless the intent is to attribute to the people of Lut as a tribe). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that the speaker receives the hadd punishment for sodomy (analogous to the act). The preferred view (mufta bihi) is ta'zir.
The dispute arises from whether the insult is analogous to a false accusation of sodomy (which has a fixed hadd) or a mere insult.
4
Apply the ruling to other terms
For terms like 'kafir', 'khabith', 'mukhanath', etc., the ruling is consistent: ta'zir applies, with the exception that if the speaker genuinely believes the target's religion is kufr (unbelief) and intends that meaning, the speaker becomes a kafir, but ta'zir is still imposed for the insult.
The text emphasizes that the context of anger eliminates alternative innocent interpretations.

Source Text

كتاب الحدود فصل في التعزير
ومسلم بيا فاسق یا کافر يا خبيث، یا سارق، یا فاجر
[١] قوله : ومسلم ؛ عطف على قوله : «مملوك» ؛ أي عزّرِ بشتم مسلم بأحد من الألفاظ الآتية، وقيد «المسلم» اتفاقي؛ فإنّ شتم «الذمي» أيضا يوجب التعزير. كذا في «الفتح»). وذكر في «القنية»: قال ليهودي أو نصراني أو مجوسي يا كافر يأثم إن شق عليه ، ومقتضاه أنه يعزّر لارتكابه الإثم. كذا في «البحر »
[٢] قوله : بيا فاسق ؛ الفسق لغة : الخروج، وشرعاً: يطلق على الخروج عن الطاعة، فيشمل الكافر أيضاً، وكثيراً ما يطلق على غير الكافر ممن يرتكب الكبائر أو يدمن بالصغائر، ومثله الفجور. وفي ذكر «يا فاجر» بعد ذكر «يا فاسق» إشارة إلى تغايرها ؛ فإنّ الفاجر في عرف الشرع يطلق على الكافر وعلى الزاني ، وكثيراً ما يطلق على كثير الخصام والمنازعة، ثمّ الشتم بالفسق ونحوه إنّما يوجب التعزير إذا لم يكن المقذوف معلوم الفسق قبل ذلك. كذا في «الفتح»
[٣] قوله : يا كافر؛ وهل يكفر بإطلاق الكفر على المسلم، المختار إنه إن أراد الشتم لا يكفر، وإن اعتقد دينه كفراً كفر ؛ لأن اعتقاد دين الإسلام كفراً كفر. كذا في «الذخيرة». وفي إطلاقه إشارة إلى أنه يستحق التعزير بمجرد هذا اللفظ ، وقيل : لا يعزر ما لم يقل : كافر بالله ؛ لاحتمال أن يريد به الكافر بالطاغوت أو الكافر من كفـران النعمة، ولا يخفى أن قرينة حال السب والغضب تقطعُ هذا الاحتمال.
[٤] قوله : يا خبيث؛ من الخباثة، هو ضدّ الطيب، وكثيراً ما يطلق على المؤذي والشرير والمنافق.
يا مخنث ، يا خائن، يا لوطي، يا زندیق
[١] قوله : يا مخنث ؛ اسم مفعول من التخنيث يقال : خنثه فتخنّث، أي عطفه خلقته . خلقةُ النِّساءِ في حركاته وسكناته وكلامه، فإن كان ذلك خلقياً وهو من فليس بمذموم وإن كان يتكلف فهو الذي يعدّ شتماً ، وجاء كسر النون أيضاً على صيغة اسم الفاعل ، وقيل : هو مرادف لمن يرتكب اللواطة.
[٢] قوله : يا خائن ؛ هو الذي يخون فيما يده من الأمانات.
[٣] قوله : يا لوطي ؛ قيل يسأل عنه، فإن أراد إنّه من قوم لوط على نبينا وعليه السلام لا يعزّر، وإن أراد أنه يعمل عملهم من الإتيان في الدبر فاعلاً أو مفعولاً عزر عنده، وعندهما: حدّ كما يحد مرتكب هذا الفعل عندهما، والصحيح تعزيره، ولو في غضب أو هزل. كذا في «الفتح».
[٤] قوله : يا زندیق ؛ - بكسر الزاي المعجمة وكسر الدال المهملة، بينهما نون ساكنة، وبعدهما ياء مثناة تحتيّة ساكنة، ثم قاف - ، فارسي معرب أصله : زنده أو زندي، والراجح هو الأول، وهو نسبة إلى زند ، وهو اسم كتاب أظهره مزدك، رئيس الفرقة المزدكية، من الكفار الثنوية القائلين بتعدد الآله في زمن كسرى قباذ فنسب إليه أصحابه، وقد قتله کسری نوشیروان وما نقله المطرزي في «المغرب» : إنّ أصله زنده؛ أي يقول بدوام الدهر، فمبني على عدم الفرق بين الدهري وبين الزنديق ، كذا حققه أحمد بن سليمان الرومي الشهير بابن كمال باشا ، مؤلّف الايضاح والاصلاح في رسالة ألفها في تحقيق معنى الزنديق. ولما كان دين الزنادقة خارجاً عن الأديان السماوية كلها، سمّى العرب كلِّ مَن خرج عن الأديان السماوية زنديقاً، ويعبر عنه بالفارسية: بي دين.
Book of Hudud, Section on Ta'zir. And [insulting] a Muslim by saying 'O sinner' or 'O disbeliever' or 'O wicked' or 'O thief' or 'O debauchee'. [1] His saying: 'and a Muslim' is conjuncted to his saying: 'a slave'; meaning, administer ta'zir for insulting a Muslim with any of the following words. The restriction to 'Muslim' is incidental; indeed insulting a dhimmi also necessitates ta'zir. Thus in al-Fath. And it is mentioned in al-Qunyah: If he says to a Jew, Christian, or Magian 'O disbeliever', he sins if it grieves him, and its implication is that he is given ta'zir for committing the sin. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'O sinner'—sin (fisq) linguistically means leaving, and legally it is used for leaving obedience, so it includes the disbeliever as well, and often it is used for non-disbelievers who commit major sins or persist in minor sins. Similar is fujur. The mention of 'O debauchee' after 'O sinner' indicates their difference; for 'debauchee' in legal custom is used for the disbeliever and the adulterer, and often for one who argues and quarrels much. Then insult by fisq and the like only necessitates ta'zir if the accused was not known for fisq beforehand. Thus in al-Fath. [3] His saying: 'O disbeliever'—and does he become a disbeliever by applying unbelief to a Muslim? The chosen view is that if he intended insult, he does not become a disbeliever; if he believed his religion to be unbelief, he becomes a disbeliever, because believing Islam to be unbelief is unbelief. Thus in al-Dhakhirah. And its mention indicates that he deserves ta'zir solely by this word. And it is said: he is not given ta'zir unless he says 'disbeliever in Allah', due to the possibility that he meant the disbeliever in the taghut or the ingrate for blessings. But it is not hidden that the context of insult and anger cuts off this possibility. [4] His saying: 'O wicked'; from khabathah, it is the opposite of good, and often used for the harmful, the evil, and the hypocrite. O effeminate, O traitor, O sodomite, O heretic. [1] His saying: 'O effeminate'—a passive participle from takhnith; it is said: khannathahu fa-takhanatha, i.e., his nature turned him to the nature of women in his movements, stillness, and speech. If that is innate, then it is not blameworthy; if it is affected, then it is considered an insult. Also with kasra of the nun as a participle; and it is said it is synonymous with one who commits sodomy. [2] His saying: 'O traitor'—he who betrays the trusts in his hand. [3] His saying: 'O sodomite'—it is said he is asked; if he meant that he is from the people of Lut, peace be upon him, he is not given ta'zir; if he meant that he does their deed of anal intercourse, as agent or object, then he is given ta'zir according to him [Abu Hanifa], and according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] he is given the hadd punishment as for the perpetrator of this act according to them, and the correct view is ta'zir, even if in anger or jest. Thus in al-Fath. [4] His saying: 'O heretic'—with kasra of the za and dhal, with a silent nun between them, then a silent ya, then qaf—Persian arabicized, its origin being zandah or zandi, and the preponderant is the first, and it is a nisba to Zand, which is the name of a book revealed by Mazdak, chief of the Mazdakian sect, among the dualist disbelievers who claimed multiple deities in the time of Kisra Qubad, so his followers were attributed to him. Kisra Nushirwan killed him. Al-Mutarrizi in al-Mughrib transmitted that its origin is zindah, i.e., he says 'the eternity of time', based on no difference between the dehrī and the zindīq. Thus verified by Ahmad ibn Sulayman al-Rumi known as Ibn Kamal Pasha, author of al-Idah and al-Islah in a treatise on investigating the meaning of zindīq. And since the religion of the zanadiqah is outside all heavenly religions, the Arabs called everyone who left the heavenly religions a zindīq, and in Persian it is expressed as 'bi-din' (without religion).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the legal rules concerning insults that warrant ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in Hanafi fiqh. It opens with the general rule that insulting a Muslim with specific terms like 'O sinner', 'O disbeliever', 'O wicked', etc., necessitates ta'zir. The commentator notes that the restriction to 'Muslim' is incidental; insulting a dhimmi also merits ta'zir, as stated in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The term 'fasiq' is discussed: it linguistically means 'one who leaves', and legally encompasses both disbelievers and sinful Muslims who commit major sins or persist in minor ones. The commentator clarifies that such an insult only warrants ta'zir if the target was not previously known for that trait. For the term 'kafir', the commentator addresses whether the speaker becomes a disbeliever: the preferred view is that if the intent is insult, he does not become a disbeliever; but if he believes the target's religion to be unbelief, he is a disbeliever. He also notes that merely uttering 'O disbeliever' warrants ta'zir, and the context of anger eliminates any other interpretation. The term 'khabith' (wicked) is defined as the opposite of good, often applied to harmful or hypocritical individuals. On page 258, the commentary covers 'mukhanath' (effeminate), stating that if it is innate it is not blameworthy, but if affected it is an insult. 'Kha'in' (traitor) is one who betrays trusts. The term 'Luti' (sodomite) involves an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, if the intent is to accuse of sodomy, it merits ta'zir; but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), it merits the hadd punishment as for the act itself. The preferred view is ta'zir. Finally, 'zindiq' (heretic) is explained in detail: it originates from the Persian 'zandah' or 'zindi', referring to followers of Mazdak, a dualist sect. Later, Arabs used it for anyone outside heavenly religions, equivalent to Persian 'bi-din'. The commentator cites various sources, emphasizing that calling someone a zindiq warrants ta'zir.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zīr): A discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses without fixed hadd or kaffarah.
فاسق (fāsiq): One who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; can include kafir in some contexts.
زندیق (zindīq): A follower of a dualistic religion, originally Zoroastrian or Manichaean; later used for anyone outside the heavenly religions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if someone calls a Muslim 'يا كافر' (O disbeliever) during a fit of anger?
A) He is considered a disbeliever himself.
B) He is given ta'zir only.
C) He receives no punishment if he didn't mean it.
D) He must perform kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is the default punishment for insulting Muslims with specific terms, with the exception of 'Luti' where the preferred view is also ta'zir despite the Sahibayn's objection.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
8 min
Ta'zir for Defamatory Insults (Sabb) – Definitions and Rulings
Commentary on definitions and ta'zir rulings for various insulting names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each insult term and state the Hanafi ta'zir ruling for each, including the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the term 'luti'.

Core Idea
Defamatory insults like calling someone effeminate or heretic incur ta'zir, but the term 'luti' has an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether it triggers hadd or ta'zir.

This lesson unpacks a key passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that defines various insulting epithets and their legal consequences in Hanafi law. You will learn the precise meanings of terms like mukhannath, kha'in, luti, zindiq, dayyuth, and qurtaban, and understand when they warrant discretionary punishment (ta'zir) rather than the fixed hadd penalty. The passage also highlights a notable disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two students on how to treat the accusation of sodomy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the insult term and its linguistic meaning
For each term (e.g., mukhannath, kha'in), first understand its literal definition as provided by the commentator. Note whether the term has an innocent usage or is inherently derogatory.
Some terms like mukhannath can be non-insulting if innate.
2
Determine if the term is intended as an insult
Examine the context of utterance. If the term is used to demean (e.g., affecting effeminacy), it is an insult; if it describes a factual state without malice, it is not.
The commentator clarifies that the intention matters.
3
State the default ruling: ta'zir
Most terms in the passage are ruled to incur ta'zir (discretionary punishment) when used as insults. This is the baseline Hanafi position.
Ta'zir is at the judge's discretion, not a fixed punishment.
4
Analyze the exception: the term 'luti' and the intra-Hanafi dispute
The term 'luti' has a special case: if intended as an accusation of sodomy, Abu Hanifa rules ta'zir, while the Sahibayn rule hadd (qadhf). The text declares the correct view (al-sahih) as ta'zir.
This shows a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school, resolved by later authorities.
5
Apply the rule to other examples in the passage
Review the remaining terms (dayyuth, qurtaban, ibn al-qahba, etc.) and confirm that each yields ta'zir, noting any minor nuances (e.g., insulting lineage is more severe).
The form of address (direct or indirect) does not change the ruling.

يا مخنث یا خائن، یا لوطی ، یا زندیق
فتعطف،
[١] قوله : يا مخنث ؛ اسم مفعول من التخنيث يقال : خنثه فتخنّث، أي عطفه خلقته . خلقةُ النِّساءِ في حركاته وسكناته وكلامه، فإن كان ذلك خلقياً وهو من فليس بمذموم وإن كان يتكلف فهو الذي يعدّ شتماً ، وجاء كسر النون أيضاً على صيغة اسم الفاعل ، وقيل : هو مرادف لمن يرتكب اللواطة.
[٢] قوله : يا خائن ؛ هو الذي يخون فيما يده من الأمانات.
(۳) قوله : يا لوطي ؛ قيل يسأل عنه، فإن أراد إنّه من قوم لوط على نبينا وعليه السلام لا يعزّر، وإن أراد أنه يعمل عملهم من الإتيان في الدبر فاعلاً أو مفعولاً عزر عنده، وعندهما: حدّ كما يحد مرتكب هذا الفعل عندهما، والصحيح تعزيره، ولو في غضب أو هزل. كذا في «الفتح».
[٤]قوله : يا زندیق ؛ - بكسر الزاي المعجمة وكسر الدال المهملة، بينهما نون ساكنة، وبعدهما ياء مثناة تحتيّة ساكنة، ثم قاف - ، فارسي معرب أصله : زنده أو زندي، والراجح هو الأول، وهو نسبة إلى زند ، وهو اسم كتاب أظهره مزدك، رئيس الفرقة المزدكية، من الكفار الثنوية القائلين بتعدد الآله في زمن كسرى قباذ فنسب إليه أصحابه، وقد قتله كسری نوشیروان
وما نقله المطرزي في «المغرب» (۲) : إنّ أصله زنده ؛ أي يقول بدوام الدهر، فمبني على عدم الفرق بين الدهري وبين الزنديق ، كذا حققه أحمد بن سليمان الرومي الشهير بابن كمال باشا ، مؤلّف الايضاح والاصلاح في رسالة ألفها في تحقيق معنى الزنديق.
ولما كان دين الزنادقة خارجاً عن الأديان السماوية كلها، سمّى العرب كلِّ مَن خرج عن الأديان السماوية زنديقاً، ويعبر عنه بالفارسية: بي دين.

كتاب الحدود فصل في التعزير
يا لص، يا ديوث يا قرطبان يا شارب الخمر يا آكل الربا، يا ابن القحبة ، يا
.
ابن الفاجرة، أنت تأوي اللصوص، أنت تأوي الزواني
[0] قوله : يا لص؛ - بكسر اللام وتشديد الصاد المهملة، وجاء فتح اللام أيضاً : السارق بين السارقية.
[٢] قوله : يا ديوث ؛ - بفتح الدال المهملة، وتشديد الياء المثناة التحتية المضمومة، ثمّ واو ساكنة، ثمّ تاء مثلثة - ، قال الزَّيْلَعِي : هو الذي يرى مع امرأته أو محرمه رجلاً فيدعه خالياً بها ، وقيل : هو المتسبب للجمع بين اثنين لمعنى غير ممدوح، وقيل : هو الذي يبعث امرأته مع غلام بالغ أو مع مزارعه إلى الضيعة، أو يأذن لهما
بالدخول عليها في غيبته.
[۳] قوله : يا قرطبان ؛ - بفتح القاف، وفتح الطاء المهملة، بينهما راء مهملة ساكنة - ، معرّب قلتبان، قيل : هو والديوث مترادفان ، وقيل : الديوث من لا غيرة له من يدخل على امرأته، والقرطبان هو الذي يعرف من يزني بامرأته ويسكت ، وقيل : هو الذي يدخلُ الرجل على امرأته ليصيب منه مالاً.
[٤]قوله : يا ابن القحبة ؛ أشار به إلى أنه إذا شتم أصله عُزر بطلب الولد، کیا ابن الفاسق ، يا ابن الكافر، ونحو ذلك ، وعلم منه أنه لو قال لامرأة : يا قحبة عزّر بالطريق الأولى. [0] قوله : أنت ؛ تغيير العنوان في هذين اللفظين مجرد تفنّن ، وإلا فالحكم لا يختلف في جميع الألفاظ الشتمية في أنه أبرزها بصورة الندا أو بصورة الخبر، سواء كان على طريقة الخطاب كانت كذا ، أو على طريقة الغيبوبة كفلان كذا ، أو هو كذا.
[٦] قوله : تأوي اللصوص ؛ وفي بعض الكتب مأوى اللصوص، ومأوى الزواني، وهو الذي تجتمع عنده السارقون والزناة ويختلطون معه اختلاط الأحباب.
O effeminate, O treacherous, O Lot-like, O heretic. He is punished with ta'zir. [1] His saying: 'O effeminate' – it is a passive participle from takhnith; it is said: khanathahu fa-takhanatha, meaning his nature inclined him. The nature of women in his movements, stillness, and speech. If that is innate and he is from them, then it is not blameworthy; but if he affects it, then it is considered an insult. The kasra of the nun also occurs in the form of the active participle, and it is said it is synonymous with one who commits sodomy. [2] His saying: 'O treacherous' – he is one who betrays the trusts in his hands. [3] His saying: 'O Lot-like' – it is said he is asked about it. If he means that he is from the people of Lot (upon our Prophet and him peace), he is not punished by ta'zir. If he means that he does their action, i.e., anal intercourse, as an active or passive partner, then in his (Abu Hanifa's) view, he is punished with ta'zir; in their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) view, he receives the hadd penalty as the one who commits this act receives. The correct view is that he is punished with ta'zir, even if in anger or jest. Thus in al-Fath. [4] His saying: 'O heretic' – with kasra of the zay and kasra of the dal, between them a sukun on the nun, then a sukun on the ya, then qaf. It is a Persian arabicized word; its origin is zinda or zandi, and the preferred is the first. It is a nisba to Zand, which is the name of a book brought forth by Mazdak, the leader of the Mazdakite sect, from the dualist unbelievers who claim multiple gods in the time of Kisra Qubadh, so his followers were attributed to it, and Kisra Nushirwan killed him. What al-Mutarrizi transmitted in al-Maghrib that its origin is zinda meaning he says with the perpetuity of time is based on not distinguishing between the materialist and the heretic. Thus verified by Ahmad ibn Sulayman al-Rumi known as Ibn Kamal Pasha, author of al-Idah and al-Islah, in a treatise he wrote on the meaning of heretic. Since the religion of the heretics is outside all heavenly religions, the Arabs called everyone outside the heavenly religions a heretic, and it is expressed in Persian as bi-din. Book of Hudud, Chapter on Ta'zir. O thief, O cuckold, O pimp, O wine-drinker, O usurer, O son of a whore, O son of a fornicator, you shelter thieves, you shelter adulteresses. [0] His saying: 'O thief' – with kasra of the lam and tashdid of the sad, and also with fatha of the lam: the thief known for thievery. [2] His saying: 'O cuckold' – with fatha of the dal, tashdid of the ya with dhamma, then waw with sukun, then ta with three dots. Al-Zayla'i said: He is one who sees a man with his wife or female relative and leaves him alone with her. And it is said: He is one who causes the gathering of two for a non-praiseworthy purpose. And it is said: He is one who sends his wife with a mature boy or with his sharecropper to the estate, or permits them to enter upon her in his absence. [3] His saying: 'O pimp' – with fatha of the qaf and fatha of the ta, between them a ra with sukun. It is arabicized from qaltaban. It is said that it and al-dayyuth are synonymous. And it is said that al-dayyuth is one who has no protective jealousy (ghayra) regarding who enters upon his wife, and al-qurtaban is one who knows who commits zina with his wife and remains silent. And it is said: He is one who brings a man to his wife to obtain wealth from him. [4] His saying: 'O son of a whore' – he indicated by it that if he insults his lineage, he is punished with ta'zir by way of seeking [retribution?] for the child, like 'O son of a transgressor,' 'O son of an unbeliever,' and similar. And it is understood from it that if he says to a woman: 'O whore,' he is punished with ta'zir even more so. [0] His saying: 'You' – changing the address in these two phrases is mere stylistic variation; otherwise the ruling does not differ in all insulting expressions: whether he presents them in the form of direct address or in the form of third person, whether it is in the manner of address like 'so-and-so,' or in the manner of absence like 'so-and-so [is] such,' or 'he is such.' [6] His saying: 'You shelter thieves' – and in some books: 'refuge of thieves' and 'refuge of adulteresses.' He is one with whom thieves and fornicators gather and mix like friends.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for various insulting names and phrases. The author defines each term and states the ruling. The general principle is that slandering or insulting someone with these specific words warrants ta'zir, not hadd, unless a specific hadd applies (as in qadhf). The commentary covers: (1) al-mukhannath (effeminate): If innate, not blameworthy; if affected, it's an insult. (2) al-kha'in (treacherous): one who betrays trusts. (3) al-luti (Lot-like): This term has an intra-Hanafi dispute. If meant as a reference to the Prophet Lot himself, no punishment. If meant as an accusation of sodomy, Abu Hanifa rules ta'zir, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule hadd (the fixed penalty for qadhf). The commentary declares the correct view (al-sahih) to be ta'zir, citing Fath al-Qadir. This applies even in anger or jest. (4) al-zindiq (heretic): a Persian term for those outside heavenly religions; insulting someone as such warrants ta'zir. (5) al-liss (thief): a known thief. (6) al-dayyuth (cuckold): one who condones his wife's illicit relations. (7) al-qurtaban (pimp): similar to dayyuth or one who profits from his wife's infidelity. (8) ibn al-qahba (son of a whore) and ibn al-fajira (son of a fornicator): insulting one's lineage incurs ta'zir, and if said to a woman directly, ta'zir is even more warranted. The commentary also notes that the form of address (direct 'you' vs. third person) does not change the ruling. Finally, 'you shelter thieves/adulteresses' applies to one who harbors criminals. The overall ruling is that all such insults are subject to ta'zir at the judge's discretion, except where a fixed hadd is proven (such as in the Luti case according to the Sahibayn, though the mufta bihi is ta'zir).

Key Terms
مخنث (mukhannath): An effeminate man, either naturally (not blameworthy) or affected (considered an insult).
خائن (kha'in): One who betrays trusts in his hands.
لوطي (luti): A person from the people of Lot (prophet) or one who commits sodomy; the intended meaning determines the ruling.
زندیق (zindiq): A heretic, one outside the heavenly religions; the term was used by Arabs for all non-monotheists.
ديوث (dayyuth): A cuckold; one who sees a man with his wife or female relative and leaves them alone, or lacks ghayra (protective jealousy).
قرطبان (qurtaban): A pimp; one who knows of his wife's adultery and remains silent, or brings men to her for money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling for calling someone 'يا لوطي' with an intent to accuse them of sodomy?
A) No punishment because the term is not an insult
B) Hadd of 80 lashes (qadhf) as per Sahibayn
C) Ta'zir as per Abu Hanifa
D) The offender must prove the accusation or face execution

Key Takeaway
Defamatory insults in Hanafi law generally incur ta'zir, but the term 'luti' has a settled intra-Hanafi dispute whose mufta bihi is also ta'zir.

Death from Lawful Hadd or Ta'zir: No Liability
Ruling on death from lawful punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that death resulting from a lawful hadd or ta'zir punishment incurs no financial liability.

Core Idea
Death from a lawful hadd or ta'zir is hâdir — no diya or qisas upon anyone.

This passage establishes a foundational principle: when a lawful punishment ordered by the imam results in death, the blood is shed with impunity. No compensation falls upon the imam, the executioner, or the one who ordered the punishment. This rule reflects the authority of the state in enforcing Islamic penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize that the ruling applies to lawful hadd (fixed penalty) or ta'zir (discretionary punishment) administered by the imam or his deputy.
The punishment must be legally valid.
2
State the rule
If the punished person dies, his blood is hâdir (shed with impunity). No diya, qisas, or any other compensation is due.
The Arabic term 'هدر' is key.
3
Identify the parties exempt from liability
No liability falls upon the imam, the executioner, or the person who ordered the ta'zir. All are exempt because the punishment was legally mandated.
This includes the entire chain of command.
4
Understand the basis
The imam's authority is comprehensive and serves the public interest. The punishment is a duty, so any resulting death is not wrongful.
Contrast with personal acts of discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن حُدّ أو عُزّر فمات هُدِرَ دَمُه. قوله : ومَن حُدٌ - بصيغة المجهول - أي من حده الإمام أو عزّرَه فمات قدمه هدر لا يجب به شيءٌ لا على الإمام، ولا على الجلاد، والمعزر بأمره. ولو عزر زوج عرسه لا. قوله : ولو عزّر قالوا: يعزّر المولى أمته، والزوج زوجته على تركها الزينة الشرعية، وتركها غسل الجنابة، وعلى الخروج من المنزل، وعلى ترك الإجابة إلى الفراش، وعلى ترك الصلاة، كما في «المجتبى»، وغيره. قوله : لا أي لا يكون دمها هدراً، بل تجب ديتها على الزوج لأنّ تأديبه مباح، فيتقيد بوصف السلامة، كذا في «منح الغفار».
And whoever is punished with hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is shed with impunity. His statement: 'And whoever is punished with hadd' — in passive form — i.e., whoever the imam punishes with hadd or ta'zir and dies due to his punishment, his blood is shed, and nothing is obligatory neither upon the imam nor upon the executioner nor upon the one ordering the ta'zir. And if a husband gives ta'zir to his wife, no. His statement: 'and if he gives ta'zir' — they said: the master gives ta'zir to his female slave, and the husband to his wife for abandoning prescribed adornment, for abandoning ghusl of janabah, for leaving the house, for refusing to come to bed, and for abandoning prayer, as in al-Mujtaba and others. His statement: 'no' — i.e., her blood is not shed with impunity, rather her diya is obligatory upon the husband because his discipline is permissible, so it is conditioned upon safety, as in 'Manh al-Ghaffar'.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents two legal rulings. First: If an imam (ruler) or his deputy administers a lawful hadd or ta'zir punishment, and the punished person dies as a result, the blood is hâdir (free from compensation). No diya or penalty falls upon the imam, the executioner, or the one who ordered the ta'zir. This is because the punishment is legally mandated. Second: Regarding a husband's ta'zir of his wife, the text says 'la' (no), meaning the same rule does not apply. The husband may discipline his wife by ta'zir for certain religious derelictions (like neglecting prayer, not performing ghusl, refusing intimacy, or leaving home without permission). However, his permission to discipline is qualified by the condition of safety. If his ta'zir causes her death, her blood is not hâdir; rather, he must pay diya (blood money) because his act, though permissible, must not exceed the bounds of safety. This distinction is based on the principle that the imam's authority is comprehensive and serves public interest, while the husband's is personal and limited.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Blood shed with impunity; no liability for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, what is the ruling if a person dies after receiving a lawful hadd punishment?
A) The imam must pay diya.
B) The executioner must pay diya.
C) The blood is hâdir (no compensation).
D) The family may demand qisas.

Key Takeaway
Lawful hadd/ta'zir death is hâdir; no compensation required.

Husband's Ta'zir Causing Death: Diya Obligatory
Ruling on husband's ta'zir causing death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a husband must pay diya if his ta'zir of his wife results in her death.

Core Idea
Husband's ta'zir is conditioned on safety; if death occurs, diya is obligatory because the condition was violated.

The second part of the passage addresses a specific exception: a husband may discipline his wife by ta'zir for certain religious lapses, but his permission is qualified. If his discipline causes her death, the blood is not hâdir; rather, he must pay diya (blood money). This highlights the principle that personal authority is limited by the condition of safety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor and permissible grounds
The husband may administer ta'zir for specific defaults: abandoning prescribed adornment, leaving ghusl of janabah, leaving the house without permission, refusing intimacy, and abandoning prayer. These are religious matters.
The list is from al-Mujtaba.
2
State the exception
The word 'la' (no) indicates that the ruling of impunity does not apply here. Her blood is not hâdir.
The response is negative to the first rule.
3
Apply the consequence
If the husband's ta'zir causes death, diya (blood money) is wajib upon him.
This is a financial obligation, not criminal retaliation.
4
Understand the condition
The permissibility of ta'zir is 'muqayyad bi-wasf al-salamah' — conditioned upon safety. If the act exceeds safety, it becomes an actionable wrong.
Safety is the limiting factor.

Liability for Death During Lawful Punishment: Hadd, Ta'zir, and Husband's Discipline
Liability rulings for death during judicial punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when death during punishment incurs liability (diyah) and when it does not, according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Lawful hadd/ta'zir by the imam — no liability; husband's discipline — diyah wajib.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains that when a person dies as a result of a lawful hadd or ta'zir punishment ordered by the imam, no blood money or retaliation is due—the blood is 'wasted.' However, the same does not apply when a husband disciplines his wife; his permission is conditional on safety, and if death occurs, he must pay diyah. Understanding this distinction clarifies the limits of authority in Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two situations: death during hadd/ta'zir by the imam, and death during a husband's discipline. List them separately.
These are the core legal events discussed.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For the imam's punishment: blood is wasted (no liability). For the husband: diyah is due. Note the difference and the legal basis.
The authority for each ruling is cited from al-Mujtaba and Manh al-Ghaffar.
3
Analyze the principle behind the difference
Why no liability for the imam? The punishment is lawful and mandated; the act is not forbidden. For the husband, his act is permissible but conditional on safety; thus if harm occurs, liability arises.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the limits of permissible discipline.
4
Apply to the terms of insult
Note that the listed insults (e.g., 'dog', 'pig') are not qadhf; they are ta'zir offenses. This sets the context for why ta'zir is applied before the death ruling.
The insults are examples of behavior that may warrant ta'zir.
5
Locate the authority
The passage cites al-Mujtaba for husband's discipline and Manh al-Ghaffar for the distinction. Identify the weight of these sources.
Authority_weight: zahir_riwayah for the imam's case; fiqh works confirm the husband's liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يا من يلعب بالصبيان، يا حرام زاده لا بیا ،حمار أو خنزير، يا كلب، یا تیس، یا قرد یا حجام، يا ابنه وأبوه ليس كذلك ، يا مؤاجر، یا بغا، یا ناکس، یا ضحكة ، يا سُخرة، ومَن حدّ أو عزّر فمات هُدِرَ دَمُه . ولو عَزْرَ زوج عرسه لا

[1] قوله : يا من يلعب بالصبيان؛ أي مع الأطفال، وهو يطلق عرفاً على من يأتي بالأطفال الفعل القبيح. [2]قوله : يا خنزير؛ - بكسر الخاء المعجمة، وكسر الزاي المعجمة، بينهما نون ساكنة، ثمّ ياء مثناة تحتيّة ساكنة ، ثم راء مهملة – ، بالفارسية : خوك.
[3] قوله : يا تيس؛ - بفتح التاء المثناة الفوقية، وسكون الياء المثناة التحتية، آخره سين مهملة - ، بالفارسية: بزد شتى، وهو معروف بكثرة النزول على الأنثى، يقال في المثل : هو أغلم من تيس.
[4]قوله : يا قرد؛ - بكسر القاف، وفتح الراء المهملة، آخره دال مهملة بالفارسية: بوزينه
[5]قوله : وأبوه؛ - الواو حالية - ، يعني قال لرجل: يا ابن الحجام، والحال : إن أباه ليس كذلك.
6 [6] قوله : ومَن حُدٌ؛ - بصيغة المجهول - ؛ أي من حده الإمام أو عزّرَه فمات قدمه هدر لا يجب به شيءٌ لا على الإمام، ولا على الجلاد، والمعزر بأمره. [7]قوله : ولو عزّر؛ قالوا: يعزّر المولى أمته، والزوج زوجته على تركها الزينة الشرعية، وتركها غسل الجنابة، وعلى الخروج من المنزل، وعلى ترك الإجابة إلى الفراش، وعلى ترك الصلاة، كما في «المجتبى»، وغيره. [8] قوله : لا؛ أي لا يكون دمها هدراً، بل تجب ديتها على الزوج؛ لأنّ تأديبه مباح، فيتقيد بوصف السلامة، كذا في «منح الغفار».
O you who plays with children, O illegitimate one, O donkey or pig, O dog, O he-goat, O monkey, O cupper, O son of (a cupper) while his father is not such, O hired, O bawd, O backward, O laughingstock, O mockery. And whoever is subjected to hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is wasted. And if a husband disciplines his wife, [it is] not [wasted]. [1] His saying: 'O you who plays with children' means with children, and it is customarily applied to one who does a reprehensible act with children. [2] His saying: 'O pig' - with kasrah on the kha and zay, sukun on the nun, then ya, then ra - in Persian: khuk. [3] His saying: 'O he-goat' - with fatha on the ta, sukun on the ya, ending with sin - in Persian: buz dashi, and it is known for frequently mounting the female; it is said in the proverb: He is more lustful than a he-goat. [4] His saying: 'O monkey' - with kasrah on the qaf, fatha on the ra, ending with dal - in Persian: buzineh. [5] His saying: 'And his father' - the waw is for the circumstantial clause - meaning he said to a man: O son of a cupper, while his father is not such. [6] His saying: 'And whoever is subjected to hadd' - in the passive voice - meaning whoever is subjected to hadd by the imam or ta'zir and dies, his blood is wasted; nothing is due, neither upon the imam nor upon the executioner nor upon the one who ordered the ta'zir. [7] His saying: 'And if he disciplines' - they said: The master disciplines his slave-woman, and the husband his wife, for abandoning lawful adornment, for abandoning ghusl of janabah, for leaving the house, for failing to respond to the bed, for abandoning prayer, as in al-Mujtaba and others. [8] His saying: 'Not' - meaning her blood is not wasted, but rather her blood money is obligatory upon the husband, because his disciplining is permissible, so it is restricted to the condition of safety, as in Manh al-Ghaffar.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah lists various insulting terms (sabb) in Persian and Arabic that are commonly used but do not constitute qadhf (slander of zina) because they are not explicit accusations of fornication. The terms include 'O you who plays with children' (implying pederasty), 'O illegitimate', 'O donkey', 'O pig', 'O dog', 'O he-goat', 'O monkey', 'O cupper' (a profession), and combinations. The commentary explains each term's meaning and cultural context. Then the ruling: if a person is subjected to the prescribed hadd punishment (such as flogging for zina or qadhf) or to discretionary ta'zir punishment, and he dies as a result, his blood is 'wasted' (hadar) — meaning no legal responsibility falls upon the imam, the executioner, or the one who ordered it; no blood money (diyah) or qisas is due. However, if a husband disciplines his wife (ta'zir) for legitimate reasons such as abandoning religious obligations, and she dies, then his disciplining is permissible but restricted to safety; if death occurs, the husband is liable for the full blood money (diyah) because his act, though allowed, must not result in harm. This distinction highlights that the husband's authority is not absolute and carries responsibility.

Key Terms
هدر الدم (hadar al-dam): Blood is considered wasted, meaning no blood money, qisas, or expiation due for the death.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for sins that have no fixed hadd, left to the judge's discretion.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina, which incurs a fixed hadd punishment of 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage
