Ruling on Insult by Calling Someone 'Qahba' and the Shubha al-Aqd Dispute
Analyzes whether calling someone 'qahba' constitutes qadhf and examines the shubha al-aqd dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether calling someone 'qahba' constitutes qadhf and evaluate the role of shubha al-aqd in the dispute.

Core Idea
Calling someone 'qahba' may not incur hadd due to shubha al-aqd, but later Hanafis favored hadd.

This passage delves into a nuanced fiqh problem: does insulting someone by calling them 'qahba' (a woman who commits zina openly for payment) trigger the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of zina)? The answer is not straightforward; it involves a deep intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab dispute centered on the concept of shubha al-aqd (legal doubt due to a contract). We will trace the arguments and see how later Hanafi authorities tipped the scale.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Imam Abu Hanifa holds regarding the hadd for the qahba herself and for the one who insults her as such.
Abu Hanifa drops the hadd for both due to shubha al-aqd.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Who disagrees with Abu Hanifa? State the view of the Sahibayn and the other three schools.
They argue that the ijarah contract for zina is void ab initio, so no shubha.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the evidence for the opposing view? Cite the analogy of hiring for cooking then committing zina.
The contract does not pertain to the act, so no doubt.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does Abu Hanifa rebut the analogy? Explain the concept of the benefit being the object of the ijarah.
The benefit of intercourse is considered an 'ayn, so the contract attaches to it, creating shubha.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence in the case of insult? Does the later tarjih change the ruling?
Later authorities like al-Ghazzi prefer hadd for the insult, making the mufta bihi different from Abu Hanifa's original view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : القحبة من يكون لها همة الزنا، فلا يحد.

أقول : القحبةُ في العرفِ أفحش من الزَّانية، لأن الزانية قد تفعل سراً [۱] قوله : فلا يحدّ ؛ لأنّ قصد الزنى أمر غير فعل الزنى، فلا يكون إطلاق القحبة قذفاً بالزني، فلا يحد. [٢]قوله : أقول ... الخ ؛ إيراد على ما ذكروه من عدم الحد بالشتم بالقحبة، وبابن القحبة، بأن القحبة أفحش من الزانية عرفاً، فإنّ الزانية قد ترتكب الزني سراً، ولا يطلق لفظ القحبة عليها، وإنّما يطلق على من ترتكبه جهراً وعلانية أو تأخذ عليه أجرة؛ ولذا ذكر في «الظهيرية» و «جامع المضمرات» وغيرها وجوب الحد بشتم القحبة ، واستظهره الغزي مؤلّف «تنوير الأبصار» في شرحه «منح الغفار»؛ وذلك لأنّ القحبة صار حقيقة عرفية بمعنى الزانية، فهو قذف بصريح الزني.

وأجيب عنه : بأن القحبة لما كانت تجاهرُ بالزنى بالأجرة سقط الحد عنها عند الإمام ؛ لوجود شبهة العقد، قال في «فتح القدير»: «ومن شبهة العقد ما إذا استأجرها ليزني بها، ففعل لا حدّ عليه، ويعزّر، وقالا : هما والشافعي ومالك وأحمد يحدّ ؛ لأنّ عقد الإجارة لا يستباحُ به البضع ، فصار كما لو استأجرها للطبخ ونحوه من الأعمال ثمّ زنى بها، فإنّه يحدّ اتفاقا.

وله : إنّ المستوفي بالزني المنفعة، وهي المعقود عليه بالإجارة، لكنّه في حكم العين فبالنظر إلى الحقيقة تكون محلاً لعقد الإجارة فأورث شبهة بخلاف الاستئجار للطبخ ونحوه ؛ لأنّ العقد لم يضف إلى المستوفى بالوطء، والعقد المضاف إلى محل يورث الشبهة فيه لا في محل آخر». (انتهى) ولَمّا سقط الحد عن القحبة بفعلها سقط الحدّ عمّن يشتم بهذا اللفظ ؛ لأنّ النسبة بفعل لا يوجب الحد لا يوجب حد القذف، وفي هذا الجواب نظر، فإنّ القحبة لا تلتزم عقد الإجارة التي يسقط به الحد عند الإمام.

[٣]قوله : القحبة ; هو مأخوذ من القحاب، وهو السفال، وكانت الزانية في العرب إذا مر بها رجل سفلت يقضي منها حاجته، فسميت الزانية لهذا قحبة.
It was said: The qahba is one who has the intention of zina, so he is not subjected to hadd. I say: The qahba in custom is more obscene than the zaniya, because the zaniya may do it secretly. [1] His saying: 'so he is not subjected to hadd' because intending zina is something other than the act of zina, so calling someone a qahba is not an accusation of zina, so no hadd. [2] His saying: 'I say...' is an objection to what they mentioned of no hadd for insulting with qahba and son of qahba, because the qahba is more obscene than the zaniya in custom, for the zaniya may commit zina secretly, and the term qahba is not applied to her; rather it is applied to one who commits it openly and publicly or takes payment for it. Therefore it is mentioned in al-Zahiriyya and Jami' al-Mudmarat and others that hadd is obligatory for insulting with qahba, and al-Ghazzi, author of Tanwir al-Absar, preferred it in his commentary Manh al-Ghaffar; because qahba has become a customary reality meaning zaniya, so it is slander with explicit zina. And it was answered: Because the qahba commits zina openly for payment, hadd is dropped from her according to Imam Abu Hanifa due to the existence of shubha al-'aqd. He said in Fath al-Qadir: 'Among the shubha of contract is the case where a man hires a woman to commit zina with her, and he does it; there is no hadd upon him, and he is to be ta'zir. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad say: he is to be hadd, because a contract of ijarah does not make sexual intercourse permissible, so it is like if he hired her for cooking and similar tasks and then committed zina with her; in that case he is to be hadd by agreement.' And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: The benefit obtained through zina is the benefit that is the object of the ijarah contract, but it is considered as an 'ayn (physical object); therefore, in reality it is a valid subject for ijarah, so it creates a shubha, unlike hiring for cooking etc., because the contract does not relate to the benefit obtained through intercourse. A contract related to a subject creates shubha in that subject, not in another. (End.) And since hadd is dropped from the qahba for her act, hadd is dropped from the one who insults with this word, because an imputation of an act that does not incur hadd does not incur hadd of qadhf. In this answer there is a problem, because the qahba does not commit to an ijarah contract that would drop the hadd according to Imam Abu Hanifa. [3] His saying: al-qahba is derived from qihab, which means lowliness; the zaniya among the Arabs, when a man passed by her she would lower herself to satisfy his need, so the zaniya was called qahba for this reason.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex issue of whether calling someone a 'qahba' (a woman who openly commits zina for payment) constitutes qadhf (false accusation of zina) incurring the hadd punishment. The text begins by stating a view that a qahba is simply one with the intention of zina, so calling her that is not an explicit accusation of the act. However, the commentator (the author of the sharh) objects, arguing that in custom, 'qahba' is more obscene than 'zaniya' because it implies open, public commission of zina often for payment. Some later Hanafi sources (al-Zahiriyya, Jami' al-Mudmarat) and al-Ghazzi in his commentary Manh al-Ghaffar hold that hadd is obligatory for such an insult because the term has become a customary synonym for 'zaniya'. The text then presents a counter-argument based on the concept of shubha al-'aqd (doubt due to contract). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a woman hires herself out for zina (or a man hires her), the hadd punishment for zina is dropped for both due to a semblance of a valid contract (ijarah). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the other three schools argue that such a contract does not make the sexual act permissible, so hadd remains. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the benefit obtained through zina is the object of the ijarah contract, even though it is legally invalid, but the mere attachment of the contract to the act creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd. Since no hadd is applied to the qahba herself for her act, the argument goes that insulting her (or her son) by that name should also not incur hadd, because the imputed act does not itself incur hadd. However, the text ends by pointing out a flaw: the qahba does not actually enter into an ijarah contract with every man she sleeps with; the shubha only applies in the specific case of an ijarah arrangement. Hence, for general insistence, hadd might still apply. The etymology of 'qahba' from 'qihab' (lowliness) is also given.

Key Terms
قحبة (qahba): A woman who commits zina openly and publicly, often for payment; derived from qihab meaning lowliness.
شبهة العقد (shubhat al-'aqd): A legal doubt created by the existence of a contract (even if invalid) that prevents the application of hudud punishments.
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract of hiring or leasing; in this context, hiring a woman for sexual intercourse, which is legally invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the hadd punishment dropped for a man who hires a woman to commit zina?
A) Because the contract of ijarah is valid for sexual intercourse.
B) Because the benefit obtained through zina is the object of the ijarah contract, creating a legal doubt (shubha).
C) Because the man did not actually commit zina.
D) Because the woman consented.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on insulting someone as 'qahba' is not settled; it depends on the shubha al-aqd analysis and the customary usage of the term.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

Hanafi Ruling on Insults in Ta'zir: When Is a Word Qadhf?
Analysis of insult terms and whether they constitute qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when an insult term constitutes qadhf, and apply the principle of shubha (doubt) to determine whether hadd is imposed or ta'zir is given.

Core Idea
Not every insult about sexual immorality is qadhf; the term must explicitly mean zina in custom, otherwise only ta'zir applies.

This passage from the chapter on Ta'zir examines four insulting terms: qahbah, fajirah, haramzadeh, and mu'ajjir. The central question is whether using these words constitutes qadhf (false accusation of zina) requiring the hadd penalty of eighty lashes. The Hanafi school distinguishes between explicit accusations and ambiguous terms, often dropping the hadd due to doubt (shubha) or because the term's customary meaning does not precisely indicate zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify each insult term and its literal/customary meaning
List the four terms: qahbah, fajirah, haramzadeh, mu'ajjir. Understand their linguistic origins and how they are used in common custom ('urf).
Custom is key to determining whether a word is explicit (sarih) or ambiguous.
2
Recall the definition of qadhf in Hanafi fiqh
Qadhf requires an explicit accusation of zina, either by using the word 'zina' or a clear equivalent. If the term is general or can be interpreted otherwise, it is not qadhf.
This is based on the principle that hudud penalties are averted by shubuhat (doubts).
3
Apply the principle to each term
For qahbah: it is more explicit than zaniyah, but because the woman may have a contractual doubt (shubhat al-aqd), the hadd is dropped. For fajirah: it means any sin, so no hadd. For haramzadeh: it is ambiguous (can mean child of zina or cheat), so hadd is dropped. For mu'ajjir: it does not imply zina at all, so no hadd.
The proof for qahbah involves a special Hanafi argument based on doubt.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that hiring a woman for zina creates a doubt (shubhat al-aqd) that drops the hadd from both parties. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and other schools disagree. Understand the reasoning on both sides.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling) and its practical application
The relied-upon position is that none of these terms incur hadd; the offender is given ta'zir (discretionary punishment) at the judge's discretion. This is based on the principle of avoiding hudud in cases of doubt.
Ta'zir can include a reprimand, fine, or lashing up to 39 lashes, depending on the severity.

I say: Al-qahbah in custom is more obscene than the adulteress, because the adulteress may do it secretly. [1] His saying: 'So he is not hadded' because the intention of zina is something other than the act of zina, so calling someone qahbah is not a slander of zina, so he is not hadded. [2] His saying: 'I say... etc.' is an objection to what they mentioned of no hadd for insulting with qahbah and ibn al-qahbah, because qahbah is more obscene than the adulteress in custom, for the adulteress may commit zina secretly, and the word qahbah is not applied to her; rather it is applied to one who commits it openly and publicly or takes payment for it; hence it is mentioned in al-Zahiriyyah and Jami' al-Mudmarat and others the obligation of hadd for insulting with qahbah, and al-Ghazzi, author of Tanwir al-Absar, adopted it in his commentary Manh al-Ghaffar; because qahbah has become a customary reality meaning [the adulteress], so it is a slander with explicit zina. And it was answered: Because qahbah openly commits zina for payment, so the hadd is dropped from her according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] due to the existence of a contractual doubt (shubhat al-aqd). He said in Fath al-Qadir: 'Among the contractual doubts is if one hires her to commit zina with her, and he does so, there is no hadd upon him, and he is ta'zired. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] as well as al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad say: He is hadded; because a rental contract does not make the private part permissible, so it is like if he hired her for cooking and similar tasks then committed zina with her, in which case he is hadded by agreement. And his [Abu Hanifa's] proof: The benefit obtained through zina is the benefit which was contracted upon by the rental, but it is in the category of a thing; so from the perspective of reality, it is a subject for the rental contract, thus it creates a doubt, contrary to hiring for cooking etc., because the contract was not attached to what is obtained through intercourse, and the contract attached to a subject creates doubt in it, not in another subject.' (End) And since the hadd is dropped from the qahbah due to her act, the hadd is dropped from the one who insults with this word; because the attribution to an act that does not necessitate hadd does not necessitate the hadd of slander. But there is consideration in this answer, because the qahbah does not commit to the rental contract that drops the hadd according to Imam. [3] His saying: al-qahbah; it is derived from al-qihab, which is baseness. And the adulteress among the Arabs, when a man passed by her, she would lower herself to fulfill his need, so the adulteress was called qahbah for this. And she feels shame from it, and qahbah is one who openly commits it for payment. And al-fajirah: she is involved in every sin, so no hadd by it. [3] The word haramzadeh: its meaning is the one born from forbidden intercourse, and it is more general than zina (like intercourse during menstruation), but in custom it is not meant by that; rather it means the child of zina, and often it is meant with al-jarbaz al-hubb (the mean cheat), so for this reason hadd is not obligatory. (1) And al-mu'ajjir: it is used for one who hires out his wife for zina, but its real customary meaning does not indicate zina; it is said: ajarat al-ajir mu'ajjara, when you assign him a payment for his act. [1] His saying: and al-fajirah; an explanation of the non-obligation of hadd for insulting with fajirah and ibn al-fajirah; its summary: al-fajirah is involved in every sin, for fujur means disobedience, and it is not specific to zina, so insulting with it is not slander of zina. [2] His saying: and it is more general than zina; for zina is more specific than forbidden intercourse, because intercourse with one's wife during menstruation and postpartum is forbidden but not zina, so this statement is not slander of zina, so he is not hadded by it. [3] His saying: but in custom; he indicated by it that one should be hadded for insulting with this word, because they use it for the child of zina, but since they often utter this word and mean by it the mean cheat, therefore he is not hadded by it. [4] His saying: al-jarbaz; with dammah on the jim, sukun on the ra', dammah on the ba', ending with a zay, it is al-khibb: with kasr on the kha', tashdid on the ba', it is the mean cheat. [0] His saying: but its real meaning; an explanation of the non-obligation of hadd by saying 'O mu'ajjir'. [6] His saying: 'when you assign' (idhā ja‘alta) is in the second person, and it is connected to 'it is said', and his saying 'ajartu' is in the first person. (1) al-jarbaz al-hubb: the mean cheat. See: al-'Umdah (2:311).
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary on the chapter of Ta'zir (discretionary punishment) within the Book of Hudud. The main discussion is whether certain insulting terms constitute qadhf (false accusation of zina) that incurs the hadd penalty of eighty lashes, or whether they fall under ta'zir (discretionary punishment) because they do not explicitly accuse of zina.

The commentary begins by addressing the term 'qahbah' (prostitute). The initial statement ('qīla') says that a qahbah is one who has the intention of zina, so the one who calls her that is not hadded. The commentator ('I say') then clarifies that in common custom ('urf), 'qahbah' is more obscene than 'zāniyah' (adulteress) because the latter may commit zina secretly, while 'qahbah' typically implies open commission or taking payment. An objection is raised: since 'qahbah' is more explicit in custom, it should be considered qadhf of zina, and thus hadd should apply. This view is supported by some Hanafi works like al-Zahiriyyah and Jami' al-Mudmarat, and adopted by al-Ghazzi.

The response to this objection is based on Imam Abu Hanifa's position: the hadd is dropped from the qahbah herself due to the existence of a 'shubhat al-'aqd' (contractual doubt). The commentary cites Fath al-Qadir, which explains that if a man hires a woman for zina and does it, there is no hadd upon him according to Abu Hanifa, because the rental contract creates a doubt (since the benefit obtained is the subject of the contract). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), along with al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, hold that hadd is obligatory because a rental contract cannot make the private part permissible. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the benefit obtained through zina is the same benefit contracted upon (satisfaction of desire), even if the act is sinful, so a doubt arises. Consequently, since the qahbah herself is not hadded due to her act, the one who calls her 'qahbah' is also not hadded, because the attribution is to an act that does not incur hadd. However, the commentator notes a problem: the qahbah does not necessarily enter into a rental contract that would drop the hadd according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Next, the term 'fajirah' (sinner/fornicatress) is discussed. The commentary states that 'fajirah' refers to any sin (fujur = fusuq), not just zina, so insulting with it is not qadhf of zina, and no hadd is imposed; rather ta'zir applies.

Then the term 'haramzadeh' (illegitimate child) is analyzed. Literally it means 'born from forbidden intercourse', which is more general than zina (e.g., intercourse during menstruation is forbidden but not zina). However, in custom it is often used to mean a child of zina, but also frequently used to mean 'mean cheat' (al-jarbaz al-hubb). Because of this dual usage, hadd is not obligatory, as it cannot be definitively established that the accuser meant zina.

Finally, the term 'mu'ajjir' (one who hires out his wife for zina) is examined. Its real, customary meaning does not indicate zina; it simply means one who hires a worker. Therefore, saying 'O mu'ajjir' is not qadhf, so no hadd.

Throughout, the Hanafi position is that hadd is only imposed for explicit slander of zina ('sarih al-qadhf') or for clear implication that custom has made equivalent. Otherwise, ta'zir is given at the judge's discretion. The key proof is that 'urf' (custom) determines the meaning of words, and where there is ambiguity, the stricter penalty (hadd) is avoided.

Key Terms
القحبة (al-qahbah): In custom, a woman who openly commits zina, often for payment; derived from 'qihab' meaning baseness.
الفاجرة (al-fajirah): A woman who commits any sin (fujur), not specifically zina.
حرام زاده (haramzadeh): Literally 'born from forbidden intercourse'; more general than zina, but in custom often used for child of zina or for a mean cheat.
المؤاجر (al-mu'ajjir): One who hires out someone; its real meaning does not imply zina, so not qadhf.
شبهة العقد (shubhat al-'aqd): A doubt arising from a contract that resembles a valid basis for sexual intercourse, even if the act is invalid, thus averting the hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the hadd of qadhf not imposed on someone who says 'O fajirah' (يا فاجرة) to a woman?
A) Because 'fajirah' is considered a customary equivalent of 'zaniyah' and thus constitutes sarih al-qadhf.
B) Because 'fujur' means any sin, not specifically zina, so the insult is not a clear accusation of zina.
C) Because the woman is not a Muslim.
D) Because the insult was said in anger.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts qadhf to explicit accusations of zina; ambiguous terms or those affected by doubt only incur ta'zir, not hadd.

The Dabita: The General Rule for Qadhf
Establishing a general rule for classifying insults

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule (dabita) for distinguishing between hadd of qadhf and ta'zir, and apply it to new insult scenarios.

Core Idea
Only attributing zina to a muhsan triggers hadd; all other insults fall under ta'zir.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya establishes a fundamental general rule (dabita) that governs all insults. Without this rule, one might mistakenly think that any insult implying immorality triggers the fixed punishment of qadhf. However, the Hanafi approach is precise: only when zina is directly attributed to a muhsan (free, adult, Muslim, chaste) does the hadd apply. Everything else is subject to the judge's discretion (ta'zir). This lesson unpacks that rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal context
Understand that the passage discusses insults (shatm) and their legal consequences: hadd (fixed punishment) or ta'zir (discretionary).
2
Extract the dabita
Locate the sentence: 'قد عرفت أن نسبة المحصن إلى الزنا توجب حد القذف، فنسبة غير المحصن إليه لا توجب الحد' – This is the general rule.
3
Define 'muhsan' and 'non-muhsan'
Muhsan means free, adult, Muslim, and not previously convicted of zina. Non-muhsan includes slaves, disbelievers, minors, etc.
Hanafi definition
4
Apply the dabita to examples
Notice that words like 'fajira', 'haram-zada', 'muajir' do not trigger hadd because they either don't specifically accuse zina or the target is not muhsan.
Check each term
5
Understand the role of urf
Custom (urf) can affect the meaning. For example, 'haram-zada' often means 'deceitful', not literally 'child of zina', so no hadd.
Hanafi use of urf

وتأنف منه ، والقحبة من تجاهرُ به بالأجرة. والفاجرة تكون بكل معصية فلا حد به.
[٣] لفظ ؛ حرام زاده ؛ معناه المتولّد من الوطء الحرام، وهو أعم من الزنا") كالواط حالة الحيض، لكن في العرف لا يراد ذلك، بل يراد ولد الزنا، وكثيراً ما يراد به بالجربز الحب ، فلهذا لا يجب الحد. (١) والمؤاخِرُ : يستعمل فيمَن يؤاجِرُ أهله للزِّنا ، لكن معناه الحقيقي المتعارف ، لا يؤذن بالزنا ، يقال : أجرتُ الأجير مؤاجرة ، إذا جعلت له على فعله أجرة.
[١] قوله : والفاجرة ؛ بيان لعدم وجوب الحد بالشتم بالفاجرة وابن الفاجرة ، وحاصله : إنّ الفاجرة تكون بكل معصية، فإنّ الفجور بمعنى الفسق، ولا يختص هو بالزني، فلا يكون الشتم به قذفاً بالزني.
[٢] قوله : وهو أعم من الزنا ؛ فإنّ الزنا أخص من الوطء الحرام، فإنّ وطء الزوجة حالة الحيض والنفاس حرام وليس بزني، فلا يكون هذا القول قذفاً بالزني فلا يحد به.
[٣] قوله : لكن في العرف ؛ أشار به إلى أنه ينبغي أن يحد بالشتم بهذا اللفظ ؛ لأنهم يستعملونه في ولد الزنى، لكن لَمّا كان كثيراً ما يطلقون هذا اللفظ ويريدون به الخداع اللئيم، فلذا لا يحد به.
[٤] قوله : الجربز ؛ - بضم الجيم ، وسكون الراء المهملة، وضم الباء الموحدة، آخره زاي معجمة - ، هو الخِب : وهو - بكسر الخاء المعجمة، وتشديد الباء الموحدة – : الخداع اللئيم.
[٠] قوله : لكن معناه الحقيقي ؛ بيان لعدم وجوب الحد بقوله : يا مؤاجر. [٦] قوله : إذا جعلت ؛ بصيغة الخطاب، وهو متعلّق بقوله : «يقال»، وقوله : آجرت» بصيغة المتكلم.
(۱) الجربز الحب : الخداع اللئيم. ينظر: «العمدة»(۲ : ۳۱۱).

ولفظ بغا : من شتم العوام ، يتفوهون به، ولا يعرفون ما يقولون. والضحكة بوزن الصفرةِ : مَن يَضْحَكَ عليه النَّاس، وبوزن الهمزة : مَن يضحك على الناس ، وكذا السُّخْرَةُ ونحوه. واعلم أن الألفاظ الدالة على القبائح لا تعد ولا تحصى، فالواجب أن يُذْكَرَ لما ضابطة يعرف به أحكام جميعها . فأقول : قد عرفت أن نسبة المحصن إلى الزنا توجب حد القذف، فنسبة غير المحصن ) كالعبد والكافر إليه لا توجب الحد ؛ لانحطاط درجتهما
[١] قوله : «بَعا» ؛ - بفتح الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الغين المعجمة – ، ومثله : به علة الابنة، وهو باغا ، وكأنه انتزع من البغاء، كذا في «المغرب»). [٢] قوله : من شتم العوام ؛ قيل : هو بمعنى المابون ؛ أي من به . الذي لا يقدر على ترك أن يؤتى في دُبُره لدودة ونحوها. كذا في «المغرب». قال في «البحر»: «وينبغي أن يجب التعزير فيه اتفاقا ؛ لأنه ألحق الشين به لعدم ظهور الكذب فيه، وصرّح في «الظهيرية» لوجوب التعزير في : يا مَعْفُوج ، وهو المأتي في الدبر"، وفي «النهر»: إنّه يعزّر في عرفنا في يا مؤاجر وفي : ولد الحرام، وفي : بغا.
[٣] قوله : والضحكة ؛ - بضم الضاد المعجمة، وسكون الحاء المهملة - [٤] قوله : السُّخرة ؛ بضم السين المهملة، وسكون الخاء المعجمة. [٠] قوله : ضابطة ؛ أي قاعدة كليّة ينضبط بها أحكام الجزئيات، ومثل هذه الضابطة يسمى قانوناً وقاعدة. [٦]قوله : يعرف به ؛ الضمير راجع إلى «الضابطة»، وتذكيره باعتبار المذكور، وفي بعض النسخ : ضابط ، فلا حاجة إلى التأويل. [۷]قوله : «نسبة المحصن» ؛ إضافة النسبة إلى المحصن نسبة إلى المفعول. [۸]قوله : فنسبة غير المحصن ؛ أشار به إلى أنَّ ذكر المملوك والكافر في المتن اتفاقي، فإنّ الحكم في كلّ غير محصن كالمملوك والكافر والمجنون والصبي وغير العفيف عن الزنى كذلك.
And it is avoided (the word qahba? to be explored), and al-qahba is the one who openly commits (zina) for payment. And al-fajira commits every sin, so no hadd is due for it. [3] The word 'haram-zada': its meaning is the one born from unlawful intercourse, which is more general than zina (like intercourse during menstruation), but in custom (urf) it is not intended that, rather it intends the child of zina, and frequently it is intended to mean deceitful cunning, so for that the hadd is not required. (1) And al-muajir: is used for one who hires out his wife for zina, but its real meaning is the common (meaning), it does not imply zina. It is said: 'I hired an employee, hiring him out', when you assign to him a fee for his action. [1] His statement: 'and al-fajira'; explanation of the non-necessity of hadd for insulting with 'fajira' and 'son of fajira', and its summary: al-fajira commits every sin, for fujur means fisq (sin), and it is not specific to zina, so insulting with it is not qadhf of zina. [2] His statement: 'and it is more general than zina'; for zina is more specific than unlawful intercourse, for a husband's intercourse during menstruation and postpartum is unlawful and is not zina, so that saying is not qadhf of zina, so hadd is not required. [3] His statement: 'but in custom'; he indicated by it that it is appropriate to impose hadd for insulting with this word, because they use it for the child of zina, but since they frequently use this word intending deceitful cunning, therefore hadd is not required. [4] His statement: 'al-jarbaz'; with dammah on jim, sukun on ra', dammah on ba', ending with zay, it is al-khib: which is with kasr on kha', tashdid on ba', meaning: deceitful cunning. [0] His statement: 'but its real meaning'; explanation of the non-necessity of hadd for saying 'O muajir'. [6] His statement: 'when you assign'; in the second person, it is attached to his statement 'it is said', and his statement 'I hired' in the first person. (1) al-jarbaz al-khib: deceitful cunning. Refer to: 'al-Umda' (2:311). And the word 'bagha': is from the insults of common people, they blurt it out, and they do not know what they say. And al-dahaka (with the pattern of sufrah): the one at whom people laugh, and with the pattern of humzah: the one who makes people laugh. Likewise al-sukhrah and similar words. Know that the words indicating ugly things are countless, so it is necessary to mention for them a general rule (dabita) by which all their rulings can be known. So I say: you have understood that attributing zina to a muhsan necessitates the hadd of qadhf, so attributing it to a non-muhsan (like a slave or a kafir) does not necessitate the hadd, due to the inferiority of their status. [1] His statement: 'bagha'; with fath on ba', tashdid on ghayn, and similarly: 'bi-'illat al-ibnah', it is 'bagha', and it seems derived from al-bigha', as in 'al-Mughrib'. [2] His statement: 'from the insults of the common people'; it is said it means al-mabun: i.e., the one who has a condition (like a worm) that prevents him from refraining from being penetrated in his anus. As in 'al-Mughrib'. In 'al-Bahr': 'It is appropriate that ta'zir is obligatory in it unanimously, because it attaches disgrace to him due to lack of apparent falsity in it.' And in 'al-Zahiriyya', ta'zir is obligatory for 'ya ma'fuj' (the one who is penetrated in the anus). And in 'al-Nahr': 'Ta'zir is applied in our custom for 'ya muajir', 'walad al-haram', and 'bagha'. [3] His statement: 'and al-dahaka'; with damm on dad, sukun on ha'. [4] His statement: 'al-sukhrah'; with damm on sin, sukun on kha'. [0] His statement: 'a dabita'; i.e., a universal rule by which the rulings of particulars can be known. Such a dabita is called 'qanun' and 'qa'ida'. [6] His statement: 'by which it can be known'; the pronoun refers to 'al-dabita', and its masculine form is by considering the mentioned word; in some copies it is 'dabita' (without ta' marbuta), so no need for interpretation. [7] His statement: 'attributing to the muhsan'; the idafa of 'attributing' to 'muhsan' is an idafa of object to agent. [8] His statement: 'so attributing to a non-muhsan'; he indicated by it that the mention of slave and kafir in the text is coincidental, for the ruling for every non-muhsan like slave, kafir, insane, minor, and the unchaste from zina is likewise.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary explaining the rulings on various insulting words in the context of qadhf (false accusation of zina) and ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The author first examines specific terms: 'qahba' (open prostitute), 'fajira' (sinner, not specific to zina), 'haram-zada' (child of unlawful intercourse, broader than zina, but custom often means deceit, so no hadd), 'muajir' (one who hires out his wife for zina, but its literal meaning does not imply zina). Then he discusses 'bagha' (a common insult), 'dahaka' (laughingstock), and 'sukhrah' (mockery). The key point is that these words do not automatically trigger hadd because they may not directly accuse someone of zina, or they are used in a general sense. The passage concludes by establishing a general rule (dabita): attributing zina to a muhsan (a free, adult, Muslim of chaste behavior) requires hadd; attributing to a non-muhsan (slave, kafir, etc.) does not require hadd but may require ta'zir. This shows Hanafi attention to linguistic precision, custom (urf), and the specific legal status of the accused.

Key Terms
محصن (muhsan): A free, adult Muslim of chaste behavior who has not been accused of zina before; in qadhf context, the victim must be muhsan for the hadd to apply.
قذف (qadhf): The legal offense of accusing a muhsan person of zina without proof, which carries a fixed hadd punishment (80 lashes).
تعزیر (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment left to the judge's discretion for offenses that do not have fixed hadd or kaffarah.
ضابطة (dabita): A general rule or principle that governs a set of particular cases; here it is the principle that qadhf applies only to muhsan.
عرف (urf): Customary usage of language; in this passage, urf determines the meaning of insulting words and whether they amount to qadhf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dabita in the passage, what is the minimum condition for hadd of qadhf to apply?
A) The victim must be a free adult Muslim.
B) The victim must be a free adult Muslim who has never committed zina (muhsan).
C) The victim must be Muslim.
D) The victim must never have been accused of zina before.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dabita is clear: hadd of qadhf only applies when zina is attributed to a muhsan; all other insults fall under ta'zir.

Insulting Words: Linguistic Analysis and Urf
Detailed linguistic analysis of individual insulting terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why specific insulting words like 'fajira', 'haram-zada', and 'muajir' do not trigger hadd, and identify how customary usage (urf) influences the legal classification.

Core Idea
Custom and precise linguistic meaning determine whether an insult implies zina; general or figuratively used words do not trigger qadhf.

In the previous lesson, we learned the general rule. Now we dive into the detailed examples the author provides. Each word is carefully examined: its literal meaning, its customary usage, and its legal consequence. The Hanafi school shows meticulous attention to language and social context. This lesson walks through the main terms from the passage and their rulings.

Guided Path
1
List the key insulting words
Identify the words discussed: 'fajira', 'haram-zada', 'muajir', 'bagha', 'dahaka', 'sukhrah'.
2
For each word, separate literal and customary meaning
Read the commentary to see if the word specifically accuses zina or is general. For example, 'fajira' means any sin, not specific to zina.
Pay attention to the commentary brackets.
3
Determine the ruling from the text
Note whether the author says hadd is required or not. For 'fajira', no hadd. For 'haram-zada', no hadd due to custom. For 'muajir', no hadd (but ta'zir in custom). For 'bagha', ta'zir.
4
Understand the role of urf
Custom can change the meaning. 'Haram-zada' is often used to mean 'deceitful', not literally 'child of zina', so hadd is not required. Similarly, 'muajir' is not used to imply hiring for zina in common speech.
Hanafi rely on urf to interpret ambiguous words.
5
Apply the dabita to these terms
Even if a term could imply zina, if it is not specific or the target is not muhsan, hadd is avoided. The dabita provides a safety net.

[1] His statement: 'and al-fajira'; explanation of the non-necessity of hadd for insulting with 'fajira' and 'son of fajira', and its summary: al-fajira commits every sin, for fujur means fisq (sin), and it is not specific to zina, so insulting with it is not qadhf of zina. [2] His statement: 'and it is more general than zina'; for zina is more specific than unlawful intercourse, for a husband's intercourse during menstruation and postpartum is unlawful and is not zina, so that saying is not qadhf of zina, so hadd is not required. [3] His statement: 'but in custom'; he indicated by it that it is appropriate to impose hadd for insulting with this word, because they use it for the child of zina, but since they frequently use this word intending deceitful cunning, therefore hadd is not required. [4] His statement: 'al-jarbaz'; with dammah on jim, sukun on ra', dammah on ba', ending with zay, it is al-khib: which is with kasr on kha', tashdid on ba', meaning: deceitful cunning. [0] His statement: 'but its real meaning'; explanation of the non-necessity of hadd for saying 'O muajir'. [6] His statement: 'when you assign'; in the second person, it is attached to his statement 'it is said', and his statement 'I hired' in the first person. (1) al-jarbaz al-khib: deceitful cunning. Refer to: 'al-Umda' (2:311). And the word 'bagha': is from the insults of common people, they blurt it out, and they do not know what they say. And al-dahaka (with the pattern of sufrah): the one at whom people laugh, and with the pattern of humzah: the one who makes people laugh. Likewise al-sukhrah and similar words. Know that the words indicating ugly things are countless, so it is necessary to mention for them a general rule (dabita) by which all their rulings can be known. So I say: you have understood that attributing zina to a muhsan necessitates the hadd of qadhf, so attributing it to a non-muhsan (like a slave or a kafir) does not necessitate the hadd, due to the inferiority of their status. [1] His statement: 'bagha'; with fath on ba', tashdid on ghayn, and similarly: 'bi-'illat al-ibnah', it is 'bagha', and it seems derived from al-bigha', as in 'al-Mughrib'. [2] His statement: 'from the insults of the common people'; it is said it means al-mabun: i.e., the one who has a condition (like a worm) that prevents him from refraining from being penetrated in his anus. As in 'al-Mughrib'. In 'al-Bahr': 'It is appropriate that ta'zir is obligatory in it unanimously, because it attaches disgrace to him due to lack of apparent falsity in it.' And in 'al-Zahiriyya', ta'zir is obligatory for 'ya ma'fuj' (the one who is penetrated in the anus). And in 'al-Nahr': 'Ta'zir is applied in our custom for 'ya muajir', 'walad al-haram', and 'bagha'. [3] His statement: 'and al-dahaka'; with damm on dad, sukun on ha'. [4] His statement: 'al-sukhrah'; with damm on sin, sukun on kha'. [0] His statement: 'a dabita'; i.e., a universal rule by which the rulings of particulars can be known. Such a dabita is called 'qanun' and 'qa'ida'. [6] His statement: 'by which it can be known'; the pronoun refers to 'al-dabita', and its masculine form is by considering the mentioned word; in some copies it is 'dabita' (without ta' marbuta), so no need for interpretation. [7] His statement: 'attributing to the muhsan'; the idafa of 'attributing' to 'muhsan' is an idafa of object to agent. [8] His statement: 'so attributing to a non-muhsan'; he indicated by it that the mention of slave and kafir in the text is coincidental, for the ruling for every non-muhsan like slave, kafir, insane, minor, and the unchaste from zina is likewise.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary explaining the rulings on various insulting words in the context of qadhf (false accusation of zina) and ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The author first examines specific terms: 'qahba' (open prostitute), 'fajira' (sinner, not specific to zina), 'haram-zada' (child of unlawful intercourse, broader than zina, but custom often means deceit, so no hadd), 'muajir' (one who hires out his wife for zina, but its literal meaning does not imply zina). Then he discusses 'bagha' (a common insult), 'dahaka' (laughingstock), and 'sukhrah' (mockery). The key point is that these words do not automatically trigger hadd because they may not directly accuse someone of zina, or they are used in a general sense. The passage concludes by establishing a general rule (dabita): attributing zina to a muhsan (a free, adult, Muslim of chaste behavior) requires hadd; attributing to a non-muhsan (slave, kafir, etc.) does not require hadd but may require ta'zir. This shows Hanafi attention to linguistic precision, custom (urf), and the specific legal status of the accused.

Key Terms
محصن (muhsan): A free, adult Muslim of chaste behavior who has not been accused of zina before; in qadhf context, the victim must be muhsan for the hadd to apply.
قذف (qadhf): The legal offense of accusing a muhsan person of zina without proof, which carries a fixed hadd punishment (80 lashes).
تعزیر (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment left to the judge's discretion for offenses that do not have fixed hadd or kaffarah.
ضابطة (dabita): A general rule or principle that governs a set of particular cases; here it is the principle that qadhf applies only to muhsan.
عرف (urf): Customary usage of language; in this passage, urf determines the meaning of insulting words and whether they amount to qadhf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the word 'haram-zada' not require the hadd of qadhf according to the passage?
A) Because it is not an insult.
B) Because its literal meaning is not limited to zina and custom often uses it to mean 'deceitful'.
C) Because the target is not specified.
D) Because the accuser must provide four witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school analyzes each insult based on its literal meaning and customary usage, avoiding application of hadd unless the word clearly and specifically accuses zina against a muhsan person.

Ta'zir for Slander and Insults: The Legal Principle (Dabitah)
Defines insulting terms and establishes a legal principle for ta'zir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle (dabitah) to classify accusations as either hadd of qadhf or ta'zir, and identify the ruling for various insulting terms.

Core Idea
Accusing a non-muhsan of zina or a muhsan of any prohibited act incurs ta'zir, not hadd.

This lesson unpacks a key passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah that defines common insulting terms and sets forth a general legal principle (dabitah) for ta'zir. The principle streamlines the countless obscene words into a single rule: any accusation of a prohibited voluntary act against a person who is not eligible for hadd of qadhf (i.e., non-muhsan) or any accusation of a non-zina prohibited act against a muhsan, incurs discretionary punishment. You will learn to navigate the boundary between fixed hadd and discretionary ta'zir in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define the insulting terms
Identify the meanings of 'bagha', 'dahka', and 'sukhrah' as given in the passage. These are examples of insults used among people.
The different vowelings of 'dahka' change whether the person is mocked or mocks others.
2
Understand the need for a principle
Recognize that obscene words are countless, so a comprehensive rule (dabitah) is necessary to cover them all without enumerating every term.
The dabitah is a general principle that governs all specific cases.
3
Apply the principle to accusations of zina
Distinguish: accusing a muhsan of zina incurs hadd of qadhf (80 lashes). Accusing a non-muhsan (e.g., slave, disbeliever) of zina does not incur hadd but incurs ta'zir because of spreading immorality.
Non-muhsan includes anyone not free, adult, Muslim, chaste, and married.
4
Extend to other prohibited acts
Accusing a muhsan of a voluntary prohibited act other than zina (e.g., theft, hypocrisy) also incurs ta'zir, not hadd. The passage lists many examples such as 'hypocrite', 'innovator', 'Wahhabi', etc.
The prohibited act must be voluntary and sinful in Shari'ah.
5
Note the maximum ta'zir and exceptions
The maximum ta'zir is 39 lashes (less than the minimum hadd of 40). In some cases like sodomy (bagha), the ta'zir is more severe.
The judge has discretion within these limits.

Source Text

كتاب الحدود فصل في التعزير
ولفظ بغا : من شتم العوام ، يتفوهون به، ولا يعرفون ما يقولون. والضحكة بوزن الصفرةِ : مَن يَضْحَكَ عليه النَّاس، وبوزن الهمزة : مَن يضحك على الناس ، وكذا السُّخْرَةُ ونحوه.
واعلم أن الألفاظ الدالة على القبائح لا تعد ولا تحصى، فالواجب أن يُذْكَرَ لما ضابطة يعرف به أحكام جميعها . فأقول : قد عرفت أن نسبة المحصن إلى الزنا توجب حد القذف، فنسبة غير المحصن ) كالعبد والكافر إليه لا توجب الحد ؛ لانحطاط درجتهما بل توجب التعزير ؛ لإشاعة الفاحشة.

[۱] قوله : «بغا» - بفتح الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الغين المعجمة – ، ومثله : به علة الابنة، وهو باغا ، وكأنه انتزع من البغاء، كذا في «المغرب»). [٢] قوله : من شتم العوام ؛ قيل : هو بمعنى المابون ؛ أي من به . الذي لا يقدر على ترك أن يؤتى في دُبُره لدودة ونحوها. كذا في «المغرب». قال في «البحر»: «وينبغي أن يجب التعزير فيه اتفاقا ؛ لأنه ألحق الشين به لعدم ظهور الكذب فيه، وصرّح في «الظهيرية» لوجوب التعزير في : يا مَعْفُوج ، وهو المأتي في الدبر»، وفي «النهر»: إنّه يعزّر في عرفنا في يا مؤاجر وفي : ولد الحرام، وفي : بغا.
[٣] قوله : والضحكة ؛ - بضم الضاد المعجمة، وسكون الحاء المهملة - [٤] قوله : السُّخرة ؛ بضم السين المهملة، وسكون الخاء المعجمة. [0] قوله : ضابطة ؛ أي قاعدة كليّة ينضبط بها أحكام الجزئيات، ومثل هذه الضابطة يسمى قانوناً وقاعدة.
[٦]قوله : يعرف به ؛ الضمير راجع إلى «الضابطة»، وتذكيره باعتبار المذكور، وفي بعض النسخ : ضابط ، فلا حاجة إلى التأويل.
[۷]قوله : «نسبة المحصن» ؛ إضافة النسبة إلى المحصن نسبة إلى المفعول.
[۸]قوله : فنسبة غير المحصن ؛ أشار به إلى أنَّ ذكر المملوك والكافر في المتن اتفاقي، فإنّ الحكم في كلّ غير محصن كالمملوك والكافر والمجنون والصبي وغير العفيف عن الزنى كذلك.

(۱) «المغرب»(ص٢٤٥). (۲) انتهى من «البحر الرائق» (٥ : ٥٠).

ونسبة المحصن إلى غيرِ الزِّنا لا توجب حد القذف ، فهل توجب التعزير أم لا؟
فإن نسبه إلى فعل اختياري يحرم في الشرع
[١] قوله : بل توجب التعزير ؛ ويبلغ به غايته ؛ أي تسعة وثلاثين سوطاً، كما لو أصاب من أجنبية كلّ محرم غير جماع، أو أخذ السارق بعد جمعه للمتاع قبل إخراجه ، وفيما عدا هذه المواضع الثلاثة لا يبلغ غاية التعزير، كذا في «البحر» (۱) وذكر في «الدرر»: قيل تارك الصلاة يعزرُ ويضرب حتى يسيل منه الدم، وفي «الخانية» : من وطئ غلاماً يشدّ أشدّ التعزير.
[٢]قوله : إلى فعل اختياري ؛ أي فعل صادر عنه باختياره ، كقوله : أنت خائن : وهو الذي يخون فيما يده من الأمانات. وأنت مباحي : وهو من يعتقد أن الأفعال كلّها مباحة. وأنت عواني: وهو الساعي إلى الحكام بالناس ظلماً. وأنت منافق : وهو قسمان: منافق اعتقادي، وهو من يظهر الإيمان بلسانه ويسر الكفر بقلبه، ومنافق عملي، وهو من يعمل عمل أهل النفاق كترك جماعة الصلوات والفجور والمشاتمة عند المابحثة، وخلف الوعد واعتياد الكذب، ونحو ذلك على ما وردت به الأخبار. وأنت رافضي، وأنت خارجي، وأنت مبتدع، وأنت وهابي - أي منسوب إلى محمد بن عبد الوهاب النجدي صاحب الفتاوى الزائغة - ، وأنت يهودي، وأنت نصراني، وأنت كافر، وأنت ساحر، وأنت مقامر وأنت مابون إلى غير ذلك مما بسطه شراح «الهداية» وأصحاب الفتاوى وغيرهم.
[٣] قوله : يحرم ؛ في الشرع المراد بالحرمة أعم من الحرمة الاصطلاحية، وهي أن يثبت كونه ممنوعاً بدليل قطعي، بحيث يكون منكره كافراً، فيشمل المكروه وغيره.
(۱) «البحر الرائق»(٥ : .(٥٢)
Book of Hudud, Section on Ta'zir. The word 'bagha' refers to the insults of the common people who utter it without knowing what they say. 'Ad-dahka' (with damma on dad and sukun on ha) means one at whom people laugh; with the form of 'humza' means one who laughs at people. Likewise 'as-sukhrah' and similar. Know that words denoting obscenities are countless, so it is necessary to mention for them a principle by which their rulings are known. So I say: You have known that attributing zina to a muhsan necessitates the hadd of qadhf, so attributing it to a non-muhsan, like a slave or a disbeliever, does not necessitate the hadd due to their low status, but necessitates ta'zir due to spreading immorality. [Commentary: Explanation of words and rulings.] And attributing to a muhsan something other than zina does not necessitate hadd of qadhf; does it necessitate ta'zir or not? If he attributes to him a voluntary action that is prohibited in Shari'ah...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the rulings on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for verbal insults. It begins by defining several insulting terms: 'bagha' (sodomite), 'dahka' (one laughed at or one who laughs), and 'sukhrah' (mockery). The author then states the need for a general principle (dabitah) to cover all such insults. The key ruling is that accusing a muhsan (a free, married, chaste Muslim) of zina incurs the fixed hadd of qadhf (80 lashes), while accusing a non-muhsan (e.g., a slave, disbeliever, or unchaste person) of zina incurs ta'zir instead, because the accusation does not meet the strict conditions for hadd. Similarly, accusing a muhsan of a non-zina but prohibited act (such as theft, hypocrisy, etc.) also incurs ta'zir, not hadd. The commentator elaborates on the meaning of 'voluntary prohibited act' and gives numerous examples from fiqh works, including insults like 'you are a hypocrite', 'refuser', 'innovator', 'Wahhabi', etc. The maximum ta'zir is 39 lashes, except in few exceptional cases like sodomy where the ta'zir is more severe. The passage is a typical Hanafi presentation of legal principles derived from the primary texts, using analogical reasoning (qiyas) within the framework of the school.

Key Terms
باغٍ (bāghī): A person who is sodomized; an insult used for one who engages in anal intercourse.
الضحكة (aḍ-ḍaḥkah): With dammah on ḍād and sukūn on ḥā’: one who is laughed at. With the form of hamzah: one who laughs at others.
التعزير (at-ta‘zīr): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge, less than fixed hadd punishments.
ضابطة (ḍābiṭah): A comprehensive principle or rule that governs all specific cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) no punishment
B) hadd of qadhf
C) ta'zir
D) the death penalty

Key Takeaway
The legal principle (dabitah) is that ta'zir applies to accusations of any prohibited voluntary act, except for the specific case of accusing a muhsan of zina which triggers hadd of qadhf.

Three Conditions for Ta'zir in Verbal Insults
Defining when ta'zir is obligatory for verbal insults

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three conditions for ta'zir in verbal insults and apply them to determine whether ta'zir is obligatory in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Ta'zir for slander requires the act to be voluntary, forbidden, and disgraceful in custom — and fails if the victim is already known for it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lays out the precise legal criteria that transform a verbal insult into an offense worthy of ta'zir. The Hanafi school emphasizes the interplay between legal prohibition and social custom, creating a nuanced framework that protects honor while avoiding frivolous punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Extract from the passage the three conditions: (1) the attributed act must be voluntary (ikhtiyari), (2) forbidden in the Law (muharram shar'an), and (3) considered a disgrace in custom ('aran fi al-'urf).
These must all be present for ta'zir to be wajib.
2
Understand the scope of 'forbidden'
The passage clarifies that 'haram' here includes even makruh; it is a broad prohibition meaning 'legally prohibited'.
Thus, even acts that are merely makruh tahrimi or tanzihi can trigger ta'zir if they are disgraceful.
3
Apply the exception for known attributes
Note that if the victim is already known for that trait (e.g., a known sinner called 'sinner'), no ta'zir is applied because the shame does not attach.
The rationale: ta'zir is a right of the victim to remove disgrace, but if the disgrace is already attached, ta'zir is pointless.
4
Differentiate natural attributes
Insults about inherent, non-voluntary characteristics (like calling someone a donkey) do not incur ta'zir because they are not voluntary and the literal meaning is not intended; the disgrace returns to the speaker.
The principle: if the insult's disgrace returns to the speaker (due to obvious falsehood), no ta'zir.
5
Recognize the heightened protection for nobles
Ta'zir is more severe when the victim is a sharif (by lineage, knowledge, or character) because the disgrace is greater.
The sharif definition includes sayyids, Alawis, jurists, and those of good character.

Source Text

بل توجب التعزير ؛ لإشاعة الفاحشة.
[1] قوله : بل توجب التعزير ؛ ويبلغ به غايته ؛ أي تسعة وثلاثين سوطاً، كما لو أصاب من أجنبية كلّ محرم غير جماع، أو أخذ السارق بعد جمعه للمتاع قبل إخراجه ، وفيما عدا هذه المواضع الثلاثة لا يبلغ غاية التعزير، كذا في «البحر» (۱) وذكر في «الدرر»: قيل تارك الصلاة يعزرُ ويضرب حتى يسيل منه الدم، وفي «الخانية» : من وطئ غلاماً يشدّ أشدّ التعزير.

من العد؛ أي يكون ذلك الفعلُ بحيث
[٢] قوله : يجب التعزير ؛ بشرط أن لا يكون ذلك الرجلُ موصوفاً بتلك الصفة، ومشتهراً بها، فمن اشتهر بالفسق لا يعزّر من يقول له : يا فاسق، وقس عليه جميع الأمثلة، والوجه في ذلك أن التعزير إنما يجب حقاً لـه ؛ لكون الشاتم الحـق بـه شـيـناً وعاراً، وهو مفقود في صورة اشتهاره بذلك الوصف.
[٣] قوله : وإلا ؛ أي وإن لم توجد الأمور الثلاثة ؛ أي كون الفعل اختيارياً، وكونه ممنوعاً شرعاً، وكونه مما يعد عاراً ، وهذا أعم مما لا يوجد فيه واحد من الأمور الثلاثة، ومما يوجد فيه بعض دون بعض.
[٤]قوله : للأشراف ؛ المراد بالشريف من كان كريم النفس، حسن الطبع، فيدخل فيه من له شرافةُ النسب، كالساداتِ والعلوية، ومن له شرافة العلم كالفقهاء ، ومن ليس له أحد الشرفين المذكورين إلا أنه حسن الطبع كريم الأخلاق. [٥] قوله : الخلقية ؛ - بكسر الخاء المعجمة - ، نسبة إلى الخلقة ؛ أي الأمور التي تكون في خلقة الإنسان بإذن خالقه، وليس لاكتسابه فيها دخل.
[٦] قوله : في يا حمار وكذا في نحو: يا ثور، يا بقر، يا حيّة، ونحو ذلك ؛ لظهور كذبه، والأصل أنّ كلّ سب عاد شينه إلى الساب؛ فإنّه لا يعزّر، فإن عاد الشين فيه إلى المسبوب عُزر، كذا في «البحر»).
وبه يعلم : إنه لو أراد بقوله : يا حمار ونحوه معناه الحقيقي لم يعزّر أيضاً؛ لظهور كذبه ، فيعود شينه إليه لا إلى المسبوب.
Rather, it necessitates ta'zir, because of spreading obscenity. [1] His statement: 'Rather, it necessitates ta'zir, and it reaches its maximum, i.e., thirty-nine lashes, just as if he touched a foreign woman in any prohibited way other than intercourse, or if he took the thief after he collected the goods before taking them out. Apart from these three instances, it does not reach the maximum of ta'zir, as in 'al-Bahr' (1). And it is mentioned in 'al-Durar': It is said that the one who abandons prayer is punished by ta'zir and beaten until blood flows from him. And in 'al-Khaniyyah': Whoever has intercourse with a boy receives the most severe ta'zir.
No? And attributing a muhsan to other than fornication does not necessitate the hadd of qadhf, so does it necessitate ta'zir or not? For if he attributed him to a voluntary act that is forbidden in the Law... [2] His statement: 'To a voluntary act,' i.e., an act issuing from him by his choice, such as his saying: 'You are a traitor'—he is one who betrays what is in his hands of trusts. And 'You are an Ibahi'—he is one who believes that all actions are permissible. And 'You are an 'Awani'—he is one who unjustly incites the rulers against people. And 'You are a hypocrite'—and it is of two types: a hypocrite in belief, who displays faith with his tongue and conceals disbelief in his heart, and a hypocrite in action, who acts like the people of hypocrisy, such as abandoning congregational prayers, immorality, reviling during discussions, breaking promises, habitual lying, and the like, according to what the reports have conveyed. And 'You are a Rafidi,' 'you are a Khariji,' 'you are a innovator,' 'you are a Wahhabi'—i.e., attributed to Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Najdi, the author of aberrant fatwas—'you are a Jew,' 'you are a Christian,' 'you are an infidel,' 'you are a sorcerer,' 'you are a gambler,' 'you are a defective [mabun?],' and so on, as elaborated by the commentators of 'al-Hidayah' and the authors of fatwas and others. (3) His statement: 'Is forbidden' in the Law—meaning by 'forbidden' is more general than the technical meaning (of haram), which is that it is established as prohibited by a definitive proof such that its denier is an infidel, so it includes the makruh and other things.
[2] And it is considered a disgrace in custom, so ta'zir becomes obligatory, otherwise not, unless it is degrading to the noble. [0] And we only said: to a voluntary act, as a precaution against natural (inborn) matters, so there is no ta'zir in: [6] 'O donkey,' because its literal meaning is not intended, but rather its metaphorical meaning. [1] His statement: 'And it is considered'—in the passive voice—the people of custom consider it a disgrace and criticize its doer. [from counting?] i.e., that act is such that [2] His statement: 'Ta'zir becomes obligatory'—on condition that that man is not described by that attribute and known for it, for whoever is known for sin is not given ta'zir to the one who says to him: 'O sinner,' and analogize all the examples. The reason is that ta'zir is obligatory as a right for him because the reviler brings shame and disgrace upon him, and that is absent in the case of his being known for that description. [3] His statement: 'Otherwise'—i.e., if the three matters are not present: that the act is voluntary, that it is legally prohibited, and that it is considered a disgrace. And this includes cases where none of the three exist and where some exist without others. [4] His statement: 'To the noble'—meaning by 'noble' one who is generous in spirit, good in nature, so it includes those who have nobility of lineage, like the sayyids and Alawis, and those who have nobility of knowledge like the jurists, and those who have neither of the two mentioned honors but are good in nature and generous in character. [5] His statement: 'Natural'—with kasr of the letter kha'—relating to creation, i.e., matters that are in a person's creation by the permission of his Creator, and in which he has no acquired role. [6] His statement: 'In 'O donkey' and similarly in 'O ox,' 'O cow,' 'O snake,' and the like, because of the obvious falsehood, and the principle is that every curse whose disgrace returns to the curser, then no ta'zir is applied. If the disgrace returns to the cursed, then ta'zir is applied, as in 'al-Bahr'. And by this it is known that if he intended by his saying 'O donkey' and the like its literal meaning, he is also not given ta'zir, due to the obvious falsehood, so its disgrace returns to him, not to the cursed.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the conditions under which ta'zir (discretionary punishment) becomes obligatory for verbal insults. The passage is an expansion of the matn that states certain insults require ta'zir due to spreading obscenity. The discussion is organized around the author's commentary, which elaborates on the maximum of ta'zir and clarifies the criteria for its application.

The passage begins by stating that ta'zir is required for spreading obscenity (إشاعة الفاحشة). The commentary specifies that the maximum ta'zir is thirty-nine lashes, except in three cases: touching a foreign woman in a prohibited manner (without intercourse), a thief caught before removing the goods, and the hadd of qadhf. In these three, ta'zir may exceed the maximum. Other examples include abandoning prayer (severe beating) and sodomy with a boy (most severe ta'zir).

The central issue is whether slandering a muhsan (chaste person) by accusing them of something other than fornication ta'zir or not. The answer depends on three conditions: 1) the act attributed must be voluntary (اختياري), 2) it must be forbidden in the Law (محرم شرعاً) – here 'forbidden' includes even makruh, 3) it must be considered a disgrace in custom (عاراً في العرف). If all three are met, ta'zir is obligatory; otherwise not. The exception is when the person is already known for that attribute (e.g., a known sinner called 'sinner'), because the shame does not attach to them.

The passage provides a detailed list of insults that fulfill these conditions: traitor, Ibahi (one who considers all acts permissible), 'Awani (one who unjustly prosecutes), hypocrite (in belief or action), Rafidi, Khariji, innovator, Wahhabi, Jew, Christian, infidel, sorcerer, gambler, defective, etc. Each of these is a voluntary action or belief that is legally prohibited and disgraceful in custom.

The commentary then addresses natural attributes (خلقية), like being called a donkey, ox, cow, or snake. Since these are not voluntary and the literal meaning is not intended, no ta'zir applies. The principle is that if the disgrace of the insult returns to the speaker (due to obvious falsehood), there is no ta'zir; if it returns to the victim, ta'zir is applied. For example, calling someone a donkey is obviously false, so the shame falls on the speaker, and no ta'zir is given. Even if the literal meaning were intended, it would still be obviously false, so no ta'zir.

Finally, the passage clarifies that ta'zir for noble people (الأشراف) is more severe, as they are more affected by disgrace. The 'noble' include those of high lineage (sayyids, Alawis), scholars, and those of good character.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah, determined by the judge based on the severity of the act.
عار ('ar): Disgrace or shame that reduces a person's honor in custom ('urf). It is a condition for establishing ta'zir.
شريف (sharif): A noble person, either by lineage (sayyids, Alawis), knowledge (jurists), or character (generous, virtuous).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for ta'zir to be obligatory when someone says 'You are a traitor'?
A) The victim must be a noble person.
B) The act of treason must be voluntary, forbidden, and considered disgraceful in custom.
C) The speaker must have intended to cause physical harm.
D) The victim must have previously committed the act.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir for verbal insults in the Hanafi school is wajib only when the attributed act is voluntary, forbidden, and disgraceful, and the victim is not already known for that trait.

Three Conditions for Ta'zir in Insults
Explaining conditions for ta'zir in insults and applying to various cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for ta'zir in insulting speech and apply them to classify whether an insult requires ta'zir.

Core Idea
For ta'zir to be wajib, the insult must be voluntary, prohibited, and a disgrace in custom.

In this passage, the commentator explains that not every insult automatically triggers ta'zir (discretionary punishment). Instead, three conditions must be met: the act is voluntary, prohibited by Shari'ah, and considered a disgrace in local custom. We'll walk through these conditions and see how they apply to common examples like calling someone 'donkey'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is insulting speech. The ruling is ta'zir (discretionary punishment). Your task is to determine when ta'zir becomes wajib.
Ta'zir is not a fixed hadd; its obligation depends on conditions.
2
Extract the three conditions from the text
From the passage, list the three conditions: (1) the act must be voluntary (ikhtiyari), not a natural trait; (2) it must be prohibited by Shari'ah (mamnu' shar'an); (3) it must be considered a disgrace (aar) in custom (urf).
All three must be present for ta'zir to be wajib.
3
Apply conditions to the 'donkey' example
Calling someone 'donkey' as a metaphor is voluntary and prohibited (insult is haram). However, if the person is not actually a donkey, the word is clearly false. According to the principle, if the disgrace returns to the speaker, no ta'zir. But if the insulted is an honorable person, the disgrace attaches to them, so ta'zir is required.
Custom determines what is disgraceful; falsehood alone does not cancel ta'zir if it degrades the honorable.
4
Consider the exception for honorable vs. base persons
The passage introduces a distinction: ta'zir is due when the insulted is among the honorable (ashraf) but not when they are base (ardhal). This is because base people are not harmed by such words. Apply this to determine ta'zir in practice.
The judge must assess the status of the victim and local custom.

The reason is that ta'zir is obligatory as a right for him because the reviler has brought disgrace and shame upon him, which is absent in the case of his fame for that description. His statement: "otherwise" i.e., if the three matters are not found: the act being voluntary, prohibited by law, and considered a disgrace in custom. This is more general than cases where none of the three are present, and cases where some are present. His statement: "for the honorable" – what is meant by the honorable is one who is noble of soul, good of character. His statement: "in 'O donkey'" and similarly in 'o ox', 'o cow', 'o snake', and the like, due to the apparent falsehood. By this it is known that if he meant by his saying 'O donkey' and the like its real meaning, he is also not punished due to the apparent falsehood, so its disgrace returns to him, not to the insulted. Like saying 'stupid' for example, which is a natural matter, and similarly 'monkey' meaning ugly-faced, 'dog' meaning bad character, unless it is said to a noble-souled person like a scholar, an Alawi, or a righteous man, for they are worthy of honor, so the insulter is punished for insulting them, unlike the base people who utter such words frequently and do not care if they are said to them. And we said: it must be prohibited in law, as a precaution against voluntary acts that are not prohibited in law but are considered a disgrace in custom, like cupping. His statement: 'like stupid' is fa'il from baladah, meaning foolishness and lack of intelligence. His statement: 'like a scholar or an Alawi' with two fatha's, i.e., one who is from the descendants of our master, i.e., from Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her), known in our custom as sayyid, or from other wives. It is said this term is specific to those from his descendants from other wives. His statement: 'people of honor' i.e., they deserve it, for we are commanded to place people in their positions and to respect those of noble knowledge or lineage. His statement: 'by insulting them' indicates that there is no disbelief in insulting scholars and other honorable people. It is mentioned in some fatawa that insulting scholars is disbelief, but that is when he insults them on account of their knowledge. His statement: 'unlike the base people' with fat-ha on the hamza, plural of radhil, who is one who has no honor in any way. This detail – i.e., ta'zir for uttering these words indicating physical defects if the insulted is among the honorable, and no ta'zir if he is among others – is that which is chosen in al-Hidayah, al-Kafi, and others. The apparent position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that there is no ta'zir for these words absolutely. The opinion chosen by some mashayikh is that ta'zir is absolutely required, and some supported it with what is established in its place that whoever commits an evil or harms a Muslim without right by word, deed, or gesture, ta'zir is binding upon him. And you know what is in it: indeed, the base people and the commoners who lack honor of status, lineage, knowledge, and action are not offended by such words; so how can ta'zir be obligatory for uttering these words in their case? His statement: 'like cupping' – for cupping is not a prohibited profession, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) was cupped.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the conditions for ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in cases of insulting speech. The author establishes three conditions for ta'zir to be obligatory: (1) the act must be voluntary (as opposed to involuntary traits like stupidity), (2) it must be prohibited by the Shari'ah, and (3) it must be considered a disgrace (aar) in custom. The passage then clarifies exceptions: first, if the insult is clearly false (like calling a non-donkey a 'donkey'), the disgrace returns to the insulter, so ta'zir is not required unless it harms the insulted's honor. Second, even if the act is not prohibited in Shari'ah (e.g., cupping as a profession), if it is considered a disgrace in custom, ta'zir may be required based on the first condition. However, the main focus is on metaphorical insults like 'donkey' or 'dog' used to insult someone's character or appearance. The commentator explains that if such words are said to a person of honor (ashraf: scholars, sayyids, righteous people), ta'zir is due because they are sensitive to such insults. But if said to base people (ardhal) who commonly use such language and are not offended, no ta'zir is imposed. The passage then discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: the Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad) holds that no ta'zir is required for such metaphorical insults in any case. The preferred view in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi (adopted by the commentator) is the detailed one: ta'zir if said to honorable people, otherwise not. A third view, from some later mashayikh, mandates ta'zir absolutely. The commentator criticizes this latter view, arguing that base people are not harmed by such words, so ta'zir would be pointless. The passage thus teaches that ta'zir is a flexible punishment tailored to circumstances, and not every insult merits it.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offences that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah, determined by the judge based on the severity and circumstances.
عار (aar): A disgrace or shame in the eyes of society; an act that brings dishonor upon the perpetrator if known.
أشراف (ashraf): Honorable people: those of noble lineage (sayyids, Alawis), noble knowledge (scholars), or noble character (righteous, good-natured).
أرذال (ardhal): Base or ignoble people: those lacking any of the honors of lineage, knowledge, or character.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three conditions for ta'zir in insults according to the passage?
A) The act must be voluntary.
B) The act must be prohibited by Shari'ah.
C) The act must be a sin that has a fixed hadd penalty.
D) The act must be considered a disgrace in custom.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is not automatic; it depends on three conditions: the act is voluntary, prohibited, and a disgrace in custom. Clear falsehoods may not require ta'zir unless they insult a person of honor.

Intra-Madhhab Dispute: Ta'zir for Metaphorical Insults
Explaining intra-madhhab differences on ta'zir for metaphorical insults.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on ta'zir for metaphorical insults and explain which is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
Within the Hanafi school, ta'zir for 'donkey'-type insults ranges from never to always; the middle view—ta'zir for honorable persons only—is preferred.

This passage not only lays out the conditions for ta'zir but also records a classic intra-madhhab dispute: should ta'zir be imposed for metaphorical insults like calling someone 'donkey' or 'dog'? The commentator presents three positions: absolute no, absolute yes, and a detailed view depending on the victim's honor. We'll analyze each position's proof and understand why the detailed view becomes the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State the disputed issue
The issue is: Are metaphorical insults (e.g., 'donkey', 'ox', 'dog') subject to ta'zir?
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
2
Identify the three positions
From the passage, list: (1) Zahir al-Riwayah (Abu Hanifa's view as transmitted by Imam Muhammad): no ta'zir absolutely. (2) Some mashayikh: ta'zir absolutely. (3) The detailed view in al-Hidayah & al-Kafi: ta'zir only if the insulted is honorable (ashraf).
The commentator explicitly mentions these three.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
Zahir al-Riwayah: insults are clearly false, so disgrace returns to insulter. Absolute ta'zir: general principle that any harm to a Muslim without right requires ta'zir. Detailed view: honorable persons are sensitive to such insults, so they are harmed; base people are not.
Each proof relies on different principles: falsehood, general harm, or custom and victim status.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The passage states that the detailed view is chosen (ikhtiyar) by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi. The commentator supports it, criticizing the absolute ta'zir view by arguing that base people are not harmed.
Tarjih is based on practical consideration: ta'zir only when it serves its purpose of punishment and deterrence.
5
Apply the mufta bihi to a scenario
Scenario: A man calls a layperson 'donkey' vs. calling a scholar 'donkey'. According to the mufta bihi, ta'zir applies to the scholar but not to the layperson.
The judge must determine the victim's status and local custom.
