الهداية والكافي وغيرهما وظاهر الرواية هو عدم التعزير بهذه الألفاظ مطلقاً ومختار بعض المشايخ هو التعزير مطلقاً بها وأيده بعضهم بما تقرر في مقره إن كل من ارتكب منكراً أو أذى مسلماً بغير حق بقول أو فعل أو إشارة يلزمه التعزيز وأنت تعلم ما فيه فإن الأراذل والسوقية الخالين عن شرافة الحسب والنسب والعلم والعمل لا يتأذون بمثل هذه الكلمات فكيف يجب التعزيز بإطلاق هذه الكلمات في حقهم قوله كالحجام فإن الحجامة ليست كسباً ممنوعاً فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم احتجم
Book of Hudud, Chapter on Ta'zir. And it is considered a disgrace in custom, so ta'zir is obligatory, otherwise not, unless it is a degradation of the honorable. And we said: it must be a voluntary act, as a precaution against matters of natural disposition, so there is no ta'zir for saying "O donkey" because its real meaning is not intended, rather its metaphorical meaning. His statement: "it is considered" in the passive voice: the people of custom consider it a disgrace and criticize its perpetrator. From counting: that is, the act is such that... His statement: "ta'zir is obligatory" on condition that the person is not described with that attribute and famous for it; whoever is famous for sin is not given ta'zir if someone says to him "O sinner," and analogize all examples. The reason is that ta'zir is obligatory as a right for him because the reviler has brought disgrace and shame upon him, which is absent in the case of his fame for that description. His statement: "otherwise" i.e., if the three matters are not found: the act being voluntary, prohibited by law, and considered a disgrace in custom. This is more general than cases where none of the three are present, and cases where some are present. His statement: "for the honorable" – what is meant by the honorable is one who is noble of soul, good of character. This includes those of noble lineage, like the sayyids and Alawis, and those of noble knowledge like the jurists, and those who have neither of these two honors but are good-natured and of noble character. His statement: "natural" with kasra on the kha' – related to creation, i.e., matters that are in the nature of man by the permission of his Creator and in which he has no role in acquiring. His statement: "in 'O donkey'" and similarly in 'o ox', 'o cow', 'o snake', and the like, due to the apparent falsehood. The principle is that every insult whose disgrace returns to the insulter is not punished with ta'zir. If the disgrace returns to the insulted, he is punished. As in al-Bahr. By this it is known that if he meant by his saying 'O donkey' and the like its real meaning, he is also not punished due to the apparent falsehood, so its disgrace returns to him, not to the insulted. Like saying 'stupid' for example, which is a natural matter, and similarly 'monkey' meaning ugly-faced, 'dog' meaning bad character, unless it is said to a noble-souled person like a scholar, an Alawi, or a righteous man, for they are worthy of honor, so the insulter is punished for insulting them, unlike the base people who utter such words frequently and do not care if they are said to them. And we said: it must be prohibited in law, as a precaution against voluntary acts that are not prohibited in law but are considered a disgrace in custom, like cupping. His statement: 'like stupid' is fa'il from baladah, meaning foolishness and lack of intelligence. His statement: 'like a scholar or an Alawi' with two fatha's, i.e., one who is from the descendants of our master, i.e., from Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her), known in our custom as sayyid, or from other wives. It is said this term is specific to those from his descendants from other wives. His statement: 'people of honor' i.e., they deserve it, for we are commanded to place people in their positions and to respect those of noble knowledge or lineage. His statement: 'by insulting them' indicates that there is no disbelief in insulting scholars and other honorable people. It is mentioned in some fatawa that insulting scholars is disbelief, but that is when he insults them on account of their knowledge. His statement: 'unlike the base people' with fat-ha on the hamza, plural of radhil, who is one who has no honor in any way. This detail – i.e., ta'zir for uttering these words indicating physical defects if the insulted is among the honorable, and no ta'zir if he is among others – is that which is chosen in al-Hidayah, al-Kafi, and others. The apparent position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that there is no ta'zir for these words absolutely. The opinion chosen by some mashayikh is that ta'zir is absolutely required, and some supported it with what is established in its place that whoever commits an evil or harms a Muslim without right by word, deed, or gesture, ta'zir is binding upon him. And you know what is in it: indeed, the base people and the commoners who lack honor of status, lineage, knowledge, and action are not offended by such words; so how can ta'zir be obligatory for uttering these words in their case? His statement: 'like cupping' – for cupping is not a prohibited profession, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) was cupped.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the conditions for ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in cases of insulting speech. The author establishes three conditions for ta'zir to be obligatory: (1) the act must be voluntary (as opposed to involuntary traits like stupidity), (2) it must be prohibited by the Shari'ah, and (3) it must be considered a disgrace (aar) in custom. The passage then clarifies exceptions: first, if the insult is clearly false (like calling a non-donkey a 'donkey'), the disgrace returns to the insulter, so ta'zir is not required unless it harms the insulted's honor. Second, even if the act is not prohibited in Shari'ah (e.g., cupping as a profession), if it is considered a disgrace in custom, ta'zir may be required based on the first condition. However, the main focus is on metaphorical insults like 'donkey' or 'dog' used to insult someone's character or appearance. The commentator explains that if such words are said to a person of honor (ashraf: scholars, sayyids, righteous people), ta'zir is due because they are sensitive to such insults. But if said to base people (ardhal) who commonly use such language and are not offended, no ta'zir is imposed. The passage then discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: the Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad) holds that no ta'zir is required for such metaphorical insults in any case. The preferred view in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi (adopted by the commentator) is the detailed one: ta'zir if said to honorable people, otherwise not. A third view, from some later mashayikh, mandates ta'zir absolutely. The commentator criticizes this latter view, arguing that base people are not harmed by such words, so ta'zir would be pointless. The passage thus teaches that ta'zir is a flexible punishment tailored to circumstances, and not every insult merits it.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offences that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah, determined by the judge based on the severity and circumstances.
عار (aar): A disgrace or shame in the eyes of society; an act that brings dishonor upon the perpetrator if known.
أشراف (ashraf): Honorable people: those of noble lineage (sayyids, Alawis), noble knowledge (scholars), or noble character (righteous, good-natured).
أرذال (ardhal): Base or ignoble people: those lacking any of the honors of lineage, knowledge, or character.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school, when is ta'zir required for calling someone 'donkey'?
A) Always, regardless of the person.
B) Never, because the word is clearly false.
C) Only when said to a person of honor (ashraf).
D) Only when said to a person known for that trait.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon view requires ta'zir for metaphorical insults only when the victim is honorable; the three positions reflect different weighting of custom and harm.

Ta'zir for Insult by Calling One Nakis or Similar
Ruling on ta'zir for insult with conditions based on 'urf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for ta'zir when an insult is considered shameful based on 'urf, and apply it to different social statuses.

Core Idea
Ta'zir for insult depends on custom and status, not the word itself.

This passage from the Book of Hudud explains that not every insult warrants ta'zir. The Hanafi school uses custom ('urf) to determine whether a particular insult is disgraceful for the person addressed. For instance, calling a noble 'nakis' is punishable, but the same word may have no effect on a common market-goer. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this differentiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is calling someone 'nakis' (lowly) or similar. The ruling is that ta'zir applies if said to nobles, otherwise not.
Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment.
2
Determine the condition based on 'urf
The condition is that the insult must be considered disgraceful ('ar) in the local custom. The author provides an example: market people do not care about such insults.
'Urf is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is rational observation: common people (السوقية) do not mind baseness, so the same insult is not disgraceful for them. Thus, ta'zir is linked to actual harm as perceived by custom.
This is a dalil aqli based on observable behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود فصل في التعزير
[۱]
ونحوه يراد به دنيء الهمة ، وكذلك بالفارسية يا ناكس : إن قيل للأشراف عزّر، ولغيرهم لا ، ألا ترى أن السوقية ، لا يبالون بأفعال فيها الخسة والدناءة. وإنما قلنا : يعد عاراً في العرف؛ احتراز عن أفعال اختيارية تحرم شرعاً، ولا يعد عاراً في العرف ، كلعب النرد
Book of Hudud, Section on Ta'zir. [1] And like it is meant lowliness of character, and likewise in Persian 'ya nakis': if said to nobles he is ta'zired, and for others no. Do you not see that the market people do not care about actions that contain baseness and vileness. And we only said: it is considered disgrace in custom, to avoid actions that are voluntary, prohibited by law, but not considered disgrace in custom, like playing backgammon.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for insulting someone by calling them 'nakis' (lowly) or similar terms. The author explains that the ruling depends on whether the term is considered shameful in the local custom ('urf). If addressed to nobles, it is disgraceful and warrants ta'zir; for commoners, it may not be disgraceful. The proof is the observation that market people (السوقية) are not bothered by such insults. This ruling guards against applying ta'zir for every prohibited insult; only those that are considered shameful in 'urf are punishable.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for crimes without fixed hadd or kaffarah, determined by the judge.
عار (ar): Disgrace or shame in the context of insult; the criterion for applying ta'zir.
عرف (urf): Common custom of the people; used as a basis for determining whether an insult is shameful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when would calling someone 'nakis' warrant ta'zir?
A) Always, because it is an insult.
B) Only if said to nobles, as it is considered disgraceful in custom.
C) Only if said to common market people.
D) Never, because ta'zir is for physical harm only.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir for insult is not automatic; it requires that the insult be considered disgraceful in the local custom and take into account the social status of the person insulted.

Prohibition of Backgammon, Chess, and Singing: Inter-Madhhab Dispute on Chess
Rulings on prohibited games and singing with proofs and madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on backgammon, chess, and singing, and explain the Shafi'i counter-argument on chess with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Backgammon is haram by explicit hadith; chess is haram by analogy for Hanafis, but Shafi'is permit it unless accompanied by corruption.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary presents rulings on three recreations: backgammon, chess, and singing. It provides clear hadith evidence for the prohibition of backgammon, then extends the ruling to chess via qiyas, while noting the Shafi'i opposition. Finally, it asserts the general prohibition of singing. Understanding the proofs and the points of ikhtilaf is essential for grasping how Hanafi jurists derive rulings and engage with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage rules that backgammon (النرد) is haram based on multiple hadith. Chess (الشطرنج) is ruled haram by most scholars including Hanafis, but Shafi'is differ. Singing (الغناء) is also indicated as haram.
Focus on the difference in evidence: backgammon has direct textual proof, chess relies on analogy.
2
Locate the dalil for backgammon
The hadith using strong language (disobedience, dipping in pig's blood) establishes prohibition. The source type is Sunnah ahad, zanni al-thubut, but qati' al-dalala due to explicit threat.
Multiple narrations reinforce the ruling.
3
Analyse the qiyas for chess
Chess is analogized to backgammon (qiyas) because both lead to heedlessness and distraction from prayer. The Hanafi position is that the illa (effective cause) is similar, so chess is also haram.
The Shafi'i school challenges this analogy.
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'is argue that chess is not explicitly prohibited, it trains the mind, and is permissible unless accompanied by gambling, prayer delay, or abuse. They limit the prohibition to these external factors.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis see chess as inherently sinful; Shafi'is see it as permissible in essence.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The text sides with the majority (including Hanafis) that chess is prohibited, emphasizing the similarity to backgammon. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is prohibition.
The passage mentions 'most scholars' and 'our Hanafi companions' to assert authority.

Source Text

قوله : كلعب النرد ; فقد ورد عن النبي : من لعب بنرد أو نردشير فقد الله ورسوله» ) ، أخرجه أبو داود وابن حبان وغيرهما. وفي رواية لأبي داود ومسلم وابن ماجة : «مَن لعب بالنرد كأنما غمس يده في لحم خنزير ودمه». وفي رواية أحمد وأبي يعلى والبيهقي: «مثلُ الذي يلعب بالنرد ثم يقوم يصلّي مثل الذي يتوضأ بالقيح ودم الخنزير». وفي الباب أخبار أخر أيضاً بسطها الحافظ عبد العظيم المنذري في كتاب «الترغيب والترهيب»، وابن حجر المكي الهيتمي في كتابه «الزواجر عن اقتراف الكبائر». ومثله : اللعب بالشطرنج على رأي أكثر العلماء القائلين بحرمته، ومنهم أصحابنا الحنفيّة خلافاً للشافعية فإنّهم قائلون بحله إلا إذا اقترن به قمار أو إخراج صلاة عن وقتها أو سباب أو نحوها ، كما فصله الدَّمِيري في حياة الحيوان عنه ذكر العقرب ، وليطلب من هناك تفصيل واضع النرد والشطرنج وكيفيتهما، وسنذكر نبذاً من كيفيتهما عن قريب إن شاء الله تعالى.

His saying: like playing backgammon; for it has come from the Prophet: 'Whoever plays backgammon or nardshir has disobeyed Allah and His Messenger.' Related by Abu Dawud, Ibn Hibban, and others. And in a narration of Abu Dawud, Muslim, and Ibn Majah: 'Whoever plays backgammon is as if he dipped his hand in pig's flesh and blood.' And in a narration of Ahmad, Abu Ya'la, and al-Bayhaqi: 'The likeness of one who plays backgammon and then stands to pray is like one who performs wudu with pus and pig's blood.' And there are other reports on this topic, detailed by al-Hafiz 'Abd al-'Azim al-Mundhiri in 'al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib', and Ibn Hajar al-Makki al-Haytami in his book 'al-Zawajir 'an Iqtiraf al-Kaba'ir'. Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on prohibited games and singing. It begins with strong hadith proofs against backgammon (النرد), showing it is a major sin. Then it discusses chess (الشطرنج): the Hanafi school holds it prohibited, while Shafi'is permit it unless accompanied by gambling, prayer delay, or abuse. This is a clear inter-madhhab dispute. Finally, it mentions singing (الغناء) as prohibited generally, with a footnote from the commentator supporting that view, though later Hanafi scholars (like al-Laknawi) differentiate depending on instruments. The passage uses the authority of hadith to establish the rulings.

Key Terms
النرد (al-nard): Backgammon, a game with dice and board; ruled as strictly haram by Hanafis based on explicit hadith.
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess; Hanafi: haram by analogy; Shafi'i: halal unless accompanied by forbidden elements.
الغناء (al-ghina'): Singing; considered haram in general in this passage, but later Hanafis differentiate based on instruments and content.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A single weak hadith.

C) Consensus of all scholars without textual proof.
D) Analogy to chess.

Key Takeaway
Backgammon is proven haram by explicit hadith; chess is haram for Hanafis by analogy, but the Shafi'i school permits it unless corrupted by external factors.

The Discretionary Nature of Ta'zir Punishment
Ruling statement with expansion on ta'zir levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the discretionary principle of ta'zir and apply the four-level classification to hypothetical offenders.

Core Idea
Ta'zir punishment is not fixed; its severity depends on the crime and the offender's status.

Unlike hadd punishments, ta'zir has no predetermined measure. The judge calibrates both the manner and quantity of punishment based on the gravity of the offense and the character of the offender. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains this principle and provides a four-tier classification from later Hanafi works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Note that ta'zir's manner and quantity are delegated to the judge's discretion. The punishment varies with the crime.
2
Recognize the factors for severity
The passage gives examples: maximum ta'zir for major sins like touching a non-mahram woman without intercourse, or for a thief who gathers goods inside but does not remove them.
These examples show that ta'zir can be severe even if hadd is not applicable.
3
Understand the four-level classification
The passage lists four levels from 'al-Nihayah' and 'al-Durar': most noble (scholars, 'Alawis) get admonition; noble (chiefs, merchants) get summons; common folk get imprisonment/beating; lowest (idle, suspicious) may face killing if not deterred.
Levels are based on social status and expected deterrence.
4
Apply the classification
Given an offender's status and crime, determine which level of ta'zir applies, considering escalation if lower levels fail.
The killing option is a last resort for those undeterred by beating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ كيفيَّةُ التَّعزير وكميَّتُهُ يفوّضان إلى رأي الإمام على ما تقتضي جنايتهم، فإنّ العقوبة فيه تختلف باختلاف الجناية. فينبغي أن يبلغ غاية التعزير في الكبيرة، كما إذا أصاب من الأجنبية كلّ محرّم سوى الجماع، أو جمع السارق المتاع في الدار ولم يخرجه ، وكذا ينظر في أحوالهم، فإنّ من الناس من ينزجر باليسير، ومنهم من لا ينزجر إلا بالكثير. وذكر في «النهاية» و«الدرر» : إن التعزير على أربع مراتب : ١ . تعزير أشرفُ الأشراف : وهم العلماء والعلوية، بالإعلام بأن يقول القاضي له : بلغني أنك تفعل كذا فينزجر به. ٢ . وتعزير الأشراف : وهم نحو الدهاقين؛ أي رؤساء القرية والتجار وأرباب الأموال بالإعلام والجرّ إلى باب القاضي والخصومة في ذلك. ٣ . وتعزير أوسط الناس : وهم السوقة، بالحبس والضرب. ٤ . وتعزير أخسّ الناس وأرذلهم : وهم الذين لا يبالون بالضرب والحبس، وهم أهل البطالة والريبة، بالقتل؛ أي إذا لم ينزجروا بالضرب والحبس، انتقل إلى السيف وأمثاله.
Then, the manner and quantity of ta'zir are entrusted to the opinion of the imam according to what their crime requires, for the punishment differs according to the crime. So it is appropriate to reach the utmost degree of ta'zir in a major sin, such as when one touches a non-mahram woman in every prohibited manner except intercourse, or when a thief gathers goods inside the house but does not take them out. Likewise, consideration is given to their states, for among people are those who are deterred by a little, and among them are those who are not deterred except by much. And it is mentioned in 'al-Nihayah' and 'al-Durar' that ta'zir is of four levels: 1. Ta'zir of the most noble of the noble: they are the scholars and the 'Alawis, by informing, such that the judge says to him: 'I have heard that you do such and such,' and he is deterred by that. 2. Ta'zir of the noble: they are like the dihqans—i.e., village chiefs, merchants, and wealthy people—by informing and summoning them to the judge's court and litigation in that matter. 3. Ta'zir of the middle people: they are the common folk, by imprisonment and beating. 4. Ta'zir of the lowest and most contemptible people: they are those who do not care about beating and imprisonment, and they are the idle and suspicious, by killing—i.e., if they are not deterred by beating and imprisonment, they escalate to the sword and the like.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the foundational principle of ta'zir (discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have fixed hadd punishments). The author explains that ta'zir has no predetermined measure; its manner and quantity are left to the judge's discretion based on the severity of the crime and the character of the offender. For example, a major sin like touching a non-mahram woman in all ways short of intercourse deserves the maximum ta'zir. Similarly, a thief who gathers goods inside a house but does not remove them (thus not meeting the hadd threshold) also receives ta'zir. The passage then outlines a four-tier classification of ta'zir from the works 'al-Nihayah' and 'al-Durar', which categorize offenders by social status and deterrence: (1) the most noble (scholars and 'Alawis) are deterred by a mere admonition from the judge; (2) the noble (chiefs, merchants) are deterred by being summoned and publicly charged; (3) the common people are deterred by imprisonment and beating; (4) the lowest (idle, suspicious) who are undeterred by beating and imprisonment may face killing as a last resort. This classification reflects the Hanafi legal principle that punishment should be calibrated to achieve deterrence, considering the offender's dignity and social standing.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd punishment (e.g., illegal sexual advances short of zina, theft without removal from custody).
الدياقين (al-dahaqin): Village chiefs or local leaders, distinguished from common people in the ta'zir classification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest level of ta'zir for the most contemptible offenders who are not deterred by beating or imprisonment?
A) Exile
B) Imprisonment
C) Killing
D) Public admonition

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir punishments are flexible and graduated according to the offense and the offender's social standing and deterrence threshold.

The Four Levels of Ta'zir Punishment
Classification of ta'zir by social status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four levels of ta'zir punishment according to the Hanafi school and explain how social status and judicial discretion affect punishment severity.

Core Idea
Ta'zir punishment varies by the offender's social class — the nobler the person, the lighter the penalty.

This passage from al-Nihaya and al-Durar presents the Hanafi classification of ta'zir punishments into four levels based on the offender's social status. Unlike fixed hadd punishments, ta'zir is discretionary and tailored to the individual, taking into account both the gravity of the crime and the character of the offender. Understanding this classification is crucial for applying discretionary penalties justly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four social classes
List the four classes: ashraf al-ashraf (scholars and sayyids), al-ashraf (village chiefs, merchants, wealthy), al-awsat (middle class), al-akhassa (lowest class).
These categories are based on social standing and honor.
2
Connect each class to its punishment
Match each class with its specific ta'zir: notification for ashraf al-ashraf; notification and summons for al-ashraf; dragging and imprisonment for al-awsat; all of the above plus beating for al-akhassa.
Punishments escalate in severity as status decreases.
3
Understand the rationale
Higher status individuals are more sensitive to reputation, so a mere notification suffices to deter them. Lower status individuals require harsher measures.
Deterrence is achieved with minimal severity for the noble.
4
Examine judicial discretion
The imam (sultan or judge) considers the magnitude of the crime and the personal state of the offender and the victim. The example of qadhf (defamation) is given: an honorable defamer is admonished, a less honorable one imprisoned, a habitual reviler beaten and imprisoned.
Discretion allows proportionality.
5
Recognize the source authority
This classification is from al-Nihaya and al-Durar, which are later Hanafi works (fatawa mutakhkhirin). It is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
Not from Zahir al-Riwayah, but universally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في «النهاية» و«الدرر» : إن التعزير على أربع مراتب : ١ . تعزير أشرفُ الأشراف : وهم العلماء والعلوية، بالإعلام بأن يقول القاضي له : بلغني أنك تفعل كذا فينزجر به. ٢ . وتعزير الأشراف : وهم نحو الدهاقين ؛ أي رؤساء القرية والتجار وأرباب الأموال بالإعلام والجرّ إلى باب القاضي والخصومة في ذلك. ٣ . وتعزير الأوساط : بالجر والحبس. ٤ . وتعزير الأخساء : بهذا كله وبالضرب. إلى رأي الإمام فيُراعى عظم الجناية وصغرها، وحال القائل والمقول فيه. قوله : رأي الإمام ؛ أي السلطان والقاضي وغيرهما ممن له حق التعزير. قوله : عظم ؛ بكسر العين المهملة، وفتح الضاد المعجمة، ومثله الصغر : بكسر الصاد المهملة، وفتح الغين المعجمة. قوله : وحال القائل ؛ من كونه شريفاً يتنبه بأدنى التنبيه، أو خبيثاً شريراً، مصراً على قوله أو فعله أو غير مصر. قوله : والمقول فيه ؛ أي حال الذي قيل فيه ذلك القول، بل هو شريف يغار مما قيل فيه، ولو كان خفيفاً، أو هو رذيل لا يبالي بما قيل أو يقال. أي إن كان القاذف ذا مروءة وعظ، وإن كان دون ذلك حبس، وإن كان شتاماً ضرب وحبس، وكذا المقذوف إذا كان من العلماء والسادات والأبرار يعزر بقذفهم كل واحد من الأشرار.
And it is mentioned in al-Nihaya and al-Durar that ta'zir is of four levels: 1. Ta'zir of the most noble of the nobles: they are the scholars and 'Alawi (descendants of the Prophet), by informing such that the judge says to him: 'I have heard that you do such and such,' and he refrains. 2. Ta'zir of the nobles: they are like the village chiefs, i.e., heads of the village, merchants, and wealthy people, by informing and dragging to the door of the judge and litigation in that. 3. Ta'zir of the middle class: by dragging and imprisonment. 4. Ta'zir of the lowest: by all of that and beating. It is to the opinion of the imam, so he takes into account the magnitude and smallness of the crime, and the state of the speaker and the one spoken about. His saying: 'opinion of the imam' means the sultan, judge, and others who have the right of ta'zir. His saying: 'magnitude' with kasr of 'ayn and fatha of dad, and similarly 'smallness' with kasr of sad and fatha of ghayn. His saying: 'state of the speaker' regarding whether he is noble and refrains with the slightest warning, or wicked and evil, insistent on his saying or action or not. His saying: 'the one spoken about' means the state of the one about whom that saying is said, whether he is noble and protective of his honor, even if the offense is light, or he is base and does not care about what is said. If the defamer (qadhif) is of good character, he is admonished; if less than that, he is imprisoned; if he is a habitual reviler, he is beaten and imprisoned. Similarly, if the one defamed is from the scholars, sayyids, and righteous, each of the wicked is ta'zir for defaming them.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of ta'zir punishment. It cites al-Nihaya and al-Durar stating that ta'zir has four levels based on the social status of the offender. The highest class (ashraf al-ashraf) includes scholars and sayyids; their ta'zir is merely a notification from the judge. The next class (ashraf) includes village chiefs, merchants, and wealthy people; their punishment includes notification and being brought to court. The middle class (awsat) is punished by being brought and imprisoned. The lowest class (akhas) receives all of that plus beating. Then it notes that the magnitude of punishment is left to the imam's discretion, considering the gravity of the crime and the personal state of the one who spoke and the one spoken about. The commentary then explains key terms: 'ra'y al-imam' refers to the sultan or judge; 'adhm' (magnitude) and 'sughr' (smallness) are explained lexically; 'hal al-qa'il' considers if the speaker is noble or evil; and 'al-maqul fihi' considers the honor of the person spoken about. An example is given: if the defamer (qadhif) is honorable, he is admonished; if less, imprisoned; if a habitual abuser, he is beaten and imprisoned. Moreover, if the defamed is from scholars or sayyids, the defamer is severely punished.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or kaffarah.
أشرف الأشراف (ashraf al-ashraf): The most noble class: scholars and sayyids.
الأشراف (al-ashraf): Nobles: village chiefs, merchants, wealthy people.
الأوساط (al-awsat): Middle class: common people not of high status.
الأخساء (al-akhassa): Lowest class: ignoble or lowly people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Dragging and imprisonment
B) Notification by the judge that he heard of the offense
C) Beating alone
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir punishment is not fixed but graduated according to the offender's social standing and the judge's discretion, ensuring proportionality and deterrence.

Ta'zir According to Social Status
Explains ta'zir levels by class and judge's discretion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the forms of ta'zir for different social classes and explain how the judge considers the offender's and victim's status.

Core Idea
Ta'zir punishment varies by social status: nobles get reprimand, the base get beating.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya explains how ta'zir (discretionary punishment) is scaled according to the social class of the offender and the victim. The judge must consider both the gravity of the crime and the character of the parties involved. The text distinguishes between the middle class (awsat) and the lowly (akhissa), and the commentary elaborates on the factors that influence the judge's decision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the social classes mentioned
The text explicitly mentions two classes: the middle class (al-awsat) and the lowly (al-akhissa). A higher class (ashraf) is implied in the commentary as those who are sensitive and easily deterred.
Also note the implicit upper class not separately named but referenced in the commentary.
2
List the prescribed punishments
For the middle class: reprimand (jarr) and imprisonment (habs). For the lowly: all of that plus beating (darb).
3
Explain the role of the judge's discretion
The judge must consider the magnitude of the crime (cizam al-janaya wa sughruha) and the condition of the speaker (hal al-qa'il) and the one spoken about (hal al-maqul fih).
The commentary expands on these conditions: e.g., a noble person is deterred by a slight warning, while a wicked person may need harsher punishment.
4
Apply to the example of slander
The text applies these principles to qadhf (slander). If the slanderer is a person of chivalry, he is admonished; if less, imprisoned; if abusive, beaten and imprisoned. Similarly, the victim's status matters: slandering scholars and nobles incurs a stricter ta'zir.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The ruling in the commentary is authoritative (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school. It is based on the earlier fatwa works like Dhakhirat al-Uqba.
The text cites 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' as a source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعزير الأوساط : بالجر والحبس. وتعزير الأخساء : بهذا كله وبالضرب. إلى رأي الإمام فيُراعى عِظَمَ الجناية وصغرها ، وحالَ القائل والمقول فيه. قوله : رأي الإمام ؛ أي السلطان والقاضي وغيرهما ممن له حق التعزير. قوله : عظم ؛ بكسر العين المهملة، وفتح الضاد المعجمة، ومثله الصغر : بكسر الصاد المهملة، وفتح الغين المعجمة. قوله : وحال القائل ؛ من كونه شريفاً يتنبه بأدنى التنبيه، أو خبيثاً شريراً، مصرا على قوله أو فعله أو غير مصر. قوله : والمقول فيه ؛ أي حال الذي قيل فيه ذلك القول، بل هو شريف يغار مما قيل فيه، ولو كان خفيفاً، أو هو رذيل لا يبالي بما قيل أو يقال. أي إن كان القاذف ذا مروءة وعظ، وإن كان دون ذلك حبس، وإن كان شتاماً ضرب وحبس، وكذا المقذوف إذا كان من العلماء والسادات والأبرار يعزر بقذفهم كل واحد من الأشرار. ينظر : ذخيرة العقبى (ص۱۹۰).
And the ta'zir of the middle class: by reprimand and imprisonment. And the ta'zir of the lowly: by all of that and by beating. It is left to the opinion of the Imam, so the magnitude of the offense and its smallness, and the condition of the speaker and the one spoken about are considered. He said: 'opinion of the Imam' meaning the sultan, judge, and others who have the right of ta'zir. He said: 'magnitude' with kasra on 'ayn and fatha on dad, similarly 'smallness' with kasra on sad and fatha on ghayn. He said: 'condition of the speaker' whether he is noble who can be alerted by the slightest warning, or wicked and evil, persistent in his words or deeds or not. He said: 'the one spoken about' meaning the condition of the one about whom that statement was said; rather he is noble who is sensitive about what is said about him even if it is light, or he is base who does not care about what is said or will be said. That is, if the slanderer is a person of chivalry, he is admonished; if less than that, he is imprisoned; if he is abusive, he is beaten and imprisoned. Similarly, the slandered person if he is among scholars, nobles, and righteous, each of the wicked is punished for slandering them. See: Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 190).
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya explains the rules of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) in the Hanafi school. Ta'zir is not fixed; the judge has discretion based on the social status of the offender and the victim, and the nature of the offense. The text distinguishes three categories: middle class (أوساط) receive reprimand and imprisonment; lowly class (أخساء) receive the same plus beating. The judge considers the magnitude of the crime and the character of the parties. The commentary elaborates that a noble person may be deterred by a mere reprimand, while a wicked person may need harsher measures. The final note applies this specifically to slander (qadhf), showing how the ruling is implemented.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment for offenses that have no fixed hadd or kaffarah; its severity is left to the judge's ijtihad.
الأوساط (al-awsāṭ): The middle class of people, not the lowest nor the highest in society.
الأخساء (al-akhissāʾ): The lowest class of people, often base or wicked individuals.
الجر (al-jarr): Reprimand or verbal rebuke as a form of ta'zir.
الحبس (al-ḥabs): Imprisonment as a form of ta'zir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir is a flexible punishment in Hanafi fiqh, adjusted according to the social status and character of both the offender and the victim, as well as the severity of the crime.

Definition of Theft and Conditions for Hadd
Defines theft linguistically and legally, enumerates conditions for amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of theft and identify the conditions required for the application of the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Theft incurs amputation only when property is taken secretly from guarded custody, meets the nisab, and has no ambiguity.

This passage from the Book of Theft establishes a precise legal definition of theft (sariqa). The hadd punishment of amputation is not for every taking; it is a highly conditional penalty. Understanding these conditions prevents misapplication and distinguishes theft from other forms of unlawful taking, such as plunder or breach of trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
Note the key elements in the definition: taking, secretly, from a guarded place, owned by another, without ambiguity. Contrast 'tarr' (taking in owner's presence).
2
Extract the conditions for hadd (shurut)
List the conditions from the text: thief must be mukallaf, speaking, seeing; stolen property must reach nisab (10 dirhams), be guarded, owned, taken secretly, no shubha, not quickly spoiling, from valid owner's hand, in Dar al-'Adl.
These are necessary for amputation.
3
Understand the exclusions (mawani)
Identify who is exempt: child, insane, mute, blind; also cases of less than nisab, non-guarded property, counterfeit dirhams, etc.
These prevent hadd.
4
Note the Hanafi nisab threshold
The minimum value is ten good dirhams or equivalent. Compare with other schools: Shafi'i holds quarter dinar or three dirhams.
The difference matters for practical application.
5
Recognize the two types of theft
Minor theft (from owner or representative) and major theft (highway robbery). Both require secrecy, but differ in victim.
Major theft falls under different rules.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
[١]قوله : كتاب السرقة ؛ هو لغة : بمعنى أخذ الشيء من الغير خُفية، وشرعاً : أخذ مال معتبر من حرز أجنبي لا شبهة فيه خُفية، وهو قاصد للحفظ في نومه أو غيبته، وأخذ الشيء من الغير، وهو حاضر يقظان قاصد حفظه يُسمى طراً، وفاعله يقال : الطرار، كذا في «كليات أبي البقاء» وغيره.
وذكر في «القاموس» وغيره : إنّ السّرقة مصدر لسَرَقَ يَسرق ، من باب ضَرَب، - وهو بفتح السين المهملة، وكسر الراء المهملة، وجاءَ بضم السين، وسكون الراء - ، وجاء أيضا سَرَق : - بفتح السين، وفتح الراء، وبفتح السين وكسر الراء، وبفتح السين وسكون الراء - . والسرقة : - بفتح السين وسكون الراء، ويضم السين مع سكون الراء، ويفتح السين مع كسر الراء - : اسم، وقد جرت عادة الفقهاء بذكر بحث السرقة بعنوان الكتاب. واعترض عليه : بأن حد السرقة كغيره من الحدود، فلم لم يدرجوه في كتاب الحدود؟ ولم لم يعنونوه بالباب، كما فعلوا في حد الزنى وغيره. وأجيب عنه : بأن بحث السّرقة لما كان متضمناً للضمان أيضاً أفردوا له كتاباً، ثمّ السرقة على نوعين :
١ . سرقة صغرى ؛ وهي المتبادرة عند إطلاق السرقة.
٢. وسرقة كبرى ؛ وهي المسماة بقطع الطريق، ويعتبر في كل منهما أخذ المال خفية، لكن في الصغرى عن عين المالك أو من يقوم مقامه كالمودع والمستعير، وفي الكبرى عن عين الإمام الملتزم حفظ حقوق المسلمين وبلادهم كذا في «الفتح»
خفية.
[٢]
ومحلها : مال محرز مملوك
[١] قوله : خفية ؛ - بضم الخاء المعجمة، وكسرها - ، يعني : بقصد الاختفاء عن عين المسروق منه ، فلو أخذه مغالبة أو نهباً فليس بسرقة.
[۲] قوله : مال محرز ... الخ ؛ خرج بقيد المال أخذ ما ليس بمال، فهو ليس بسرقة، والحرزُ بصيغة المفعول من الإحراز أي المحفوظ الذي يقصد المالك حفظه عن الغير. واحترز به عن أخذ ما ليس بمحرز ، واحترز بقيد المملوكِ عن أخذ ما ليس بمملوك لأحد ؛ كأخذ أثمار أشجار الصحاري، والقول الجامع في حد السرقة التي يجب بها القطع هو ما ذكره الغزي في «تنوير الأبصار»: «إنّها أخذ مكلّف ناطق بصير عشرةً دراهم جياد أو مقدارها مقصودة ظاهرة الإخراج خفية من صاحب يد صحيحة، مما لا يتسارع إليه الفساد في دار العدل من حرز لا شبهة فيه ولا تأويل».
واحترز بقيد المكلّف عن أخذ غير المكلّف كالصبي والمجنون، فلا قطع عليهما ؛ إذ مدار جميع التكاليف على كونه مكلّفاً.
وبقيد الناطق عن الأخرس ، فلا يقطع الأخرس ؛ لاحتمال نطقه بشبهة. وبقيد البصير عن الأعمى، وإنما لا يقطع لجهله بمال غيره.
وزاد في «البحر» هنا قيداً آخر وهو كونه صاحب يد يسرى ورجل يمنى
وبقيد عشرة دراهم عن سرقة ما دونها فلا قطع بسرقة ما دون النصاب، وهو عندنا مقدار عشرة دراهم. وبقيد الجياد عن سرقة الزيوف أو النّبهرجة أو الستوقة، فلا قطع فيها. وبقيد مقصودة عمّالو سرق ثوباً قيمته دون عشرة، وفيه دينار أو دراهم مصرورة فلا قطع فيها، إلا إذا كان وعاء لها عادة.
The Book of Theft. [1] His saying: The Book of Theft; it is linguistically: taking something from another secretly, and legally: taking valuable property from a guarded place belonging to a non-relative without any ambiguity, secretly, while the owner intends to preserve it during his sleep or absence. And taking something from another while he is present, awake, and intending to preserve it is called 'tarran', and the doer is called 'attarrar', as stated in 'Kulliyat Abi al-Baqa' and others. And it is mentioned in 'al-Qamus' and others that 'al-sariqa' is the verbal noun of 'saraqa yasriqu', from the paradigm of daraba, and it is with fath of the sin and kasr of the ra, and it also occurs with damm of the sin and sukun of the ra, and also 'saraq' with fath of the sin and fath of the ra, and with fath of the sin and kasr of the ra, and with fath of the sin and sukun of the ra. And 'al-sariqa' (with fath of the sin and sukun of the ra, and with damm of the sin and sukun of the ra, and with fath of the sin and kasr of the ra) is a noun. And the habit of the jurists is to discuss the topic of theft under the title of a Book. And it was objected: that the hadd of theft is like other hudud, so why did they not include it in the Book of Hudud? And why did they not title it as a chapter, as they did for the hadd of zina and others? And it was answered: because the discussion of theft also includes liability (daman), they devoted a separate book to it. Then theft is of two types: 1. Minor theft; which is the one understood when theft is mentioned absolutely. 2. Major theft; which is called highway robbery. In both, taking property secretly is considered, but in minor theft from the owner or his representative like the depositary and borrower, and in major theft from the Imam who is obligated to protect the rights of Muslims and their lands, as stated in 'al-Fath'. Secretly. [2] And its subject matter: property, guarded, owned. [1] His saying: 'secretly'; with damm of the kha and kasr of it; meaning: with the intention of concealment from the eye of the one stolen from; so if he took it by overpowering or plunder, it is not theft. [2] His saying: 'property, guarded... etc.'; with the restriction of property, taking what is not property is excluded, so it is not theft. 'Al-hirz' is in the passive form from 'al-ihraz', meaning the preserved thing which the owner intends to preserve from others. With it, he excludes taking what is not guarded. And with the restriction of 'owned', he excludes taking what is not owned by anyone, like taking fruits of desert trees. And the comprehensive statement regarding the definition of theft for which amputation is obligatory is what al-Ghazzi mentioned in 'Tanwir al-Absar': 'It is the taking by a legally responsible, speaking, seeing person of ten good dirhams or their equivalent, intended, manifest in removal, secretly, from a person with a valid hand, of something that does not quickly spoil, in the abode of justice, from a guarded place without ambiguity or interpretation.' And with the restriction of 'legally responsible', he excludes the taking by one who is not legally responsible, like a child or a madman, so amputation is not required for them, since the basis of all obligations is being legally responsible. And with the restriction of 'speaking', he excludes the mute, so amputation is not required for the mute, due to the possibility of his speech having an ambiguity. And with the restriction of 'seeing', he excludes the blind, and amputation is not required because of his ignorance of another's property. And in 'al-Bahr', another restriction is added: that he be possessor of a left hand and right foot. And with the restriction of 'ten dirhams', he excludes theft of less than that, so amputation is not required for theft below the nisab, which with us is the amount of ten dirhams. And with the restriction of 'good dirhams', he excludes theft of counterfeit, substandard, or alloyed dirhams, so amputation is not required. And with the restriction of 'intended', if someone steals a garment worth less than ten dirhams but it contains a dinar or sewn-in dirhams, amputation is not required, unless it is ordinarily a container for them.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the Book of Theft. The author first gives linguistic and legal definitions. Linguistically, theft is taking something secretly. Legally, it is taking valuable property from a guarded place belonging to a non-relative, secretly, with no ambiguity, while the owner intends to preserve it. The definition distinguishes theft from 'tarr' which is taking in the owner's presence. The author explains the morphology of the word 'sariqa'. He then addresses why the topic of theft is a separate book rather than a chapter in the Book of Hudud, answering that theft also involves liability (daman) so it is treated separately. Theft is divided into minor theft (ordinary) and major theft (highway robbery). Both require secrecy, but minor theft is from an owner or representative, while major theft is from the Imam's custody. The commentary then explains each word of the definition: 'secretly' means concealment of the act; taking by force or plunder is not theft. 'Property' excludes non-property. 'Guarded' (hirz) means a place the owner preserves. 'Owned' excludes ownerless property like desert fruits. The author then quotes al-Ghazzi's comprehensive definition for amputation, which adds conditions: the thief must be legally responsible (mukallaf) - thus children and insane are not cut; speaking - the mute is not cut due to possible ambiguity; seeing - the blind is not cut due to ignorance; the stolen item must be ten good dirhams or equivalent (nisab); it must be intended; removal must be manifest; from a person with valid hand; property that does not spoil; in the abode of justice; from a guarded place without ambiguity. Additional conditions from al-Bahr include the thief having a left hand and right foot (since amputation is of right hand and left foot). The passage clarifies that counterfeit substandard dirhams do not incur amputation, nor does theft of a garment containing money unless it is the usual container. This is a thorough Hanafi treatment of the definition and conditions for the hadd of theft.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqa): Taking property secretly from a guarded place belonging to a non-relative without ambiguity.
حرز (hirz): A place where property is preserved by the owner, such that taking from it is considered theft.
نصاب (nisab): Minimum value of stolen property for amputation: ten dirhams or equivalent.
طرار (tarrar): One who takes property from a person who is present and awake (pickpocket).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a condition for the hadd of amputation in theft?
A) The thief must be a legally responsible adult (mukallaf).
B) The stolen property must be worth at least ten dirhams.
C) The theft must occur at night.
D) The property must be taken from a guarded place (hirz).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines theft narrowly for hadd purposes: only secret taking of valuable, guarded, owned property from a valid owner, by a legally responsible person, without ambiguity, meets the strict conditions for amputation.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Definition of Theft and Conditions for Hadd
Defines sariqa and lists conditions for hadd punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sariqa and list all conditions required for the hadd punishment, identifying which conditions exclude different scenarios.

Core Idea
Theft warranting amputation requires ten conditions — missing any one prevents the hadd.

Theft in Islamic law is not simply taking another's property without permission. The Hanafi school defines sariqa with precise conditions that must all be present for the fixed punishment (hadd) of amputation to apply. This lesson unpacks the definition from the classical texts and explains each condition's legal purpose.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core definition
Understand the three-part requirement: property that is guarded (muhraz), owned (mamluk), and taken secretly (khafiyyah). Note exclusions: force or plunder is not theft.
The three words 'mal muhraz mamluk khafiyyah' are the foundation.
2
Work through al-Ghazzi's comprehensive definition
Identify all conditions from the definition in Tanwir al-Absar: mukallaf, natiq, basir, nisab, jiyad, maqsudah, zahirat al-ikhraj, khafiyyah, sahib yad sahihah, la yatasara' ilayhi al-fasad, dar al-adl, hirz, la shubhata.
Each condition excludes a specific type of taking.
3
Examine each condition's rationale
For each condition, understand which scenario it excludes (e.g., child, blind, forced taking, etc.). The text provides explicit reasoning.
Focus on the phrases 'ikhtara bi qayd...'
4
Distinguish condition (shart) from cause (sabab)
The passage argues that the property's attributes are conditions (shurut) not causes (asbab). Understand the difference: a condition is external and needed, but does not lead to the act; a cause leads to the act.
This is usul al-fiqh reasoning applied to the definition.

[٢] ومحلها : مال محرز مملوك خفية.

[١] قوله : خفية ؛ - بضم الخاء المعجمة، وكسرها - ، يعني : بقصد الاختفاء عن عين المسروق منه ، فلو أخذه مغالبة أو نهباً فليس بسرقة.

[١] قوله : وهو شرط ؛ أي كون المسروق مالاً محرزاً مملوكاً شرط لفعل السرقة حيث لا تحقق السرقة شرعاً إلا به. [٢] قوله : فإنّ ... الخ ؛ تعليل لكونه شرطاً، وظاهره : أن نفس المحل شرط ، أو يقال : كون المحلّ موصوفاً بتلك الصفات شرط كما أشرنا إليه. وحاصله : أن محل الفعل يكون خارجاً عن الفعل، ويكون الفعل محتاجاً إليه، فيكون شرطاً له.
فإن قلت : نفس كون الشيء خارجاً محتاجاً إليه لا يكفي لكونه شرطاً ؛ فإنّ هذا القدر يوجد في السبب أيضاً.

[2] And its locus: property that is guarded, owned, and taken secretly. [1] His statement: 'secretly' – with damm of the letter kha and kasra – meaning: with the intention of hiding from the eyesight of the one from whom it is taken. If he takes it by force or as plunder, it is not theft. [2] His statement: 'guarded property' etc.: By the restriction of 'property' is excluded taking what is not property, so it is not theft. Al-hirz is in the passive form of ihraz, meaning the guarded item that the owner intends to preserve from others. By it is guarded against taking what is not guarded. By the restriction of 'owned' is guarded against taking what is not owned by anyone, like taking fruits of desert trees. The comprehensive statement on the definition of theft that necessitates amputation is what al-Ghazzi mentioned in Tanwir al-Absar: 'It is the taking by a legally responsible, speaking, seeing person of ten good dirhams or their equivalent, intended, manifest in removal, secretly, from an owner with valid possession, of something that does not quickly spoil, in the abode of justice, from a guarded place without any doubt or interpretation.' By the restriction of 'legally responsible' is guarded against the taking of the non-responsible like the child and the insane, so there is no amputation on them; by the restriction of 'speaking' against the mute, so the mute is not amputated due to the possibility of his speech having a doubt; by the restriction of 'seeing' against the blind, and he is not amputated because of his ignorance of another's property. (2) In al-Bahr he added another restriction: that he be with a left hand and right foot. By the restriction of 'ten dirhams' [is guarded] against theft of less than that, so no amputation for theft below the nisab, which according to us is the amount of ten dirhams. By the restriction of 'good' against theft of counterfeit, defective, or copper coins, so no amputation. By the restriction of 'intended' regarding one who steals a garment worth less than ten dirhams but containing a dinar or wrapped dirhams, there is no amputation unless it is a customary container for them. [2] And it is a condition. For the locus of the act is a condition for the act because it is external to it and needed by it. By the restriction of 'manifest in removal' is guarded against one who swallows a dinar in the guarded place and exits; he is not amputated. By the restriction of 'secretly' is guarded against taking by raid and force. By the restriction of 'the one from whom it is taken being an owner with valid possession' is guarded against theft from the thief, for there is no amputation. By his statement 'something that does not quickly spoil' is guarded against theft of what spoils in a short time, for there is no amputation. By his statement 'in the abode of justice' is guarded against theft in the abode of rebellion or the abode of war. By his statement 'from a guarded place' is guarded against theft from an unguarded place. By his statement 'without any doubt or interpretation' is guarded against theft with a doubt or interpretation. This is the summary of what the author of al-Bahr, al-Fath, al-Bada'i' and others mentioned, and also added the condition that the property be valuable absolutely, i.e., according to every religion, so no amputation for theft of a Muslim's wine. [1] His statement: 'and it is a condition' i.e., that the stolen property be guarded, owned property is a condition for the act of theft, because theft is not realized legally except with it. [2] His statement: 'for...' etc. is an explanation for it being a condition. Its apparent meaning is that the locus itself is a condition, or it is said: the locus being described with those attributes is a condition as we indicated. The gist is: the locus of the act is external to the act, and the act is in need of it, so it is a condition for it. If you say: the mere fact that something is external and needed is not sufficient for it to be a condition, for this quality is also found in the cause (sabab). I say: the cause leads to the act and has an effect in bringing it about generally, while the condition does not lead to it, and it is known that the locus of the act does not lead to it, so it is only a condition.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and condition analysis for the crime of theft (sariqa) that warrants the hadd punishment (amputation of the hand). The author begins by explaining the phrase 'mal muhraz mamluk khafiyyah' (property that is guarded, owned, and taken secretly). 

First, 'khafiyyah' (secretly) means the taking is done with the intention of concealing from the owner's sight. If one takes openly by force (mugalabah) or as plunder, it is not considered theft. 

Second, 'mal' (property) excludes taking things that are not considered property, such as free things. 'Muhraz' (guarded) means the item is kept in a place that the owner intends to protect from others. 'Mamluk' (owned) excludes taking things that are not owned by anyone, like fruits from wild desert trees — such taking is not theft.

The passage then cites the comprehensive definition from al-Ghazzi's Tanwir al-Absar, which lists all conditions for amputation: the taker must be a legally responsible (mukallaf) adult, speaking (natiq), seeing (basir), and the stolen item must be ten good dirhams or their equivalent, intended (maqsudah), manifest in removal (zahirat al-ikhraj), taken secretly (khafiyyah), from an owner with valid possession (sahib yad sahihah), from something that does not quickly spoil (la yatasara' ilayhi al-fasad), in the abode of justice (dar al-adl), from a guarded place (hirz), and without any doubt or interpretation (la shubhata fihi wa la ta'wil). Each of these conditions has a purpose: 
- Mukallaf excludes minors and the insane. 
- Natiq excludes the mute because he may have a doubt that prevents his speech. 
- Basir excludes the blind because he is unaware of others' property. 
- The nisab of ten dirhams excludes smaller amounts. 
- 'Jiyad' (good) excludes counterfeit, defective, or base coins. 
- 'Maqsudah' excludes cases where a container has less value than ten dirhams but contains a valuable item not intended to be stolen; but if the container is customarily used for such items, then it is theft. 
- 'Zahirat al-ikhraj' excludes swallowing a dinar inside the guarded place and then exiting — since the removal is not manifest. 
- 'Khafiyyah' excludes open raids. 
- 'Sahib yad sahihah' excludes theft from a thief (the stolen property is not in valid ownership). 
- 'La yatasara' ilayhi al-fasad' excludes items that quickly spoil. 
- 'Dar al-adl' excludes theft in an unjust or war zone where the hadd is not applied. 
- 'Hirz' excludes unguarded property. 
- 'La shubhata' excludes cases where there is a legal doubt (e.g., a father taking from his child's property). 

Finally, the passage discusses the distinction between a condition (shart) and a cause (sabab). The author clarifies that the locus (mahall) of the act — the property being guarded, owned, etc. — is a condition for the act of theft, not a cause. A cause leads to the act and has an effect, while a condition is external but necessary for the act to be valid. Thus, the property's attributes are conditions for the theft to be punishable by amputation.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqa): Theft that warrants the hadd punishment; defined as taking property that is guarded, owned, and taken secretly, with all the listed conditions.
حرز (hirz): A place where property is guarded and the owner intends to keep it safe from others.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of stolen goods (ten dirhams or equivalent) that triggers the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, which of the following would qualify as sariqa requiring amputation?
A) A blind person takes a guarded wallet containing five dirhams.
B) A sane adult secretly takes a guarded item worth ten good dirhams from its owner in an Islamic state.
C) A child takes a guarded gold dinar from a store.
D) A person openly raids a house and takes ten dirhams by force.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hadd for theft applies only when thirteen precise conditions are fulfilled; missing any one reduces the punishment to tazir.
